2 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chavez-Barragan


Supreme Court Case No. 16SA133


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 66; 379 P.3d 330; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 979



September 26, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. Morgan County District Court Case No. 14CR245. Honorable Douglas R. Vannoy, Judge.
People v. Chavez-Barragan, 365 P.3d 981, 2016 Colo. LEXIS 193, 2016 CO 16 (2016)



DISPOSITION: Order Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Evidence of the methamphetamine found in defendant's truck should not have been suppressed because the investigatory detention following the traffic stop was reasonable; the deputy proceeded with reasonable diligence, defendant's move to the patrol car did not render the stop unreasonable, and the overall length of the detention was not inherently unreasonable; [2]-Because the traffic stop was reasonable as the search was authorized by defendant's voluntary consent, no prior illegality tainted his statements and the trial court erred in suppressing them.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

3 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Zuniga


Supreme Court Case No. 16SA92


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 52; 372 P.3d 1052; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 675



June 27, 2016, Decided


NOTICE:

THIS OPINION IS NOT THE FINAL VERSION AND SUBJECT TO REVISION UPON FINAL PUBLICATION


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. Weld County District Court Case No. 15CR1408. Honorable Thomas Quammen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The odor of marijuana could properly be considered as part of the totality of the circumstances test for probable cause and, thus, the officer had probable cause to search the vehicle due to the fact that defendant and the driver gave remarkably disparate accounts of their visit to Colorado, defendant and the driver exhibited extreme nervousness, the officer detected a "heavy odor" of raw marijuana, and a drug-sniffing dog suggested that illegal drugs were present in the vehicle; hence, the trial court erred in granting defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

4 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Roberson


Supreme Court Case No. 13SA268


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 36; 377 P.3d 1039; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 459



May 16, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Roberson, 2016 Colo. LEXIS 598, 2016 CO 36 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 [***1] . Douglas County District Court Case No. 08CR109. Honorable Richard B. Caschette, Judge.
People v. Roberson, 2013 Colo. App. LEXIS 2035 (Colo. Ct. App., Dec. 26, 2013)



DISPOSITION: Rule Made Absolute.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's privilege against self-incrimination precluded the district court from revoking his probation based on his refusal to answer the polygraph examiner's question regarding his use or viewing of child pornography while he was on probation; threatening him with the revocation of his probation based on the proper invocation of his rights under U.S. Const. amend. V amounted to compulsion; [2]-It could not be determined whether the district court correctly found that defendant's privilege against self-incrimination precluded it from revoking his probation based on his refusal to answer the polygraph examiner's questions regarding any post-trial sexual fantasies involving minors that he might have had.


OUTCOME: Case remanded.

 

5 of 734 DOCUMENTS


In re Colo. Ethics Watch v. Indep. Ethics Comm'n


Supreme Court Case No. 15SA244


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 21; 369 P.3d 270; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 420



April 25, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 [***1] . District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 15CV31862. Honorable A. Bruce Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Rule Made Absolute.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Although the General Assembly may enact legislation relating to the Independent Ethics Commission's (IEC's) enforcement decisions under Colo. Const. art. XXIX, § 5(3)(c), it may not encroach upon IEC's decisions not to enforce; therefore, IEC's decision to dismiss a complaint as frivolous under Colo. Const. art. XXIX, § 5(3)(b) is not subject to judicial review under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-18.5-101(9) (2015).


OUTCOME: Rule to show cause made absolute remanded.

 

6 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Dean v. People


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC585


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 14; 366 P.3d 593; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 195



February 29, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA1747.
People v. Dean, 292 P.3d 1066, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 998, 2012 COA 106 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where defendant was convicted of second degree murder, the trial court sentenced him under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-801(2) as a habitual offender and he was required to serve 72 years before he was eligible for parole; [2]-The Colorado Supreme Court held that the interplay of the habitual criminal statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-801(2), and the parole eligibility statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 17-22.5-102.5, did not violate defendant's right to equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1; [3]-The habitual criminal sentencing and parole eligibility scheme is rationally related to the legislative purposes of public safety and punishment of recidivist offenders.


OUTCOME: Granted, affirmed, and remanded.

 

7 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graves


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC1005


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2016 CO 15; 368 P.3d 317; 2016 Colo. LEXIS 196



February 29, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the District Court. Adams County District Court Case No. 12CV839.
People v. Graves, 2014 Colo. LEXIS 64 (Colo., Feb. 3, 2014)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As construed to prohibit only overtly sexualized behavior in public and not innocuous public displays of affection, in accordance with the plain meaning of the term "lewd" as connoting lustful or lascivious intent, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-7-301(1)(d) (2015) did not reach any protected speech or expressive activity under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, and therefore was not unconstitutionally overbroad; [2]-Because defendant's alleged conduct of sexually touching another person in view of other patrons in a movie theater, which was a public place as defined in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-901(3)(n) (2015), fell within any reasonable definition of "lewd," such conduct was clearly proscribed and § 18-7-301(1)(d) was not vague as applied to that conduct; defendant could not complain of any hypothetical vagueness of the law as it might be applied to the conduct of others.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

8 of 734 DOCUMENTS


T.W. v. M.C. (In the Interest of Baby A)


Supreme Court Case No. 14SC1045


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 72; 363 P.3d 193; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 1183



December 21, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by T.W. v. M.C. (In the Interest of Baby A), 2016 Colo. LEXIS 13 (Colo., Jan. 11, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by M. C. v. T. W., 2016 U.S. LEXIS 4026 (U.S., June 20, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 13CA2280.
M.C. v. Adoption Choices of Colo., Inc., 369 P.3d 659, 2014 Colo. App. LEXIS 1952, 2014 COA 161 (2014)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court satisfied due process requirements when it terminated a father's parental rights for failing to promptly take substantial responsibility for his children pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-5-105(3.1)(c) because his single child support payment of $250 was not regular child support; [2]-The trial court sufficiently protected the father's fundamental liberty interest in his children because it applied a presumption in favor of preserving parental rights and made findings required by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-5-105 to overcome this presumption by clear and convincing evidence; [3]-Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-5-105 adequately protects parents' due process rights; [4]-The trial court's findings supported the conclusion that remaining in the adoptive home would serve the children's best interests; [5]-The Court of Appeals erred by reversing the trial court's decision.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

9 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Madden


Supreme Court Case No. 13SC496


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 69; 364 P.3d 866; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 1185



December 21, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Madden, 2016 Colo. LEXIS 149 (Colo., Feb. 8, 2016)
Reversed by, Remanded by Nelson v. Colorado, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 2615 (U.S., Apr. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA2081.
People v. Madden, 2013 Colo. App. LEXIS 607, 2013 COA 56 (2013)



DISPOSITION: Order Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Upon the reversal of defendant's conviction for attempting to patronize a prostituted child, the trial court lacked the authority to order a refund of his costs, fees, and restitution because defendant did not seek relief under the Colorado Exoneration Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-65-101 to 13-65-103 (2015); [2]-Colo. R. Civ. P. 60(b) permitted the trial court to hear defendant's motion seeking relief from his vacated conviction, but it did not give the court the authority to draw on public funds; [3]-The Colorado Exoneration Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-65-101 to 13-65-103, specifically identifies a procedure and a source of funding for exonerated defendants seeking refunds of costs, fees, and restitution; [4]-Because the fines were not illegally imposed, Colo. R.Crim. P. 35(a) did not apply or grant the trial court authority to order a refund from public funds.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

10 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nelson


Supreme Court Case No. 13SC495


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 68; 362 P.3d 1070; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 1182



December 21, 2015, Decided


NOTICE:

THIS OPINION IS NOT THE FINAL VERSION AND SUBJECT TO REVISION UPON FINAL PUBLICATION


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Nelson, 2016 Colo. LEXIS 151 (Colo., Feb. 8, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Nelson v. Colorado, 137 S. Ct. 30, 195 L. Ed. 2d 902, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 4465 (U.S., 2016)
Reversed by, Remanded by Nelson v. Colorado, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 2615 (U.S., Apr. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 11CA1206.
People v. Nelson, 369 P.3d 625, 2013 Colo. App. LEXIS 610, 2013 COA 58 (2013)



DISPOSITION: Order Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where defendant's conviction for five sexual assault charges was reversed, the trial court did not have the authority to grant her a refund of costs, fees, and restitution because she did not seek a refund through the process created by the Colorado Exoneration Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-65-101 to 13-65-103; [2]-A monetary sanction payable from public funds is beyond the authority of the trial court; [3]-The trial court correctly ordered--and the Colorado Department of Corrections correctly withheld--money from defendant based on her initial conviction; [4]-Defendant was not deprived of due process under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV § 1, because the money was not wrongfully withheld as she was initially convicted after a jury trial; [5]-The Colorado Exoneration Act provides sufficient process for defendants to seek a refund of costs, fees, and restitution.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

11 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Siewiyumptewa v. State (In re Dwyer)


Supreme Court Case No. 15SA22


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 58; 357 P.3d 185; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 916



September 21, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 [***1] . District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 14CV32543. Honorable Herbert L. Stern, III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Rule Made Absolute.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because the question presented by an appeal implicated the apportionment of nearly one billion dollars of state funding, it was an issue of first impression and significant public importance so that the court elected to hear the appeal under Colo. App. R. 21; [2]-Colo. Const. art. IX, § 17(2)(a) (Amendment 23) required regular increases to one of the variables within the formula for funding public eduction in the Public School Finance Act of 1994, Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 22-54-101 to -137, C.R.S. (2015): statewide base per-pupil funding; [3]-Because Amendment 23 only required increases to statewide base per-pupil funding, not total per-pupil funding, the General Assembly could permissibly reduce factor funding by implementing the negative factor, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 22-54-104(5)(g) (2011).


OUTCOME: Rule made absolute; case remanded with instructions to dismiss the parents' complaint.

 

12 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Taxpayers for Pub. Educ. v. Douglas County Sch. Dist.


Supreme Court Case No. 13SC233


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 50; 351 P.3d 461; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 589



June 29, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by Colo. State Bd. of Educ. v. Taxpayers for Pub. Educ., 2017 U.S. LEXIS 4282 (U.S., June 27, 2017)
Vacated by, Remanded by Doyle v. Taxpayers for Pub. Educ., 2017 U.S. LEXIS 4283 (U.S., June 27, 2017)
Vacated by, Remanded by Douglas County Sch. Dist. v. Taxpayers for Pub. Educ., 2017 U.S. LEXIS 4284 (U.S., June 27, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case Nos. 11CA1856 & 11CA1857.
Taxpayers for Pub. Educ. v. Douglas County Sch. Dist., 356 P.3d 833, 2013 Colo. App. LEXIS 266, 2013 COA 20 (Feb. 28, 2013)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Petitioners lacked standing to challenge the scholarship program under the Colorado Public School Finance Act of 1994 because nothing in the Act suggested that the Colorado General Assembly intended to allow private parties to redress violations of the Act in court; the Act, in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 22-54-120(1) (2014), manifested the General Assembly's intent that the Colorado State Board of Education, not private citizens, be responsible for ensuring lawful implementation; [2]-The program created financial partnerships between the school district and religious schools and, in so doing, facilitated students attending such schools, thus constituting aid to religious institutions as contemplated by Colo. Const. art. IX, § 7; because Colo. Const. art. IX, § 7 prohibited school districts from aiding religious schools, the program was in violation of the Colorado Constitution.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

 

13 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Frees v. Tidd (In re Water Rights of Tidd)


Supreme Court Case No. 14SA234


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 39; 349 P.3d 259; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 462



June 1, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court. Alamosa County District Court, Water Division 3, Case No. 10CW31. Honorable Pattie P. Swift, Water Judge.



DISPOSITION: June 1, 2015.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The water court did not err in decreeing the non-consumptive, non-injurious hydropower conditional water right that defendants sought because it was legally separate and distinct from plaintiffs' irrigation water right; the fact that the same physical water owned by the public and diverted from the creek would be put to an additional use under a separate water right before reaching plaintiffs' place of beneficial use in no way deprived the water court of authority to issue the conditional water right decree; [2]-In granting defendants' non-consumptive conditional water right application, the water court followed Colorado law allowing the public's scarce water resource to be put to multiple beneficial uses while protecting decreed senior water rights.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

People v. Tate


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC932, Supreme Court Case No. 12SC1022, Supreme Court Case No. 13SC211


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 42; 352 P.3d 959; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 466



June 1, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Tate, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 644, 2015 CO 42 (2015)
Rehearing denied by Tenarro Banks v. People, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 645 (Colo., July 13, 2015)
Rehearing denied by Jensen v. People, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 646 (Colo., July 13, 2015)
Rehearing denied by Jensen v. People, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 711 (Colo., Aug. 3, 2015)
Rehearing denied by Banks v. People, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 733, 2015 CO 42 (2015)
Rehearing denied by People v. Tate, 2015 Colo. LEXIS 734, 2015 CO 42 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA2467. Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 08CA105. C.A.R. 50 [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 05CA0864, Douglas County District Court Case No. 98CR422. Honorable Richard Caschette, Judge.
People v. Tate, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1492 (Colo. Ct. App., Sept. 13, 2012)
People v. Jensen, 55 P.3d 135, 2001 Colo. App. LEXIS 1186 (Colo. Ct. App., 2001)
People v. Tenarro Banks, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1566, 2012 COA 157 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed in Part and Affirmed in Part. Judgment Reversed in Part and Affirmed in Part. Order Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-With regard to two of the juveniles, a hearing on whether a life in prison without the possibility of parole (LWOP) sentence was appropriate considering their youth and attendant characteristics was the remedy that preserved as much of the Colorado Legislature's work as possible given the United States Supreme Court's holding in Miller; hence, it was necessary to remand their cases for such a determination; [2]-The third juvenile was properly denied postconviction relief because the Miller rule did not apply retroactively to cases on collateral review of a final judgment.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.

 

15 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Coffman v. Williamson


Supreme Court Case No. 14SA249


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 35; 348 P.3d 929; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 450



May 26, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 11CV7027. Honorable Michael A. Martinez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A company's alleged debt-management services to attorneys did not fall within the scope of the legal services exemption in the original Debt-Management Services Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 12-14.5-202(10)(A)(i) because the company performed substantive debt-management services without meaningful supervision by an attorney; [2]-The legal services exemption did not violate separation of powers under Colo. Const. art. III because DMSA did not regulate out-of-state attorneys who fell within the parameters of Colo. R. Civ. P. 205.1, and there was no conflict between DMSA and the regulation of attorneys; [3]-Because the amended DMSA did not benefit in-state economic interests by burdening out-of-state competitors or otherwise favor residents over nonresidents, it did not violate the Commerce Clause or the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; case remanded.

 

16 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Cantina Grill, JV v. City & Cnty. of Denver Cnty. Bd. of Equalization


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC819


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2015 CO 15; 344 P.3d 870; 2015 Colo. LEXIS 198



March 16, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 11CA2270.
Cantina Grill v. City & County of Denver County Bd. of Equalization, 292 P.3d 1144, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1490, 2012 COA 154 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Food and beverage concessionaires at a city-owned airport held taxable possessory interests under Colo. Const. art. X, §§ 3(1)(a), 4, 6, 9, 10; Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-1-102(16) because the concessionaires' interests were sufficiently exclusive, in that they had the right to exclude others from using their respective spaces to operate a concession business; the totality of the circumstances reflected that their revenue-generating capability was independent of the city, and the valuation of their interests was consistent with the possessory interest valuation scheme; [2]-The concessionaires' interests were properly valued under the possessory interest valuation provisions, based on their reasonably estimated future annual rents; [3]-Some of the payment was not excludable either as payment for the right to conduct a business or reimbursement to the city for operating expenses.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

17 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hickenlooper v. Freedom from Religion Found., Inc.


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC442


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 77; 338 P.3d 1002; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 1012



November 24, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 10CA2559.
Freedom from Religion Found., Inc. v. Hickenlooper, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 741, 2012 COA 81 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Respondents nonbelievers had no taxpayer standing, under Colo. Const. art. III, to sue petitioner governor for issuing proclamations recognizing a day of prayer because the use of public funds to cover incidental overhead costs was not, alone, a sufficient injury; [2]-The nonbelievers had no individual standing to sue the governor because they did not allege they were coerced to participate in the day of prayer, nor that they suffered any negative consequences from the government due to nonparticipation, nor that the government prevented them from exercising their right to nonbelief, so they identified no personal injury from the alleged constitutional error, under Colo. Const. art. II, § 4, except psychological consequences presumably produced by observing conduct with which they disagreed, which was too indirect and incidental an injury to confer individual standing.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

18 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Justus v. State


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC906


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 75; 336 P.3d 202; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 944



October 20, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 11CA1507.
Justus v. State, 337 P.3d 1219, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1652, 2012 COA 169 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether plaintiffs Colorado Public Employees' Retirement Association (PERA) members had contractual rights to cost-of-living adjustment (COLA) formulas in place when they retired for life without change. HOLDINGS: [1]-The members had no such rights because PERA legislation providing COLAs established no such contract between PERA and its members, so legislation altering the COLA formula did not violate the contract clauses in U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1 and Colo. Const. art. II, § 11, as the legislature stated no clear intent to be contractually bound, while it used such language to describe the underlying pension benefit, and mandatory language in Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 24-51-1001(1) and 24-51-1002(2) (2014) was directed at the PERA administrator, not the legislature, so retirees could not reasonably expect the state's provision of a given COLA was a statutory contract.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

19 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carbajal


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC235


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 60; 328 P.3d 104; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 517



June 30, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 10CA2252.
People v. Carbajal, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 303, 2012 COA 34 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court did not err by instructing the jury that "defendant possessed a firearm for the purpose of defending himself, home, or property from what he reasonably believed to be a threat of imminent harm," and therefore the appellate court erred by reversing defendant's convictions of Possession of a Weapon by a Previous Offender (POWPO) under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-12-108(1), (2)(a) (2013), because under Blue and Ford, the POWPO affirmative defense was the statutory defense of choice of evils of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-702 (1973).


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

20 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Francen v. Colo. Dep't of Revenue


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC610


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 54; 328 P.3d 111; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 524



June 30, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at Petitioner: Hanson v. Respondent: Colo. Dep't of Revenue, 328 P.3d 122, 2014 Colo. LEXIS 525, 2014 CO 55 (Colo., June 30, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 10CA2382.
Francen v. Colo. Dep't of Revenue, Div. of Motor Vehicles, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1081, 2012 COA 110 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The appellate court did not err by concluding that the legality of the initial contact between the police and the driver was not relevant to in the civil administrative proceeding to revoke his driver's license because the court held that, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-2-126 (2011), "probable cause" referred to the quantum and quality of evidence necessary for a law enforcement officer to issue a notice of driver's license revocation, not whether the officer's initial contact with the driver was lawful; [2]-The exclusionary rule did not apply to suppress evidence of the driver's blood alcohol content in the driver's license revocation proceeding because the proceeding was not quasi-criminal in nature.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

21 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hayes v. Spalding (In re Title, Ballot Title, & Submission Clause for 2013-2014 #76)


Supreme Court Case No. 14SA105


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 52; 333 P.3d 76; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 513



June 23, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding Pursuant to § 1-40-107(2), C.R.S. [***1] (2013). Appeal from the Ballot Title Setting Board.



DISPOSITION: Title Board Action Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a proposed ballot initiative on the recall of public officials violated the single subject rule of Colo. Const. art. V, § 1(5.5). HOLDINGS: [1]-The proposed initiative violated the single subject rule because it proposed to comprehensively revamp the provisions of Colo. Const. art. XXI, §§ 1 and 4, on the recall of state and local officials, constituting one purpose, and create a new constitutional right to recall non-elected officials, constituting a second purpose that was distinct from the first and not dependent on or necessarily and properly connected with it.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed.

 

22 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Gessler v. Colo. Common Cause


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC783


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2014 CO 44; 327 P.3d 232; 2014 Colo. LEXIS 436



June 16, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 11CA2405.
Colo. Common Cause & Colo. Ethics Watch v. Gessler, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1422, 2012 COA 147 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the promulgation by the Secretary of State of Colorado of Colo. Sec. State R. 4.1, 8 Colo. Code Regs. § 1505-6:4.1 (2013), exceeded his authority. HOLDINGS: [1]-The $200 contribution and expenditure threshold for issue committees under Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 2(10)(a)(II) and the retrospective reporting requirement for issue committees under Colo. Rev. Stat. 1-45-108(1)(a)(I) (2013) of the Colorado Fair Campaign Practices Act were not facially invalidated by the Tenth Circuit's holding in Sampson. Because Rule 4.1 directly conflicted with these still-valid provisions, Rule 4.1 was unlawful and was to be set aside pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-4-106(7) (2013).


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

People ex rel. O.C.


Supreme Court Case No. 12SC835


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2013 CO 56; 308 P.3d 1218; 2013 Colo. LEXIS 659



September 9, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 12CA649.
People ex rel. O.C., 312 P.3d 226, 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1569 (2012)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred when it interpreted Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-3-507(5)(a) (2013) as requiring the grandparents to have had the child in their care for more than three months before being able to intervene as a matter of right and thus, their motion to intervene should have been granted. Section 19-3-507(5)(a)'s three-month time requirement did not apply to parents, grandparents, or relatives. Instead, it permitted parents, grandparents, and relatives to intervene as a matter of right without regard to whether the child had previously been in their care. That reading conformed with the legislative purpose and scheme of the Children's Code, was supported by the legislative history of the provision, and avoided a potentially unconstitutional alternative.


OUTCOME: Appellate court's judgment affirmed.

 

32 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Qwest Corp. v. Colo. Div. of Prop. Taxation


Supreme Court Case No. 11SC669


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2013 CO 39; 304 P.3d 217; 2013 Colo. LEXIS 446



June 24, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Qwest Corp. v. Colo. Div. of Prop. Tax, 2013 Colo. LEXIS 513 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case Number 10CA1320.
Qwest Corp. v. Colo. Div. of Prop. Taxation, 310 P.3d 113, 2011 Colo. App. LEXIS 1296 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 4, 2011)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility sought judicial review of respondent Colorado Division of Property Taxation, Department of Local Affairs' (DPT), refusal to apply the intangible property exemption in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-3-118 (2012) and the cost cap valuation method in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-1-103(13) (2012) to its property. The trial court and the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed the decision. The utility sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The utility sought assessment in the same way as its cable company competitors. The supreme court held the intangible property exemption did not apply because (1) it was not found in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-4-102 (2012), and (2) Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-4-102(1)(b) (2012) required the DPT administrator to consider the utility's intangible property. The cost cap valuation method did not apply because (1) it capped a locally assessed entity's value, and (2) the utility's value was determined under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-4-102 (2012). Equal protection was not violated because (1) the statutory scheme was presumed constitutional as no fundamental right or suspect classification was involved, (2) a failure to receive cable companies' tax benefits did not negate every conceivable basis for the scheme, and (3) this was consistent with rational policy choices. Colorado's uniform taxation clause in Colo. Const. art. X, § 3(1)(a) allowed the scheme because (1) it required uniform assessment in the same territory, and (2) the utility was assessed state-wide, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-4-102 (2012), while the cable companies were assessed locally, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-1-103(5)(a) (2012).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

 

33 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lobato v. State


Supreme Court Case No. 12SA25


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2013 CO 30; 304 P.3d 1132; 2013 Colo. LEXIS 383



May 28, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 05CV4794. Honorable Sheila A. Rappaport, Judge.
Lobato v. People, 218 P.3d 358, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 998 (Colo., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Governmental defendants appealed a judgment of the District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, which declared the state's public school financing system unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the state's public school financing system satisfied the rational basis test articulated in Lobato v. State and complied with the Education Clause of the Colorado Constitution, Colo. Const. art. IX, § 2. The system was rationally related to the constitutional mandate of a thorough and uniform system of public education. Plaintiffs did not establish beyond a reasonable doubt that the system failed to pass constitutional muster under the rational basis test. The financing system was also constitutional under the Local Control Clause of the Colorado Constitution, Colo. Const. art. IX, § 15. The system afforded local school districts control over locally-raised funds and therefore over "instruction in the public schools." The local control built into the financing system provided each school district with the opportunity for experimentation and innovation in using limited resources to achieve educational excellence. The trial court reversibly erred when it declared the public school financing system unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Perez v. People


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC616


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2013 CO 22; 302 P.3d 222; 2013 Colo. LEXIS 277



April 8, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Perez v. Colorado, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 6169 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 08CA2296.
Rizo v. People, 302 P.3d 232, 2013 Colo. LEXIS 275 (2013)
People v. Perez, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 1114 (Colo. Ct. App., July 29, 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals found that the practice of referring to prospective jurors by number did not invalidate defendant's conviction. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that defendant did not have an anonymous jury. He received all identifying information, including the names, of the voir dire panel. The supreme court concluded that the trial court's practice of using numbers, instead of names, to refer to prospective jurors did not undermine the presumption of innocence. The court presented the issue of referencing jurors by number as its general practice. This general practice did not suggest anything in particular with regard to this defendant. Because there was no suggestion that information was being kept from defendant, no inference could be drawn that there was any need to withhold jurors' information from him. Finally, the trial court instructed the jury on the presumption of innocence prior to deliberations.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

35 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Webb v. City of Black Hawk


Supreme Court Case No. 11SC536


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2013 CO 9; 295 P.3d 480; 2013 Colo. LEXIS 99



February 4, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the District Court. Gilpin County District Court Case No. 11CV17. Honorable Jack W. Berryhill, Judge.
Webb v. City of Black Hawk, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 944 (Colo., Dec. 12, 2011)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Gilpin County District Court (Colorado) affirmed the municipal court convictions of petitioners bicyclists for violating Black Hawk, Colo., Municipal Code § 8-111, banning bicycles on certain city streets. The bicyclists appealed, contesting the ordinance's legality.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance barred bicycles from some city streets. The supreme court held the ordinance was a matter of mixed state and local concern because (1) the record showed the ordinance affected the expectations of residents outside respondent city and created a "ripple effect" impacting other communities, (2) the state traditionally regulated bicycle transportation by giving bicyclists the same rights and responsibilities as other vehicle operators, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-4-1412(1) (2012), (3) the Colorado Constitution did not specifically commit the regulation of bicycles to the city or the state, and (4) both the city and the state had important interests in the matter, as the state had an interest in the consistent application of statewide laws to avoid patchwork bicycle regulations, while the city had an interest, under Colo. Const. art. XX, § 6, in controlling traffic on its streets, as authorized by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-4-111(h) (2012). The ordinance conflicted with and was preempted by state law because it conflicted with Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-4-109(11) (2012), letting cities ban bicycles only if suitable bike paths were established, since the city provided no bike paths.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Kazadi v. People


Supreme Court Case No. 11SC264


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 73; 291 P.3d 16; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 951

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA2640.
People v. Kazadi, 284 P.3d 70, 2011 Colo. App. LEXIS 325 (Colo. Ct. App., 2011)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner moved under Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) for postconviction review of his felony plea leading to a deferred judgment under Colo. Rev. Stat. § § 18-1.3-102, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel for counsel's failure to notify him of possible deportation consequences for pleading guilty to obtain a deferred judgment and sentence. The district court denied the motion and the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that petitioner could not seek postconviction review of his deferred judgment and sentence under Rule 35(c) and Colo. Rev. The court also held that petitioner could, alternatively, seek to withdraw his guilty plea for a "fair and just reason" under Colo. R. Crim. P. 32(d) because, in the unique situation of the deferred judgment, the defendant's case was continued and there was no imposition of sentence and entry of judgment while the deferred judgment was in effect.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the ruling of the court of appeals.

 

37 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Gen. Steel Domestic Sales, LLC v. Bacheller


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC403


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 68M; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 953; 34 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1429



December 20, 2012, Decided


NOTICE:

THIS OPINION IS NOT THE FINAL VERSION AND SUBJECT TO REVISION UPON FINAL PUBLICATION


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA1383.
Gen. Steel Domestic Sales, LLC v. Bacheller, 291 P.3d 1, 2012 Colo. LEXIS 903 (2012)
Bacheller v. Gen. Steel Domestic Sales, LLC, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 571 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 22, 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by refusing to include additional elements reflecting a prior case's (POME) heightened standard in the jury instruction for respondent employee's malicious prosecution claims against petitioner companies. The court of appeals held the trial court did not err by trebling an exemplary damages award against two of the companies. The companies petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The companies argued that, under POME and the federal case law, their filing of an arbitration claim against the employee constituted petitioning the government such that their alleged activity merited First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, protection. The supreme court noted that the companies initiated a private arbitration action pursuant to a binding arbitration clause in an employment contract. As such, they did not petition any branch of government for a redress of grievances. They did not bring suit against a governmental entity in an effort to overturn an administrative decision. Their arbitration action was not instituted in an attempt to vindicate the rights of the public. The companies' alleged petitioning activity did not merit protection under the First Amendment right to petition and POME. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by trebling the exemplary damages award against the companies under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-102(3). The companies made multiple requests that the employee submit to an independent medical examination. There was evidence that the companies petitioned the supreme court for the purpose of delay.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

38 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Gen. Steel Domestic Sales, LLC v. Bacheller


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC403


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 68; 291 P.3d 1; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 903; 162 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,305



November 27, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted as modified at, Substituted opinion at Petitioners: Gen. Steel v. Respondent: Harold Irving Bacheller, 2012 Colo. LEXIS 953 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA1383.
Bacheller v. Gen. Steel Domestic Sales, LLC, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 571 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 22, 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a former employee, sued his former employers for malicious prosecution, abuse of process, and other claims based on the employers filing an arbitration complaint against him. A jury returned a verdict for the employee and awarded punitive damages, and the trial court trebled the punitive damages under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-102(3). The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed, and the employers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee had gone to work for a competitor. The employee's malicious prosecution action was based on an arbitration that the employers had filed against him pursuant to an arbitration clause in the parties' employment agreement. The court held that a heightened standard of protection for petitioning activity protected by U.S. Const. amend. I did not apply in this case because the underlying alleged petitioning activity was the filing of an arbitration complaint in a purely private dispute. The employers did not petition any branch of government for a redress of grievances, and the arbitration was not instituted in an attempt to vindicate the rights of the public. The trial court did not err in denying the employers' request to include additional elements reflecting a heightened standard in the jury instructions. The court upheld the trebling of exemplary damages under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-102(3), given the employers' harassing behavior, such as multiple requests that the employee submit to an independent medical examination, attempts to gain competitive information from the competitor, and the filing of an original action with the court two weeks prior to trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

Neuhaus v. People


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC27


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 65; 289 P.3d 19; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 867



November 19, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA896.
People v. Neuhaus, 240 P.3d 391, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 1897 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court denied defendant's pretrial motion to suppress evidence of weapons and ammunition found during a warrantless search of his car. He entered a guilty plea to possession of a weapon by a previous offender and appealed the suppression issue. The Colorado Court of Appeals held that it had no authority to review the trial court's ruling on the motion to suppress evidence. Defendant sought certiorari review by the state supreme court.


OVERVIEW: In a postconviction appeal, defendant attempted to appeal a pretrial motion to suppress evidence after he entered a guilty plea to possession of a weapon by a previous offender. The Supreme Court of Colorado held that while Fed. R. Crim. P. 11(a)(2) permitted conditional pleas, they were not permitted under Colorado rule or statute. A guilty plea foreclosed appellate review of suppression issues. The court declined to create by judicial decision an exception allowing conditional guilty pleas that reserved the right to appeal a pretrial motion to suppress evidence because a reservation of that right was better created by statute or court rule, if at all.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

40 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Churchill v. Univ. of Colo. at Boulder


Supreme Court Case No. 11SC25


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 54; 285 P.3d 986; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 672; 34 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 647



September 10, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Churchill v. Univ. of Colo. at Boulder, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 2688 (U.S., Apr. 1, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals, Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA1713.
Churchill v. Univ. of Colo. at Boulder, 293 P.3d 16, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 1745 (Colo. Ct. App., 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff professor's termination claim against defendant public university, affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the professor's claim for equitable remedies, and affirmed the trial court's directed verdict in favor of the university on the professor's bad faith investigation claim. Certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that it was not error for the court of appeals to consider the regents' personal immunity defenses that were raised by the university in defense of the professor's claims. The regents' decision to terminate the professor's employment for academic misconduct was a quasi-judicial action functionally comparable to a judicial process. Hence, the regents were entitled to absolute immunity concerning their decision to terminate the professor. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it ruled that the professor, who had engaged in repeated instances of academic dishonesty, was not entitled to the equitable remedies of reinstatement and front pay. The professor's bad faith investigation claim was barred by qualified immunity because the regents' investigation into the professor's academic record did not implicate a clearly established statutory or constitutional right or law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Glustrom v. Colo. Pub. Utils. Comm'n


Supreme Court Case No. 11SA164


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 53; 280 P.3d 662; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 462



June 25, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the District Court. District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 09CV8968. Honorable Herbert L. Stern III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor utility company sought to recover a portion of its construction costs for building a coal-fired electric power unit. Petitioner intervened. Respondent, the Colorado Public Utilities Commission, denied her challenges. The District Court, City and County of Denver, Colorado, affirmed. The state supreme court reviewed the case on appeal.


OVERVIEW: The utility company constructed a coal-fired electric power unit. When it sought to recover a portion of its construction costs in a rate proceeding, petitioner intervened. She sought to introduce testimony that the utility company acted improperly and should not recover its costs. The Supreme Court of Colorado held that the PUC did not abuse its discretion when it struck substantial portions of petitioner's testimony, as it was duplicative and involved subjects properly addressed by expert testimony. The depreciation rate approved by the PUC was established pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 40-4-112 (2011) and in accordance with the evidence. The PUC was free to exercise its discretion in departing from a strict application of the used and useful principle. Petitioner failed to meet her burden of showing why a departure would result in a rate that was unjust and unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Olds v. Berry (In re B.B.O.)


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC623


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 40; 277 P.3d 818; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 390



May 29, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at In re B.B.O., 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 1117 (Colo. Ct. App., July 12, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA1874.
In the Interest of B.B.O., 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 1292 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 19, 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the half-sister of a minor child, petitioned for an allocation of parental responsibilities (APR). The trial court found petitioner had standing and determined that it was in the child's best interest to reside with petitioner. Respondent biological parent appealed. The Colorado Court of Appeals concluded petitioner lacked standing to seek an APR. The state supreme court granted petitioner's request for certiorari review.


OVERVIEW: The minor child was in the physical care petitioner and her father for six years prior to his death. Petitioner continued to care for her until she filed the petition for an allocation of parental responsibilities (APR). The Supreme Court of Colorado held that respondent's consent was not required in order for petitioner to establish standing to petition for an APR. Because the child was in the physical care of petitioner and not in the physical care of either parent at the time the petition was filed, petitioner had standing to petition for an APR under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 14-10-123(1)(b) (2011).


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the remaining issues.

 

44 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hall v. Moreno


Supreme Court Case No. 11SC842


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 14; 270 P.3d 961; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 106



February 27, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
C.A.R. 50 Certiorari to the Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case Nos. 11CA2340 and 11CA2341. District Court, City and County of Denver. District Court Case Nos. 11CV3461 and 11CV3463. Honorable Robert S. Hyatt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving the redistricting of Colorado's congressional districts following the results of the 2010 census, the District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, issued an order declaring the congressional districts unconstitutional. The district court adopted the Moreno/South Map and ordered respondent Colorado Secretary of State to implement it in future congressional elections. Certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the trial court reasonably balanced the many competing non-constitutional factors set forth in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 2-1-102 (2011) in a manner that would maximize fair and effective representation for all citizens. The trial court's findings were supported by the record, which was compiled through a thorough, open, and fair process. The trial court's adoption of the Moreno/South Map did not constitute an abuse of discretion. The trial court was reasonable in placing its concern for present communities of interest above a mechanistic attempt to minimize the disruption of existing district boundaries. While the adopted plan moved almost 1.4 million Coloradoans from their existing districts, it appeared that the trial court adopted the proposed map that it determined best balanced the many competing interests at stake. This conclusion was reasonable and was supported by the evidence, and thus, was neither arbitrary nor capricious. The trial court properly applied the controlling law and adopted a lawful redistricting scheme.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

 

45 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Colo. Ethics Watch v. Senate Majority Fund, LLC


Supreme Court Case No. 10SC276


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2012 CO 12; 269 P.3d 1248; 2012 Colo. LEXIS 103



February 21, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case Nos. 08CA2689 and 09CA384.
Colo. Ethics Watch v. Senate Majority Fund, LLC, 275 P.3d 674, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 368 (Colo. Ct. App., Mar. 18, 2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ethics organization filed a civil complaint against respondent political organizations, alleging their advertisements violated Colo. Const. art. XXVIII. The administrative law judge (ALJ) dismissed the complaint for failure to state a claim upon which relief could be granted. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. The ethics organization petitioned the state supreme court for certiorari review, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: Two political organizations distributed seventeen political advertisements that cost more than $200 each. An ethics organization alleged that the advertisements violated Colo. Const. art. XXVIII. Because none of the ads contained words or phrases that specifically directed the viewer to "vote for," "elect," or "support" a candidate, the facts were insufficient to render the ads subject to regulation as "express advocacy." That term was limited to speech that explicitly advocated for the election or defeat of a candidate through the use of the "magic words" set out in Buckley. Because none of the ads at issue amounted to "expenditures" under Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 2(8), the Supreme Court of Colorado held that neither political organization met the definition of a "political committee" under Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 2(12)(a). Thus, it was proper to dismiss the action for failing to state a claim on which relief could be granted.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded to the court of appeals to return the case to the ALJ with instructions to take action consistent with this opinion.

Huber v. Colo. Mining Ass'n


Case No. 10SC220


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


264 P.3d 884; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 840; 178 Oil & Gas Rep. 372



October 31, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Huber v. Colo. Mining Ass'n, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 845 (Colo., Oct. 31, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 09CA132.
Colo. Mining Ass'n v. Huber, 240 P.3d 453, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 209 (Colo. Ct. App., 2010)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals ruled that Colo. Const. art. X, § 20 (Amendment 1) required statewide voter approval each time petitioner Colorado Department of Revenue (Department) calculated an increase in the amount of tax due per ton of coal extracted as directed by the tax rate formula contained in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-29-106 (2011). The Department petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that because the limitations of Amendment 1 applied only to discretionary action taken by legislative bodies, the Department's implementation of the formula and collection of the tax did not require voter approval. The Department had no tax making or tax policy change authority. It could not modify the coal severance tax statutory mechanism or refuse to implement it. The rule the Department adopted in 2007 following a fifteen-year hiatus in implementation of the statute merely paralleled and enforced the statute. The amount of tax due was simply a function of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-29-106's tax rate. The Department's duties were plainly non-discretionary under the statute. The Department had no taxing or tax policy-making authority.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and the trial court's judgment was reinstated.


 

47 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lewis v. People


Case No. 09SC252


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


261 P.3d 480; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 691



September 12, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Lewis v. People, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 692 (Colo., Sept. 12, 2011)
Rehearing denied by Lewis v. People, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 805 (Colo., Oct. 17, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA2072.
People v. Lewis, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 251 (Colo. Ct. App., Feb. 12, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of three counts each of kidnapping (Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-3-302) and sexually assaulting his kidnap victims. He appealed his convictions and sentence; the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. He sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in elevating his kidnapping convictions from class four to class two felonies for the reason that he sexually assaulted his kidnap victims. He argued that People v. Henderson, which had held that sexual assault was not a lesser included offense of second degree kidnapping involving sexual assault, should be overruled in light of the subsequent U.S. Supreme Court holdings in Blakely v. Washington and Apprendi v. New Jersey. The high court declined to do so. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-408 (2010) barred the conviction of both a greater and its lesser-included offense; whether an offense was a lesser-included offense was determined by a strict comparison of the elements of the two offenses (the "strict elements test"). Nothing in the U.S. Supreme Court's interpretations of U.S. Const. amends. V or VI undermined the high court's prior determination that legislative reliance on a distinction between elements and sentencing factors evidenced an intent to avoid the creation of a new offense of second degree kidnapping involving sexual assault, into which the lesser offense of sexual assault could then merge.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

48 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pickering


Case No. 10SC446


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


276 P.3d 553; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 689



September 12, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at People v. Pickering, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 694 (Colo., Sept. 12, 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pickering v. Colorado, 132 S. Ct. 2429, 182 L. Ed. 2d 1061, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 3806 (U.S., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA2322.
People v. Pickering, 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 438 (Colo. Ct. App., Mar. 25, 2010)



DISPOSITION: ORDER REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with second-degree murder. After a jury trial, he was convicted of the lesser included offense of reckless manslaughter; he appealed. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed, finding error in the trial court's instruction given pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-704(4) (2010). The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court's instruction on the elements of reckless manslaughter stated that the People had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant recklessly caused death. The trial court charged the jury on the interaction between self-defense and the knowing and reckless requirements of the respective charges, and gave a carrying instruction stating the People did not bear the burden of disproving that defendant acted in self-defense with respect to the reckless manslaughter charge. The high court held that instructing the jury under § 18-1-704(4) that the People bore no burden of disproving self-defense with respect to crimes, such as reckless manslaughter, for which self-defense was not an affirmative defense did not improperly shift the prosecution's burden to prove recklessness, extreme indifference, or criminal negligence. So long as the trial court properly instructed the jury regarding the elements of the charged crime, a carrying instruction using the language of § 18-1-704(4) was not constitutionally erroneous. People v. Lara, 224 P.3d 388 (Colo. App. 2009), and People v. Taylor, 230 P.3d 1227 (Colo. App. 2009), were overruled to the extent that they held contrary.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and reinstated defendant's conviction of reckless manslaughter.

 

49 of 734 DOCUMENTS


In re Parental Responsibilities of L.S.


Case No. 09SC989


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


257 P.3d 201; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 550



June 27, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by In re Parental Responsibilities of L.S., 2011 Colo. LEXIS 645 (Colo., Aug. 15, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 08CA1872.
In re Parental Responsibilities Concerning L.S., 226 P.3d 1227, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 1782 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Nebraska district court entered an initial child custody determination awarding custody to respondent father. A Colorado district court awarded custody to petitioner mother, refusing to enforce the Nebraska determination. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed, holding that although Nebraska did not have jurisdiction, Colorado had to accord that determination full faith and credit. The mother petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that because Colorado statutes and case law incorporated the requirements of the Parental Kidnaping Prevention Act (PKPA), 28 U.S.C.S. § 1738A (2010), if a sister state exercised jurisdiction in accordance with the PKPA, then its exercise of jurisdiction would necessarily be in substantial conformity with Colorado law. Because the child had lived in Colorado for six consecutive months immediately before the father commenced the custody proceeding and had not live in Nebraska for six consecutive months, Colorado, and not Nebraska, was the child's home state. Because Colorado, and not Nebraska, had jurisdiction as the child's home state and Colorado did not decline jurisdiction on the ground that Nebraska was more appropriate, Neb. Rev. Stat. § 43-1238(a)(2), Nebraska did not have jurisdiction over the matter. As Nebraska did not have jurisdiction under its own law, the PKPA did not require Colorado to accord the Nebraska custody determination full faith and credit.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and remanded to that court to return the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Townsend v. People


Case No. 09SC652


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


252 P.3d 1108; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 434



May 31, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Townsend v. People, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 514 (Colo., June 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 06CA1207.
People v. Townsend, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 938 (Colo. Ct. App., May 28, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals that affirmed his conviction for escape by means of failing to remain within the extended terms of his confinement as established under his participation in an intensive supervision program as a condition of his parole, arguing that the jury instructions failed to set forth all elements of escape under the combined meanings of Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 17-27.5-104 and 18-8-208 (2010).


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that the jury instructions sufficiently set forth all elements of the escape offense with which defendant was charged. Read together, the jury instructions plainly set forth the elements of escape under Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 17-27.5-104 and 18-8-208 in requiring the jury to find that defendant was within the extended limits on his confinement as established under an intensive supervision program as a condition of his parole and that he knowingly escaped from those limits. While the jury instructions did not specifically equate the term "escaped" to the statutory language of "failed to remain," the plain and ordinary definitions of those terms conveyed substantially the same meaning. As such, the instructions set forth the elements of escape.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

Qwest Servs. Corp. v. Blood


Case No. 09SC534


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


252 P.3d 1071; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 428



May 23, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by Qwest Servs. Corp. v. Blood, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 519 (Colo., June 20, 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari dismissed by Qwest Servs. Corp. v. Blood, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 581 (U.S., Jan. 12, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 08CA134.
Blood v. Qwest Servs. Corp., 224 P.3d 301, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 716 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a suit arising from injuries suffered by respondent, an utility company lineman, while working on a wooden utility pole owned by petitioner, petitioner appealed a decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals that affirmed a judgment in favor of the lineman, arguing that the award of exemplary damages violated the Due Process Clause and that the evidence was insufficient to support a finding that its conduct was willful and wanton.


OVERVIEW: At trial, the jury found petitioner negligent for failing to maintain a utility pole that collapsed while the lineman was climbing it as part of his employment with a utility company and awarded compensatory and exemplary damages to the lineman. On review, the court held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-102(1)(b) (2010) did not violate due process because it did not permit a jury to award exemplary damages to punish a defendant for harm to non-parties; instead, the statute complied with the Philip Morris ruling by merely permitting a jury to consider the rights and safety of others in assessing the willful and wanton nature (i.e., the reprehensibility) of defendant's conduct. Further, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to demonstrate that petitioner's failure to implement a periodic pole inspection for the 46 years prior to the accident at issue was willful and wanton beyond a reasonable doubt and therefore satisfied the requirements for an exemplary damages award under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-102(1)(a).


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' decision upholding the jury's award of exemplary damages was affirmed.

Sanchez-Martinez v. People


Case No. 10SC83


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


250 P.3d 1248; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 366



May 9, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the District Court. Adams County District Court, Case No. 09CV578. Honorable John T. Bryan, Judge.
Sanchez-Martinez v. People, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 381 (Colo., May 9, 2011)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Adams County District Court, Colorado, reversed the county court's order vacating defendant's guilty plea to third degree assault as unknowing, involuntary, and unintelligent. Defendant petitioned for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court concluded the prosecution was on notice that the constitutionality of defendant's plea would be at issue. The prosecution instituted questioning in which it sought to establish that defendant had a knowing, voluntary, intelligent plea. The magistrate explicitly stated that he was considering setting aside the guilty plea as unconstitutional. The prosecution was on notice of this issue. Defendant filed his Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) motion within eighteen months of the date of conviction, as required by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-5-402(1). The trial court was permitted to address the issue of whether defendant's guilty plea was knowing, voluntary, and intelligent. The Colo. R. Crim. P. 11 court fell short of its duty to ensure defendant's understanding of the important constitutional rights he possessed, and that were waived by pleading guilty. Defendant was not informed of and did not understand the alternative courses of action available to him. Because he could not write or read in English or Spanish, defendant could not have understood the important constitutional rights waived by pleading guilty.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed and the county court's judgment vacating defendant's guilty plea was reinstated.

People v. Gallegos

Reported at People v. Santistevan, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 296 (Colo., Apr. 11, 2011)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. El Paso County, Case No. 09CR3830. Honorable Deborah J. Grohs, Judge. Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. El Paso County, Case No. 09CR3853. Honorable Deborah J. Grohs, Judge. Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. El Paso County, Case No. 09CR3848. Honorable Deborah J. Grohs, Judge. Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court. DISPOSITION: ORDER REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Five defendants jointed in a motion to suppress evidence derived from illegal wiretaps, claiming that the orders authorizing wiretap surveillances were invalid because the judge who issued them did so while his son worked in the District Attorney's Office. The trial court granted the motion to suppress. The State appealed.

There was no support for the trial court's finding that the renewal application for Subject Telephone Two did not contain a statement of results sufficient to support the issuance of the extension order. The fifteen-day reports were proper. The notice issues did not warrant suppression of the evidence. Defendants did not claim prejudice from the late delivery of materials because they initially succeeded on their motion to suppress the wiretap evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Kobobel v. State Dep't of Natural Res.


Case No. 10SA92


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


249 P.3d 1127; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 248



March 28, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Kobobel v. Dep't of Natural Res., 2011 Colo. LEXIS 361 (Colo., Apr. 25, 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kobobel v. Colo., 2011 U.S. LEXIS 5755 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, Water Division 1, Case No. 09CW175. Honorable James F. Hartmann, Judge.
Kobobel v. State, 215 P.3d 1218, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 381 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court, Water Division 1, Colorado, dismissed plaintiff well owners' inverse condemnation claim. The well owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The well owners argued that the district court was the proper forum for their complaint because their claims for inverse condemnation were not "water matters" within the water court's exclusive jurisdiction. The supreme court found that the nature of the claim and relief sought required a court to determine whether the well owners had the right to use water from their wells without State interference. Such a determination was a water matter that fell uniquely within the jurisdiction of the water court. The well owners had no constitutionally protected property interest in the unfettered use of the water in their wells; consequently, they could not show that the State had taken their property by curtailing the out-of-priority use of their wells. The well owners neither held title to the water in their decreed wells, nor was their right to use the water unfettered. The State's order did not deprive the well owners of any constitutionally protected right to the unfettered use of the water in their wells; the well owners had no such right. The Water Right Determination and Administration Act of 1969 preserved Colorado's prior appropriation doctrine.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

55 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Sidman v. Sidman (In re D.I.S.)


Case No. 09SC483


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


249 P.3d 775; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 231



March 21, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Sidman v. Sidman (In re D.I.S.), 2011 Colo. LEXIS 236 (Colo., Mar. 21, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA1971.
D.I.S. v. Sidman, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 699 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 23, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals that affirmed a lower court judgment denying their petition to terminate a consensual guardianship of their child, arguing that the lower courts erred in concluding that, when the parents consented to the guardianship, they lost the presumption that their decision to terminate that guardianship was in the best interests of the child.


OVERVIEW: Parents entered into a guardianship relationship with appellees, non-parent relatives, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-14-204(2)(a) (2010) to allow them to address the mother's post-partum depression. When they petitioned to terminate the guardianship, the guardians objected. The trial and appellate courts ruled that, by consenting to the guardianship, the parents lost the constitutional presumption that they made decisions in the best interests of their child. On review, the court held that, where the guardianship was established through parental consent, the parents delegated the day-to-day care, custody, and control of their child to the guardians. However, just as the fit parents' decision to consent to the guardianship was presumed to be in the best interests of the child, so too was their subsequent decision to seek termination of the guardianship and regain care, custody, and control of the child presumed to be in the best interests of the child. In the absence of a limitation in the guardianship order, when fit parents seek to terminate a guardianship, the guardians bear the burden of demonstrating that termination of the guardianship is not in the best interests of the child.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

Cropper v. People


Case No. 09SC828


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


251 P.3d 434; 2011 Colo. LEXIS 184



March 14, 2011, Decided

Rehearing denied by Cropper v. People, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 358 (Colo., Apr. 25, 2011)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA1001.
People v. Cropper, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 1546 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 27, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5) (2010) was constitutional as applied to defendant. The court of appeals upheld the admission of the shoe-print report, finding that defendant's failure to follow the procedural requirements in § 16-3-309(5) resulted in a waiver of his confrontation rights. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Colo. Rev. Defendant had notice of the presence of the report and had an adequate opportunity to assert his confrontation rights and request that the technician be present for cross-examination. Because defendant's attorney did not follow the procedural rules set forth in the statute, she waived defendant's right of confrontation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

57 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Glab v. Julian (In re B.J.)


Case No. 10SA146


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


242 P.3d 1128; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 907



November 30, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. El Paso County District Court, Case No. 09DR1720. Honorable Barney Iuppa, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding pursuant to Colo. R. App. P. 21, petitioner father sought issuance of a rule to show cause to determine whether the standards enunciated in Troxel v. Granville, 530 U.S. 57 (2000), and In re Adoption of C.A., 137 P.3d 318 (Colo. 2006), applied to a trial court's consideration of any order allocating parental responsibilities to a non-parent over the objection of a fit, custodial parent.


OVERVIEW: Even after the father was granted sole custody of the children, he permitted significant ongoing contact with the foster parents from December 2005 until March 2009. After the father decided to cease having the children stay at the foster parents' home, they petitioned for allocation of parenting responsibilities (APR) under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 14-10-123(1)(c) (2010). When a child and family investigator (CFI) recommended visits with the foster parents over the father's objection, the El Paso County District Court (Colorado) concluded that the Troxel and C.A. standards were inapplicable to the investigatory stage of an APR proceeding. The court reversed. Pursuant to the Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, the father had the fundamental right to make decisions regarding the care, custody, and control of their children. Therefore, ordering visitation with the foster parents had to be in accordance with the Troxel and C.A. standards to determine the best interests of the children, considering the factors in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 14-10-124(1.5)(a)-(b). No investigatory exception existed as the father's fundamental liberty interest in raising his children would thus be suspended.


OUTCOME: The court made the rule absolute, reversed the district court's visitation order, and returned the matter for further proceedings with this decision.
