City of Broomfield v. Farmers Reservoir & Irrigation Co. (In re Water Rights of Broomfield)


Case No. 09SA269


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


239 P.3d 1270; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 726



September 27, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by City & County of Broomfield v. City & County of Broomfield, 2010 Colo. LEXIS 815 (Colo., Oct. 25, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court. Water Division No. 1, Case No. 05CW290. Honorable Roger Klein, Judge.
City & County of Broomfield v. Farmers Reservoir & Irrigation Co. (In re Application for Water Rights), 235 P.3d 296, 2010 Colo. LEXIS 507 (Colo., 2010)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court, Water Division No. 1, Colorado, awarded costs against appellant mutual ditch company for the trial concerning appellee city's application for a change in the use of water rights. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company argued that, as a mutual ditch company, it was exempt from costs under Colo. R. Civ. P. 54(b) because a substantial number of its shareholders were exempt from costs under that rule. The supreme court noted that the General Assembly had defined a mutual ditch company as an association of three or more people to form a corporation for the purposes of constructing a ditch or reservoir as set forth in its articles of incorporation, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 7-42-101(1). The legislature had not designated mutual ditch companies as state agencies. Given the plain meaning of Rule 54(b), a mutual ditch company was not the state, its officers, or its agencies, and a water court may award costs against a mutual ditch company. Because the company did not make a specific argument regarding the Petition Clause of the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, it did not preserve the issue for review. The right to oppose a water application originated from statutory provisions and was not a fundamental constitutional right. Distinguishing between governmental and nongovernmental entities did not create a suspect class. Rule 54(d) did not violate due process or equal protection.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

59 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Howes v. Brown


Case No. 10SA135


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


235 P.3d 1071; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 508



June 28, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to § 1-40-107(2), C.R.S. (2009). Appeal from the Ballot Title Setting Board.



DISPOSITION: ACTION REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner registered elector filed suit pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 1-10-107(2) (2009) challenging respondent Ballot Title Setting Board's action in setting the title, ballot title and submission clause, and summary for an initiative that proposed a constitutional amendment to Colo. Const. art. XVI, § 5 that would impose a tax on beverage containers, exempting containers that held alcohol beverages, dairy products, and medicine.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that the initiative contained multiple subjects in violation of Colo. Const. art. V, § 1(5.5), namely: (1) creating and administering a beverage container tax, and (2) prohibiting the Colorado General Assembly from exercising its legislative authority over the basin roundtables and interbasin compact committee until the year 2015, while embedding these entities within the water sections of the Colorado Constitution and vesting them with significant new authority. The initiative's broad statement of purpose, to protect and preserve the waters of the state, did not properly unite the separate subjects into one. Contrary to the proponents' argument, there was no necessary and proper connection between the establishment and administration of a beverage container tax and a prolonged prohibition on the exercise of the General Assembly's authority over the basin roundtables and the interbasin compact committee. The initiative's prohibition on actions by the General Assembly was especially troubling considering the oversight and authority the General Assembly traditionally had over agencies it established.


OUTCOME: The action of the board was reversed and the case was returned to the board with directions to strike the title and return the initiative to the proponents.

 

60 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Misenhelter v. People


Case No. 09SC183


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


234 P.3d 657; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 457



June 21, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 21, 2010.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Misenhelter v. Colo., 2010 U.S. LEXIS 8727 (U.S., Nov. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA1785.
People v. Misenhelter, 214 P.3d 497, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 25 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to aggravated incest and negligent child abuse and was sentenced to 25 years on the child abuse charge. The Colorado Court of Appeals upheld the sentence, holding that consideration of a concurrent conviction did not violate the constitutional requirement - described in Blakely v. Washington and its progeny - that facts must be submitted to a jury and proved beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant petitioned for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The presumptive sentencing range for negligent child abuse was 4 to 16 years, but the range of possible penalties listed in trial documents stated that a maximum of 32 years imprisonment was possible. Defendant argued that the trial court erred by considering his "concurrent" conviction for aggravated incest during his sentencing for the child abuse charge. The court held that a "concurrent" conviction - or a conviction that issues from the same proceeding as the crime being sentenced - is Blakely-exempt if it is entered according to proper constitutional procedure and prior to sentencing. The trial court properly used defendant's aggravated incest conviction to aggravate defendant's sentence for negligent child abuse beyond the presumptive range. Defendant's conviction for aggravated incest was entered before his sentencing for negligent child abuse. The aggravated incest conviction was Blakely-exempt because there was no error in entering the plea and the conviction predated sentencing for negligent child abuse. It did not matter that the original plea included both the incest and child abuse charges because the incest conviction clearly preceded the child abuse sentence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was affirmed.

 

61 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCarty


Case No. 09SA161


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


229 P.3d 1041; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 361



May 10, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court, Mesa County District Court. Case No. 08CR1817. Honorable Richard Gurley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: ORDER AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People of the State of Colorado sought review of an order from the Mesa County District Court (Colorado), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence of drugs that were found in his vehicle.


OVERVIEW: After being followed from a store that was the object of police surveillance and being stopped for a traffic infraction, defendant conceded buying a "pot pipe" at the store, took the unwrapped pipe from his pocket, and turned it over to the police. While searching defendant's vehicle, an officer discovered methamphetamine. Defendant was charged with possession of drug paraphernalia and more than one gram of methamphetamine. The trial court granted his motion for suppression of the drugs but denied suppression of the pipe. On appeal, the court found that being stopped for a traffic infraction immediately after leaving a suspect store and being in possession of a recently purchased and still unwrapped "pot pipe," although sufficient to justify an arrest for possession of drug paraphernalia, was insufficient to provide reasonable, articulable suspicion that additional evidence of that offense might be found in defendant's vehicle. Although it was conceivable that the vehicle might contain some evidence of the possession offense for which the officers had probable cause to arrest, nothing peculiar to the circumstances supported a reasonable suspicion that additional evidence existed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

62 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Specialty Rests. Corp. v. Nelson


Case No. 09SC536


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


231 P.3d 393; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 363



May 10, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 08CA1069.
Nelson v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office of Colo., 219 P.3d 416, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 990 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent claimant was awarded permanent total disability (PTD) benefits and received a lump sum payment in the amount of the maximum aggregate available at the time of her injury. In 2007, the legislature increased the maximum aggregate lump sum available. The Colorado Court of Appeals concluded the claimant was entitled to the additional lump sum payment. Petitioner Colorado Insurance Guaranty Association (CIGA) petitioned for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The claimant suffered a work-related injury in 1990. The court concluded that the claimant was entitled to the additional lump sum payment under the 2007 amendment to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-43-406(2) (2009). The 2007 amendment applied prospectively. The claimant requested the additional lump sum payment five months after the legislature amended § 8-43-406. The claimant's rights and CIGA's liabilities vested on December 5, 2006, when an administrative law judge determined that the claimant was permanently and totally disabled and ordered CIGA to pay her PTD benefits. Neither the $ 26,292 lump sum payment the claimant requested and received in February 2007, nor the additional $ 33,708 payment to which the court found the claimant was entitled, altered these rights or liabilities. Instead, the lump sum payments constituted an advance of the award of PTD benefits to which the claimant already was entitled. The lump sum provision was procedural in nature and applied prospectively to requests for lump sum payments filed subsequent to the amendment's date of enactment, irrespective of the date of the claimant's injury.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

63 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Owens


Case No. 09SA248


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


228 P.3d 969; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 305



April 12, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at People v. Ray, 252 P.3d 1042, 2011 Colo. LEXIS 357 (Colo., 2011)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by In re Plaintiff: the People of Colo., 2014 Colo. LEXIS 526 (Colo., June 30, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Arapahoe County District Court. Case No. 06CR705. Honorable Gerald Rafferty, Judge.
People v. Owens, 219 P.3d 379, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 563 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: ORDER REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People immediately appealed a ruling of the Arapahoe County District Court, Colorado, declaring unconstitutional a portion of the statutory scheme providing for unitary review in death penalty cases.


OVERVIEW: The trial court construed Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-12-208(3) (2009) to impose a two-year time limit on the completion of all proceedings for postconviction review, the certification of the record, and all appellate briefing and to prohibit extensions of any kind beyond that time. Although it upheld what it considered to be the legislatively imposed two-year limit, the trial court found the prohibition against any extension beyond that period unconstitutional for conflicting with Colo. R. Crim. P. 32.2 and infringing on the rulemaking power of the judiciary. The supreme court held that because § 16-12-208(3) does not itself impose an absolute two-year time limit on presenting a unitary appeal to the supreme court but rather directs it to do so, the trial court misconstrued the statute as conflicting with the rule. Because the rule implements the legislature's direction by imposing a series of highly specific time limits, which are designed to meet the two-year goal when, but only when, that can be accomplished without violating the defendant's constitutional rights or the legislature's other expressly articulated goals, no absolute two-year time limit exists in either statute or rule.


OUTCOME: The trial court's ruling finding § 16-12-208(3) to be unconstitutional in part was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

64 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Dallman v. Ritter


Case No. 09SA224, Case No. 09CV1188, Case No. 09CV1200


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


225 P.3d 610; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 314; 188 L.R.R.M. 2544



February 22, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, District Court, City and County of Denver. Case Nos. 09CV1188 and 09CV1200.
Dallman v. Ritter, 2010 Colo. LEXIS 165 (Colo., Feb. 22, 2010)



DISPOSITION: ORDER AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, granted judgment in favor of plaintiffs, electors and organizations, a preliminary injunction on the campaign finance restrictions of Amendment 54, Colo. Const. art. XXVIII. Defendants, Governor and Executive Director of the Colorado Department of Administration, appealed.


OVERVIEW: The plaintiffs argued that Amendment 54 was substantially overbroad on its face. The supreme court found that because Amendment 54 applied to "any government contract" that did not solicit three bids, without including any further limiting language, it was overbroad. Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 15 was over-inclusive as it failed to tailor its prohibitions towards those who had some control over awarding no-bid contracts, which would be directly correlated to its purpose of preventing the appearance of impropriety. Some of Amendment 54's penalty provisions were overbroad. Amendment 54's prohibition on contributions made on behalf of family members served to substantially chill speech. A restriction on U.S. Const. amend. I activity for those that served a nonprofit fell outside of Amendment 54's plainly legitimate sweep. Possible applications of Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 15 rendered it unconstitutionally vague with respect to prohibited contributions of immediate family members. The organized labor provisions violated U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The entire amendment was unconstitutional and could not be salvaged as a meaningful legislative enactment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

65 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Disher


Case No. 07SC1088


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


224 P.3d 254; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 113



February 16, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Adams County District Court. Case No. 07CV650.
People v. Disher, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 432 (Colo., Apr. 21, 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State petitioned for certiorari, challenging the affirmation by the Adams County District Court (Colorado) of a county court ruling that no domestic violence evaluation could be required of defendant when he was convicted of harassing a woman whom he previously dated because there was no showing of an "intimate relationship" under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-6-800.3 (2009).


OVERVIEW: After defendant's dating relationship with the victim ended, he entered her apartment uninvited and refused to leave. The police were summoned and were able to remove defendant following a struggle. Defendant was convicted of harassment and obstructing an officer. While the State requested that defendant be required to undergo a domestic violence evaluation, the county court held that there was no evidence of an intimate relationship because there was no testimony about a sexual relationship and that, without evidence of a sexual relationship, a domestic violence evaluation would be inappropriate. The district court affirmed. On review, the court held that, while a sexual relationship may be an indicator of an intimate relationship, it was not a necessary condition of an intimate relationship for the purpose of the Colorado domestic violence statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-6-800.3 (2009). The relationship must be more than that of a roommate, friend, or acquaintance; there must be a romantic attachment or shared parental status between the parties. The victim's testimony that she and defendant had dated exclusively was enough for the judge to find an intimate relationship.


OUTCOME: The decision of the district court was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

66 of 734 DOCUMENTS


MDC Holdings, Inc. v. Town of Parker


Case No. 08SC972


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


223 P.3d 710; 2010 Colo. LEXIS 74



February 8, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA1758.
Town of Parker v. Colo. Dep't of Revenue, 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 1516 (Colo. Ct. App., Oct. 16, 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, towns, requested injunctive relief to prevent the Executive Director of the Colorado Department of Revenue from hearing an appeal filed by petitioners, home builders, from the towns' denial of a use tax refund request. The district court dismissed the injunction. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed and enjoined the Executive Director's proceedings. Certiorari review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The builders requested and obtained an informal hearing by the towns to review the denial of their use tax refund requests. Following the informal hearing, the towns insisted that the builders request a formal hearing before their Finance Directors, as a pre-condition to exercising the right of appeal under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-2-106.1 (2009) to the Executive Director. The reviewing court held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-2-106.1 provided for only an informal hearing at the local level and superseded provisions of the towns' codes that required a taxpayer to submit to a formal hearing. The reviewing court agreed with the district court that the Executive Director had jurisdiction to hear the use tax refund appeals because the builders properly exhausted its local remedies in accordance with Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-2-106.1. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-2-106.1 (2009) contained its own controlling prescription for exhaustion of local remedies that defined and controlled the appellate process in local sales and use tax appeals. A local government was not free to ignore or frustrate a taxpayer's effort to exercise its appellate rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded with directions that it return the case to the district court for dismissal of the towns' injunction complaint against the Executive Director.

Curious Theatre Co. v. Colo. Dep't of Pub. Health & Env't


Case No. 08SC351


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


220 P.3d 544; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 1158



December 14, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Curious Theatre Co. v. Colo. Dep't of Pub. Health & Env't, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 3998 (U.S., May 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 06CA2260.
Curious Theater Co. v. Colo. Dep't of Pub. Health & Env't, 216 P.3d 71, 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 442 (Colo. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonprofit theaters sued defendant Colorado Department of Public Health and Environment, seeking a judgment declaring Colorado's ban on theatrical smoking to be an unconstitutional infringement on their freedom of speech and an order enjoining its enforcement. The trial court denied the theaters' motion for a preliminary injunction. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's decision. The theaters petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that, even assuming that theatrical smoking actually could amount to protected expressive conduct under some circumstances, the statutory ban on such smoking contained in the Colorado Clean Indoor Act did not impermissibly infringe on the theaters' constitutionally protected freedom of expression under the U.S. and Colorado Constitutions because it was content neutral and narrowly tailored to serve the State's substantial interest in protecting the public health and welfare. There was no question that the State's legitimate interest in preserving and improving the health, comfort, and environment of the public was furthered by limiting the public's exposure to environmental smoke, even from tobacco-free alternatives. Apart from its interest in the public's physical health, the State had a weighty, essentially esthetic interest in proscribing intrusive and unpleasant formats for expression sufficient to justify a content-neutral restriction on expression. The connection between limiting exposure to environmental smoke and banning indoor public smoking in places of public accommodation was apparent without further evidentiary support.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

68 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Wolf Ranch, LLC v. City of Colo. Springs


Case No. 08SC1073


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


220 P.3d 559; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 1262



December 14, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 07CA2184.
Wolf Ranch, LLC v. City of Colo. Springs, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 1167 (Colo., Dec. 14, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developer contended that respondent, the City of Colorado Springs, Colorado, violated the Regulatory Impairment of Property Rights Act (RIPRA), Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-20-201 to -205 (2009), when it denied the developer's request to exempt its property from a drainage fee. The state supreme court granted certiorari to determine whether the Colorado Court of Appeals erred in ruling that RIPRA was not applicable.


OVERVIEW: The City of Colorado Springs decided to impose a drainage fee as a condition to land use approvals connected with the development of real property. The developer claimed that Colorado Springs violated the Regulatory Impairment of Property Rights Act (RIPRA), Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-20-201 to -205 (2009), when it denied the developer's request to exempt its property from the fee. The Colorado Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals, holding that Colorado Springs' drainage fee system did not trigger RIPRA. The fee assessed to the developer was promulgated by Colorado Springs via legislative process and uniformly assessed to all property owners occupying the drainage basin. Accordingly, Colorado Springs' drainage fee system fell under the Act's exception for legislatively formulated fees imposed upon a broad class of property owners, as set forth in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 29-20-203(1) (2009).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was affirmed, and the case was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

 

69 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gutierrez


Case No. 09SA69


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


222 P.3d 925; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 1160



December 14, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court Weld County District Court Case No. 08CR2087. Honorable James F. Hartmann, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution brought an interlocutory appeal from an order of the Weld County District Court (Colorado), which, in a case of identity theft and criminal impersonation, granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence of tax records showing that he reported income from work that he performed while providing his employer with a social security number registered to another person.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's tax returns and other documents were seized from the office of his tax preparer, along with several thousand other client files, pursuant to a search warrant. The trial court found that the search warrant, which did not identify defendant or the tax preparer as the target of the search, was invalid and that the good faith exception to the exclusionary rule did not apply because the warrant lacked indicia of probable cause. The court agreed with the trial court's conclusion that the search was essentially a fishing expedition and that the officers' reliance on the warrant was objectively unreasonable. Probable cause to search defendant's individual file was absent because the supporting affidavit, which indicated only that some clients might have provided false information to the tax preparer, did not establish any connection at all between defendant and criminal activity. Without evidence of a pervasive fraudulent scheme, searching all files could not be justified. Defendant had standing to invoke the protections of the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, because he had a reasonable expectation of privacy in his tax documents in the possession of the tax preparer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting the motion to suppress evidence and remanded to that court for further proceedings.

 

70 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lobato v. People


Case No. 08SC185


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


218 P.3d 358; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 998



October 19, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Lobato v. State, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 1007 (Colo., Oct. 19, 2009)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Lobato v. State, 2013 Colo. LEXIS 383 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 06CA0733.
Lobato v. People, 216 P.3d 29, 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 69 (Colo. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals concluded that plaintiff school districts lacked standing to sue defendant State, and that plaintiff parents, who challenged the adequacy of the state's public school funding system under the education clause of the Colorado Constitution, presented a nonjusticiable political question. Plaintiffs sought review of the appellate court's judgment on school district standing and justiciability.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs alleged that Colorado's public school system violated the mandate set forth in Colo. Const. art. IX, § 2, that the legislature provide a "thorough and uniform" system of public education throughout the state. The court concluded that plaintiffs were entitled to the opportunity to prove their allegations. To be successful, plaintiffs would have to prove that the state's current public school financing system was not rationally related to the legislature's constitutional mandate to provide a thorough and uniform system of public education. Although the State asserted that Amendment 23, Colo. Const. art. IX, § 17, put in context, set the constitutionally minimum level of state funding required by the education clause, and therefore, plaintiffs did not present a justiciable question, the court noted that Amendment 23 prescribed minimum increases for state funding of education and was not intended to qualify, quantify, or modify the thorough and uniform mandate expressed in the education clause. Consequently, the Amendment 23 mandate related solely to a minimum level of funding and did not affect the court's holding that plaintiffs presented a justiciable claim for relief.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to that court to be returned to the trial court for further proceedings.

Eddie's Leaf Spring Shop & Towing LLC v. Colo. PUC


Case No. 08SA359


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


218 P.3d 326; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 936



October 13, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Eddie's Leaf Spring Shop & Towing LLC v. Colo. PUC, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 992 (Colo., Oct. 13, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court City and County of Denver, Case No. 07CV11505. Honorable Kenneth M. Plotz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant towing company challenged a civil penalty assessment notice for failure to provide records to the appellee Public Utilities Commission (PUC). The District Court City and County of Denver, Colorado, upheld the fine assessed to the towing company. The towing company sought direct appellate review.


OVERVIEW: The towing company operated as a towing carrier and had obtained a permit from the PUC, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 40-13-106 (2009). The towing company towed the car owner's car without his consent. The car owner filed a complaint with the PUC alleging that the towing company refused a timely release of his car and personal belongings, including his wife's insulin medication. The PUC initiated an investigation into the alleged complaint. The towing company challenged the lead investigator's credential, and it failed to provide requested documents. The towing company was fined. The towing company claimed that 49 U.S.C.S. § 14501(c) preempted PUC regulations concerning towing carriers. Additionally, the towing company contended that the PUC rule authorizing a civil penalty for failure to provide records constituted a warrantless search in violation of the U.S. and Colorado Constitutions. The supreme court held that 49 U.S.C.S. § 14501(c) did not preempt the state from regulating towing carriers conducting non-consensual tows. Further, the regulation authorizing records requests was not a warrantless search in violation of the U.S. and Colorado Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the district court upholding the PUC's findings and assessment of a civil penalty. The matter was remanded to the district court with instructions to return the case to the PUC for further proceedings.

Krutsinger v. People


Case No. 08SC378


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


219 P.3d 1054; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 935

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Krutsinger v. Colorado, 130 S. Ct. 2353, 176 L. Ed. 2d 562, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 3104 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 06CA0532.
People v. Krutsinger, 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 603 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for review of the Colorado Court of Appeals' judgment affirming his convictions for multiple sexual-assault-on-a-child and incest-related crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for multiple sexual-assault-on-a-child and incest-related crimes. The court of appeals held that the trial court erred in excluding testimony from a therapist of defendant's stepdaughter. Rather than deciding whether the error was harmless, the court of appeals remanded the case to the trial court. Upon recertification, the court of appeals found the error harmless according to the standard for evaluating nonconstitutional error. The Supreme Court of Colorado agreed with the outcome. By conditioning a designation of federal constitutional error on a determination that the error would impact the verdict sufficiently to be constitutionally material, the court of appeals applied the wrong legal standard. Because the exclusion of the therapist's testimony did not deprive defendant of a meaningful opportunity to challenge the victim's credibility, the error did not rise to the level of federal constitutional error.

73 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Miles v. Fleming


Case No. 08SC383


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


214 P.3d 1054; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 590



June 29, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Miles v. Fleming, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 639 (Colo., June 29, 2009)
Rehearing denied by Miles v. Fleming, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 767 (Colo., Aug. 24, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 2007CV10404.
Miles v. Fleming, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 885 (Colo., Aug. 18, 2008)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court, City and County of Denver, Colorado, affirmed an eviction of petitioner tenant by the county court. The county court determined that respondent owner proved reasonable grounds to believe criminal conduct was being conducted on the premises. The tenant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the county court correctly determined that the owner provided adequate notice of the grounds upon which her demand for possession was predicated. Nothing more was required than notification of the legal ground upon which the owner claims a right of possession, and the inclusion of pattern language of compliance did not render the owner's demand equivocal or conditional. The county court's oral order of restitution, however, was not supported by the statutorily required finding of an unlawful detention. The county court correctly understood that the owner had the burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence a violation of the lease. The county court never found proof of prohibited criminal activity. Unlawful detention for violation of a covenant of the lease, whether express or implied by statute, required proof by a preponderance of the evidence that the covenant was actually violated - not merely that the owner had reasonable grounds to believe it was violated. In the absence of sufficient proof of an unlawful detention, the county court erred in granting restitution of the premises.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court affirming the order of restitution was reversed.

 

74 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Huber v. Kenna


Case No. 07SC657


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


205 P.3d 1158; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 665



April 20, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 06CA0608.
Huber v. Kenna, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 412 (Colo., Apr. 20, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Colorado Department of Revenue issued notices of deficiency to respondents, taxpayers, on the ground that their tax credits exceeded the $ 100,000 limit. The taxpayers contested the notices, and relief was denied. The district court reversed the Department's decision, and the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. Review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers were two couples who purchased property as tenants in common and subsequently donated the property as a conservation easement. The taxpayers split the value of the donated easement in half and claimed tax credits totaling $ 154,700 pursuant to the Conservation Easement Tax Credit Act. The Conservation Easement Tax Credit Act limited the amount of the credit allowed to $100,000 per donation. The reviewing court held that although each taxpayer could claim a tax credit, the statute expressly limited the amount of the credits claimed to an aggregate total of $ 100,000 per donation. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-22- 522(2) (1999) did not allow each taxpayer to claim a tax credit of up to $ 100,000. The reference to "per donation" necessarily limited the available tax credit to an aggregate of $ 100,000. Because the tax credits claimed by the taxpayers exceeded $ 100,000, the Department correctly issued the notices of deficiency to the taxpayers. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-22-522 (1999) limited the tax credit given to a tenancy in common for a donated conservation easement to $ 100,000.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed.

 

75 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Sperry v. Field


Case No. 08SC438


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


205 P.3d 365; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 324



April 13, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 07CA282.
Sperry v. Field, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 344 (Colo., Apr. 13, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant personal injury judgment creditor sued appellee driver for personal injuries suffered in an accident and obtained a damages award. The driver appealed, challenging the award of prejudgment interest. The award was reversed and remanded. On remand, the creditor was awarded post-judgment interest, but from the date judgment was entered. The creditor appealed, and the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. The creditor sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the creditor contended post-judgment interest should run from "the date the claim accrues" until satisfaction of the judgment. The supreme court disagreed and held that this interpretation was contrary to the common understanding of the term "post-judgment interest" and did not serve the statutory goal of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-21-101 (2009); it was unconstitutional to award interest earned during the period between the accrual of the cause of action and the entry of judgment at a different rate based on whether or not a judgment debtor appealed. Because the creditor did not request, and was therefore not awarded, pre-judgment interest, she could not receive interest for the time period between the accrual date and the judgment date, regardless of whether it was termed "post-judgment" interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

76 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Catholic Health Initiatives Colo. v. City of Pueblo


Case No. 07SC905


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


207 P.3d 812; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 321



March 30, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing June 1, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 05CA2432.
Catholic Health Initiatives Colo. v. City of Pueblo, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 313 (Colo., Mar. 30, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer, a nonprofit organization affiliated with a church, challenged an assessment by respondent city of sales and use taxes associated with a facility that provided care and housing for the elderly. The trial court found that the facility was not exempt from taxation, and the Colorado Court of Appeals reversed, holding that the operation of the facility was a religious activity. Both parties sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The Colorado Supreme Court interpreted Pueblo, Colo., Mun. Code, § 14-4-76 (2008), which provided an exemption for charitable organizations, as requiring that an organization be engaged in qualifying activities in order to be eligible for the exemption, regardless of whether it was a religious or charitable organization with I.R.C. § 501(c)(3) status. Under that interpretation, the operation of the facility was not a charitable organization because its ministry to the physical, mental, and spiritual needs of residents was not exclusively free and voluntary and because it did not exclusively lessen the burden of government. Its services were not exclusively provided freely and voluntarily in part because they were provided on a quid pro quo transactional basis. If a resident required more intensive nursing care, that care was provided for an additional cost. The pricing and fee structure indicated the transactional, rather than charitable, nature of the services provided. The fact that the facility maintained some residents who were unable to pay for the services they received did not change the analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holding of the court of appeals and remanded the case in order to provide the parties with an opportunity to raise issues and present additional evidence as appropriate under the clarified understanding of the definition of "charitable organization," given that the focus below was on whether the taxpayer engaged in religious activity, rather than the question of whether it was a charitable organization.

 

77 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shari


Case No. 08SA383


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


204 P.3d 453; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 322



March 30, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 Jefferson County District Court, Case No. 08CR1421. Honorable R. Brooke Jackson, Judge.
People v. Shari, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 320 (Colo., Mar. 30, 2009)



DISPOSITION: RULE MADE ABSOLUTE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Alternative defense counsel petitioned for relief under Colo. App. R. 21 from a judgment of the Jefferson County District Court (Colorado) disqualifying all attorneys employed by the Public Defender's Office from representing defendant in his murder case.


OVERVIEW: The district court disqualified attorneys employed by the Public Defender's Office from representing defendant because the Office had previously represented three witnesses for the State. On review, the court held that the district court abused its discretion in doing so because no direct conflict warranting disqualification of defendant's counsel existed, as none of defendant's attorneys had represented the witnesses and did not receive confidential, material information from the witnesses. Any direct conflict that may have existed relating to an individual public defendant's prior representation of the State's witness could not be imputed to defendant's attorneys under Colo. R. Prof. Conduct 1.11 and any concerns regarding the communication of confidential information from the public defenders to defendant's attorneys were assuaged by the Office's screening policy.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

78 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Mesa County Bd. of County Comm'rs v. State


Case No. 08SA216


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


203 P.3d 519; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 236



March 16, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court City and County of Denver, Case No. 07CV12064. Honorable Christina M. Habas, Judge.
Mesa County Bd. of County Comm'rs v. State, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 279 (Colo., Mar. 16, 2009)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Governmental defendants appealed a judgment of the District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, finding unconstitutional the amendments to the Colorado School Finance Act made by SB 07-199 that allowed local school districts to collect and expend revenues in excess of the property tax revenue limitations of Colo. Const. art. X, § 20.


OVERVIEW: The effect of SB 07-199 was to shift some of the burden of funding public schools from the state back to local school districts. In this case, plaintiffs sought a refund of $ 117.8 million allegedly collected in violation of Colo. Const. art. X, § 20. The court concluded that the legislature was acting within constitutional limits when it amended the School Finance Act. SB 07-199's treatment of school districts as the relevant taxing authorities for purposes of waiving the revenue limits was consistent with constitutional provisions governing dual state/local funding and constitutional provisions applicable to public education. Plaintiffs failed to show that SB 07-199 violated the Colorado Constitution. Colo. Const. art. X, § 20(4)(a), did not require a second election, at either the local or state level, for legislation directing how revenue received as a result of a waiver election should be used. The school districts remained the relevant taxing authority for purposes of the locally raised revenue, and a statewide vote was not required to waive a revenue limit at the local level. The local school district elections validly waived the revenue limit at issue in this case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded with directions to enter judgment for defendants.

 

79 of 734 DOCUMENTS


N. Sterling Irrigation Dist. v. Simpson


Case No. 08SA29


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


202 P.3d 1207; 2009 Colo. LEXIS 200



March 2, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at North Sterling Irrigation Dist. v. Simpson, 2009 Colo. LEXIS 233 (Colo., Mar. 2, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, Water Division 1, Case No. 05CW125. Honorable Roger A. Klein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, Colorado's North Sterling Irrigation District (NSID), filed a complaint for declaratory judgment, seeking a determination as to whether defendant state and division engineers could administer NSID's water rights according to a fixed water year. Intervenors, a city, a water district, and well users, also participated in the suit. The District Court, Water Division 1 (Colorado), ruled in favor of the engineers. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: The engineers advised NSID that its diversions under its storage rights would be based on a November 1 to October 31 water year, and that after it obtained one annual fill of its decreed rights, it calls would not be honored until the administrative water year began anew on November 1. The high court held that as NSID's storage decrees were silent as to the administration of the one-fill rule, the engineers had authority under Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 37-92-501, -502 (2008) to determine how to administer Colorado's one-fill mandate; thus, the use of a fixed water year was proper. That NSID had operated under low-point administration for almost 100 years was immaterial, as water rights were not administered according to custom. The fixed water year did not itself interfere with NSID's decreed storage rights, but was merely the administrative mechanism by which the one-fill rule lawfully limited those rights. As NSID's rights had historically been administered consistent with a fixed water year, it suffered no legal injury due to the change in administrative policy. Intervenors' claims of derivative injury to their own water rights were not grounds to contest the engineers' authority.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Huspeni v. El Paso County Sheriff's Dep't (In re Freedom Colo. Info., Inc.)


Case No. 08SA151


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


196 P.3d 892; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1003



November 10, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. El Paso County District Court Case No. 08CV1861. Honorable G. David Miller, Judge.
Huspeni v. El Paso County Sheriff's Dep't. (In re Freedom Colo. Info., Inc.), 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1033 (Colo., Nov. 10, 2008)



DISPOSITION: RULE MADE ABSOLUTE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding, pursuant to Colo. App. R. 21, intervenors, John Doe arrestees, sought review of a decision of the El Paso County District Court (Colorado) allowing plaintiff newspaper to inspect the file documenting a former county sheriff's malfeasance after the sheriff's termination from his employment with defendant county sheriff's office.


OVERVIEW: The John Does, who were wrongfully arrested because of the former sheriff's malfeasance, wanted the criminal records of their arrests sealed. The local newspaper brought suit, under the Colorado Open Records Act (CORA), Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 24-72-202 to 24-72-206 (2008), and the Colorado Criminal Justice Records Act (CCJRA), Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 24-72-301 to 24-72-309 (2008), after it was refused access of the sheriff's internal affairs investigation file pertaining to the discharged sheriff. The district court ordered the release of the entire file, including the names of the John Does. The court held that the district court applied the wrong legal standard in performing the balancing of public and private interests required by the CCJRA, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-308(1)(c) (2008), which addressed sealing records of official action and assigned the role of balancing the public and private interests involved to the district court. Because the file was not a record of official action, public disclosure should have been subject to the discretion of the sheriff, not the district court, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 24-72-304, 24-72-305.


OUTCOME: The court made the rule absolute and ordered the district court to return the matter to the sheriff for an inspection determination that complied with the CCJRA.

 

81 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Barber v. Ritter


Case No. 07SC373


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


196 P.3d 238; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 999



November 3, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 05CA0752.
Barber v. Ritter, 170 P.3d 763, 2007 Colo. App. LEXIS 480 (Colo. Ct. App., 2007)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals held that the transfer of monies from 28 of 31 special funds did not violate Colo. Const. art. XI, § 3 and 4. As to the transfers from the remaining three funds, the appellate court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of respondents, the Governor of Colorado and the Colorado State Treasurer, and remanded the case for further proceedings. The parties petitioned for certiorari review.


OVERVIEW: The instant court concluded that petitioners had taxpayer standing to challenge the fund transfers, the transfers did not violate Amendment 1, Colo. Const. art. X, § 20, and the appellate court erred in reversing the grant of summary judgment in favor of the Governor and the State Treasurer with respect to petitioners' article XI claims. The transfers did not constitute a tax policy change directly causing a net tax revenue gain and did not trigger Amendment 1's voter approval requirement. The transfer of fees from the special cash funds to the state's general fund did not transform those fees into taxes, nor did the transfers result in a net revenue gain within the meaning of Amendment 1. The post-transfer collection of fees by the cash funds did not constitute a new tax or a tax rate increase. The transfers did not create unconstitutional debt in violation of article XI of the Colorado Constitution. Even if the cash funds were public trusts, they were not irrevocable trusts, and the legislature had the authority to amend them to allow for the transfer of monies to the general fund.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded to the appellate court to be returned to the trial court with directions to enter judgment in favor of the Governor and the State Treasurer.

O'Donnell v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


Case No. 07SA296


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


186 P.3d 46; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 607



June 9, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Bryant v. State Farm Mutual Auto. Ins. Co. (In re O'Donnell), 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1673 (Colo., June 9, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 District Court, City and County of Denver. Case No. 06CV9591. Honorable John W. Madden, IV, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurer sued defendant uninsured car owner to recover damages to an insured vehicle. The District Court of the City and County of Denver (Colorado) did not allow an appeal of a default judgment against the owner because although the owner was indigent, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-6-311(1)(a) (2007) and Colo. R. Civ. P. 411(a) required her to post a judgment bond. The owner petitioned for a rule to show cause.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether an indigent defendant had to post a judgment bond as a precondition for appealing an adverse money judgment from the county court to the district court. The owner argued that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-16-103 (2007) allowed her as an indigent party to appeal the default judgment obtained against her by the insurer without first having to post a judgment bond. A county court party found to be indigent and allowed to proceed in forma pauperis was not required to post a judgment bond before appealing to the district court. However, as with appeals from the district court to the court of appeals, the prevailing party in the county court would be able to execute the judgment while the appeal was still pending because the judgment would not have been stayed by a judgment bond. This interpretation of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-6-311 (2007) and Colo. R. Civ. P. 411 properly reflected Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-16-103 (2007)'s requirement that court costs be waived for indigent parties. It was also consistent with the appellate court's wide range of case law in other appellate proceedings waiving appellate fees like judgment bonds for indigent parties.


OUTCOME: The rule to show cause was made absolute. The district court was ordered to allow the owner to proceed with her appeal without first posting a judgment bond.

City of Commerce City v. Enclave West, Inc.


Case No. 07SC445


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


185 P.3d 174; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 573



June 2, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at City of Commerce City v. Enclave West, Inc., 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1567 (Colo., June 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 05CA2686.
Enclave West, Inc. v. City of Commerce City, 2007 Colo. App. LEXIS 667 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 12, 2007)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent corporation sought a business license to operate a sexually-oriented business in petitioner city. The city council denied the business license because the location of the proposed business was within one thousand feet of an occupied single-family residence. The corporation filed a Colo. R. Civ. P. 106(a)(4) appeal. A trial court affirmed the decision. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed. The instant court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that the city counsel was foreclosed from considering and receiving evidence concerning the residence because an ordinance restricted the council to considering only the grounds for denial provided by the city staff. The council found that the corporation's premises was located within one thousand feet of an occupied single-family residence, in violation of Commerce City, Colo., Ordinance art. II, § 21-39(1). The instant court held that, under Commerce City, Colo., Ordinance § 9-456(d), while the council had to consider the city staff's reason for denying the license, it could have also heard other evidence regarding compliance with the zoning and use requirements applicable to the license being sought. The corporation's argument that the council was restricted to the ground relied on by the city staff in denying the license was not supported by the ordinances read as a whole and led to an absurd conclusion. The council did not act arbitrarily and capriciously in refusing to order issuance of the license based on the existence of an occupied single-family residence located within one thousand feet of the proposed sexually-oriented business.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. The case was remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the other issues the corporation raised in its appeal.

Town of Telluride v. San Miguel Valley Corp.


Case No. 07SA101


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


185 P.3d 161; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 575



June 2, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Town of Telluride v. San Miguel Valley Corp., 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1583 (Colo., June 2, 2008)
Costs and fees proceeding at Town of Telluride v. San Miguel Valley Corp., 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 1408 (Colo. Ct. App., Oct. 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, San Miguel County, Case No. 04CV22. Honorable Charles R. Greenacre, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee home rule municipality (the town) sought to condemn 572 acres of nearby property for open space and park purposes. Appellant owners contested the condemnation, relying on Colo. Rev. Stat. § 38-1-101(4)(b) (2007) (subsection 4b). The San Miguel District Court (Colorado) ruled that subsection 4b unconstitutionally denied home rule municipalities their eminent domain power under Colo. Const. art. XX. Appellants challenged the ruling.


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that the town was barred from condemning the property by subsection 4b, which prohibited home rule municipalities from condemning property outside municipal boundaries for parks, recreation, open space, or other similar purposes. Colo. Const. art. XX granted home rule municipalities the power to condemn property for any lawful, public, local, and municipal purpose. Because the Colorado General Assembly could not deny home rule municipalities the eminent domain power conferred to them in the constitution, subsection 4b was unconstitutional with respect to home rule municipalities. The extraterritorial condemnation of property for parks and open space constituted a lawful, public, local, and municipal purpose within the scope of art. XX. Thus, the town's condemnation of the property was lawful. The court rejected the contention that the town's eminent domain power was limited to the purposes enumerated in Colo. Const. art. XX, § 1. It was not necessary to weigh competing state and local concerns implicated by the exercise of that authority to determine whether the authority could be preempted by the legislature when the action was authorized by the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Colo. Educ. Ass'n v. Rutt


Case No. 06SC559


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


184 P.3d 65; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 495; 184 L.R.R.M. 2411



May 19, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Colo. Educ. Ass'n v. Rutt, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 1112 (Colo., May 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals. Case No. 05CA1718.
Rutt v. Poudre Educ. Ass'n, 151 P.3d 585, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 1173 (Colo. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens filed a complaint with the Colorado Secretary of State's office alleging that respondent labor unions illegally contributed to the campaign of a candidate for state senate. An administrative law judge (ALJ) dismissed the citizens' claims. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed, and the unions sought review.


OVERVIEW: The unions organized volunteer events for their members to distribute campaign literature in support of the candidate in question. The court held that in pursuing such activities, the unions did not make prohibited campaign expenditures in violation of Colo. Const. art. XXVIII, § 3(4)(a) because the payment of union staff salaries for time spent organizing these events fell within the membership communication exception of Colo. Const. art. Const. art. XXVIII, § 3(4)(a) when they organized these events for members. The court concluded that allowing the same payments to be protected from regulation as expenditures yet prohibited as contributions would be contrary to the will of the electorate and would result in an unreasonable application of Article XXVIII. Thus, the membership communication exception had to be extended to the definition of a "contribution."


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the decision of the intermediate appellate court, to which the case was remanded with directions to return it to the ALJ for further proceedings on the remaining issue of attorney fees.

Blake v. King (In re Title, Ballot Title, & Submission Clause 2007-2008 # 62)


Case No. 08SA90


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


184 P.3d 52; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 455



May 16, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to § 1-40-107(2), C.R.S. (2007). Appeal from the Ballot Title Setting Board.



DISPOSITION: ACTION AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner registered elector sought review of respondent Title Board's action in fixing the title, ballot title, and submission clause for a proposed ballot initiative to amend the Colorado Constitution to establish standards and procedures to discharge or suspend employees.


OVERVIEW: On review, the elector contended the ballot initiative was unconstitutional because it violated the single-subject requirement of the Colorado Constitution; the elector also argued that the titles of the initiative were misleading and did not correctly and fairly express the initiative's true intent and meaning. The supreme court disagreed and held that the initiative contained only one subject in compliance with Colo. Const. art. V, § 1(5.5): the establishment of a just cause requirement for discharging or suspending an employee. All of the sections of the measure related to this subject and the necessary association existed among the provisions of the initiative, in that each provision related to creating, implementing, or enforcing a just cause standard in the employment setting for certain employees. Further, the titles set by the Title Board clearly expressed the subject of the initiative and contained no catch phrases. Amendments made to the initiative in response to comments from legislative staff did not violate the requirement that substantial amendments had to be resubmitted to the directors of the Legislative Council and the Office of Legislative Legal Services.


OUTCOME: The Title Board's action was affirmed.

 

87 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thompson


Case No. 07SA339


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


181 P.3d 1143; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 349



April 7, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Thompson, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 443 (Colo., Apr. 28, 2008)
Decision reached on appeal by People v. Thompson, 2017 Colo. App. LEXIS 538, 2017 COA 56 (May 4, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. District Court, Arapahoe County. Case No. 07CR1483. Honorable J. Mark Hannen, Judge.
People v. Thompson, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 362 (Colo., Apr. 7, 2008)



DISPOSITION: RULE TO SHOW CAUSE MADE ABSOLUTE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A rule to show cause was issued to determine whether the District Court, Arapahoe County (Colorado), erred in redacting portions of defendant's grand jury indictment which contained extensive factual allegations, prior to making the indictment open for public inspection in response to a newspaper's motion to unseal the factual allegations and identities of any deceased victims contained in the indictment.


OVERVIEW: Before considering the newspaper's constitutional claims, the appellate court first examined the statutory provisions concerning public access to court documents. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-304(4) required that identifying information of sexual assault victims be deleted from records of official actions as well as other criminal justice records prior to their disclosure to the public. Because a grand jury indictment was a record of official action, the trial court could not redact any factual allegations contained in the indictment unless required otherwise by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-304(4)(a) or by other law. Although defendant's indictment did not bear the notation "sexual assault," it was sufficiently related to a possible sexual assault for purposes of the victim protection mandated by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-304(4). Thus, the Colorado Criminal Justice Records Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-301 et seq. (2007), required that the entire indictment be made available for public inspection, subject to the deletion of identifying information of any alleged sexual assault victims. The indictment's allegations were not covered by grand jury secrecy as they were not grand jury evidence.


OUTCOME: The rule to show cause was made absolute. The case was remanded to the trial court with direction to delete from the indictment identifying information of any alleged sexual assault victims and to make the indictment, subject to such deletion, open for public inspection.

 

88 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Close v. People


Case No. 06SC520


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


180 P.3d 1015; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 284



March 24, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Close v. People, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 311 (Colo., Mar. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals. Case No. 03CA1833.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted of six counts of second-degree assault under Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 18-3-203(1)(b) (1986), among other crimes, and was sentenced to 60 years' imprisonment. Petitioner sought review of a decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals affirming the trial court's disposition of his pro se motions under Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(a) and (c) as time-barred under Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 16-5-402(1).


OVERVIEW: After petitioner's convictions, the case of People v. Nguyen was decided. Nguyen held that equal protection was violated by treating attempted second-degree assault under Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 18-3-203(1)(b) (1986) as an automatic crime-of-violence for sentencing purposes, because attempted first-degree assault was not an automatic crime-of-violence. Application of Nguyen to petitioner's case would reduce his sentence from 60 years to 30 years on the attempted assault charges; however, his public defender did not argue Nguyen. The court found that petitioner had alleged facts which, if true, would establish justifiable excuse or excusable neglect under Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 16-5-402(2)(d), meriting a hearing. The allegation that counsel failed to inform the trial court of the constitutional limitations of petitioner's sentence, which in turn resulted in a sentence twice the constitutionally permissible level, was within the standard for measuring ineffective assistance of counsel under Strickland. The public defender could not argue its own office's ineffective assistance due to a conflict of interest, so appointment of other counsel was required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals with directions that it return the case to the trial court to appoint conflict-free counsel for petitioner and hold a hearing on the issue of whether petitioner was entitled to relief from the post-conviction time bar based on justifiable excuse or excusable neglect under Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 16-5-402(2)(d).

 

89 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Simpson v. Cotton Creek Circles, LLC (In re Rules Governing New Withdrawals of Ground Water)


Case No. 07SA42


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


181 P.3d 252; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 283



March 24, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Simpson v. Cotton Creek Circles, LLC (In re Rules Governing New Withdrawals of Ground Water), 2008 Colo. LEXIS 285 (Colo., Mar. 24, 2008)
Related proceeding at In re Rules Governing New Withdrawals of Ground Water v. Appellees: Rio Grande Water Conservation Dist., 2009 Colo. LEXIS 997 (Colo., Oct. 19, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, Water Division 3, Case No. 04CW24. Honorable O. John Kuenhold, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant opponent challenged the Rules Governing New Withdrawals of Ground Water in Water Division Three Affecting the Rate or Direction of Movement of Water in the Confined Aquifer System adopted by appellee state engineer, alleging that they were unconstitutional and contrary to statute. The District Court, Water Division 3 (Colorado), upheld the rules. The opponent challenged the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The artesian pressure requirements were valid where new or increased withdrawals from the confined aquifer system would have caused material injury to existing water rights. Thus, the requirements did not violate the Colo. Const. art. XVI, § 6, right to divert unappropriated waters because the waters were not unappropriated. The one-for-one replacement requirement did not violate the constitutional right to divert unappropriated waters for similar reasons. The rules related to non-irrigated native vegetation were valid as they mirrored Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 37-90-137(12)(a) and 37-92-305(6)(c) (2007) and former Colo. Rev. Stat. § 37-90-137(12)(b)(I) (repealed 2004). The finding of injury for new withdrawal was permissible as the rules left open the possibility that an applicant would be permitted to withdraw new water. The rules did not violate statutory authority by regulating only new water uses as there was no authority to regulate existing uses. The rules did not violate equal protection as there was a rational basis for treating the groups at issue differently. Finally, the state engineer was not required to follow the state Administrative Procedure Act in adopting the rules.


OUTCOME: The judgment approving the Rules Governing New Withdrawals of Ground Water in Water Division Three Affecting the Rate or Direction of Movement of Water in the Confined Aquifer System as promulgated was affirmed.

 

90 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Developmental Pathways v. Ritter


Case No. 07SA181


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


178 P.3d 524; 2008 Colo. LEXIS 169



February 25, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Developmental Pathways v. Ritter, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 174 (Colo., Feb. 25, 2008)
Rehearing denied by Developmental Pathways v. Ritter, 2008 Colo. LEXIS 306 (Colo., Mar. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 07CV1353. Honorable Christina M. Habas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: ORDER REVERSED AND PRELIMINARY INJUNCTION VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonprofit entities and individuals sued defendant governor, alleging that the gift ban provisions of Amendment 41, Colo. Const. art. XXIX, § 1 et seq., were unconstitutional. The District Court for the City and County of Denver (Colorado) found that the bans impermissibly chilled plaintiffs' First Amendment rights and preliminarily enjoined enforcement of them. The governor appealed the injunction and review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The governor was a proper party defendant where the Ethics Commission, which was responsible for implementing the Amendment, had not been appointed at the time the case was filed. The governor, as the state's chief executive officer, was thus the proper defendant. The Amendment was self-executing because even though it did not contain a self-executing clause, the language of the Amendment, the Colorado Legislative Council's analysis, and the nature of initiative amendments as a whole suggested that the voters wanted to minimize legislative involvement. Nonetheless, the First Amendment as applied challenge was not ripe for review because the Commission was not in existence and had not yet developed the means to enforce the gift bans. The relief plaintiffs sought, a declaration that the bans were unconstitutional and leaving only the provisions creating the Commission, was only available in a successful facial challenge. Since there was no actual application of the Amendment or a reasonable possibility of enforcement or threat of enforcement, plaintiffs' claims were not real or immediate. Thus, the district court did not have jurisdiction to grant the preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The denial of the motion to dismiss on the proper party and self-execution issues was affirmed. The order granting a preliminary injunction was reversed. The district court was directed to vacate the preliminary injunction.

 

91 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Juhl v. People


Case No. 06SC637


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


172 P.3d 896; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 1137



December 17, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Juhl v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 1155 (Colo., Dec. 17, 2007)
Subsequent civil proceeding at Lincoln Gen. Ins. Co. v. Bailey, 224 P.3d 336, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 811 (Colo. Ct. App., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 05CA0180.
People v. Juhl, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 1370 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 17, 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to review the trial court's statutory authority to impose consecutive rather than concurrent sentences for defendant's convictions for first degree assault and vehicular assault. Both charges, to which defendant pled guilty, arose out of his head-on collision with the victim's truck after a high-speed chase in which defendant attempted to elude the police.


OVERVIEW: As a sentencing statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-408(3) (2007) could be applied equally to a conviction that arose from a trial or a conviction that arose from the entry and acceptance of a guilty plea. Defendant's act of colliding head-on with a victim's truck while he was engaged in a high-speed chase to elude the police was the basis of both the first degree assault and vehicular assault charges. It was that single collision that resulted in serious bodily injury to the victim. The act that was the basis of defendant's first degree assault conviction could not be logically separated from the act that formed the basis of the vehicular assault conviction. Both convictions were supported by evidence of the collision with the victim's truck and the circumstances under which that collision occurred. Because defendant's assault convictions were supported by identical evidence, the trial court was mandated by statute to impose concurrent rather than consecutive sentences for the first degree assault and vehicular assault convictions.


OUTCOME: Because the trial court did not have the statutory authority to impose consecutive sentences, the court reversed and returned the decision to the court of appeals with directions to remand to the trial court for amendment of the mittimus to reflect that the three-year conviction for vehicular assault was to run concurrent with the 15-year conviction for first degree assault.
