93 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Crow v. Penrose-St. Francis Healthcare Sys.


Case No. 06SA323


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


169 P.3d 158; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 912



October 15, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Crow v. Penrose St.Francis Healthcare Sys., 2010 Colo. App. LEXIS 1127 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 12, 2010)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by Crow v. Penrose-St. Francis Healthcare Sys., 262 P.3d 991, 2011 Colo. App. LEXIS 1397 (Colo. Ct. App., 2011)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by Crow v. Penrose-St. Francis Healthcare Sys., 2012 Colo. App. LEXIS 410 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 El Paso County, Case No. 06CV1649. Honorable Thomas Kelley Kane, Judge.



DISPOSITION: RULE MADE ABSOLUTE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff physician filed a common law action against defendant hospital relating to its peer review process. The El Paso County District Court (Colorado) refused to dismiss the physician's case, and a rule to show cause was issued.


OVERVIEW: The physician's hospital staff privileges were suspended due to allegations that he failed to properly treat patients. Before the peer review process was completed, the physician filed an action against the hospital. The trial court allowed the physician's claim to proceed, and the hospital objected. The supreme court then issued a rule to show cause. In making the rule absolute, the supreme court held that, pursuant to the Colorado Professional Review Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 12-36.5-101 to -203 (2007), the physician was required to exhaust all peer review administrative remedies before seeking relief in the trial court. This exhaustion requirement under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 12-36.5-106(7), (8) applied, even though the physician here was seeking money damages for common law claims arising out of the process. Because no final decision had been rendered in this case, the physician had not exhausted his available administrative remedies. The physician's argument relating to a prompt hearing was meritless since his conduct caused several delays. Finally, an issue related to immunity was not reached since the case was not ripe.


OUTCOME: The rule to show cause was made absolute. The district court was ordered to grant the hospital's motion to dismiss.

 

94 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Brodeur v. Am. Home Assur. Co.


Case No. 06SC499


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


169 P.3d 139; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 902



October 9, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA1710.
Brodeur v. Am. Home Assur. Co., 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 267 (Colo. Ct. App., Mar. 2, 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wife challenged a decision from the Colorado Court of Appeals, which affirmed a summary judgment in favor of respondents, an insurer and an adjuster, in a claim alleging bad faith, breach of fiduciary duty, fraud, and a violation of the Colorado Consumer Protection Act (CCPA), Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 6-1-101 to -1120 (2007).


OVERVIEW: In a workers' compensation claim, the carrier denied coverage for back surgery and drugs prescribed by a specialist. During this dispute, the worker died from unrelated causes. Thereafter, his wife filed the instant action against the carrier. Judgment was entered in favor of the carrier, and this appeal followed. In affirming, the supreme court held that bad faith tort claims accrued independently of a workers' compensation proceeding; therefore, the law of the case doctrine did not apply. The bad faith tort claims were barred by limits of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-80-102(1)(a) (2007); there were no facts to support equitable tolling of these claims. Moreover, there was no fiduciary or quasi-fiduciary relationship between a workers' compensation insurer and the insured. The denial of treatment was a statement of a legal opinion, not a misrepresentation of fact required to support a fraud claim, and no exceptions applied. Finally, the public nature of the workers' compensation program did not satisfy per se the public impact requirement for a CCPA claim, nor did the facts in the record support a finding that the public impact element had been satisfied in this particular case.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed. This included the decision to remand the case for further proceedings, including discovery.

 

95 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hinojos-Mendoza v. People


Case No. 05SC881


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


169 P.3d 662; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 825



September 10, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Hinojos Mendoza v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 1036 (Colo., Nov. 5, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hinojos-Mendoza v. Colorado, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4963 (U.S., June 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA0645.
People v. Hinojos-Mendoza, 140 P.3d 30, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1206 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Colorado Court of Appeals, which determined that a laboratory report under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5) (2006) was admissible in a drug case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with a drug offense, and the State sought to introduce a laboratory report under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5) (2006). The report was admitted over defendant's objection. The court of appeals found that the report was nontestimonial in nature under Crawford, but it refused to hear defendant's constitutional challenges. It found that the report was admissible, and this appeal followed. On review, the supreme court determined that the report was testimonial in nature under the Sixth Amendment and Colo. Const. art. II, § 16 because it was prepared in anticipation prosecution of a crime. However, the procedural notice requirements of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5) (2006) did not deny the right of confrontation; therefore, it was facially constitutional. Moreover, § 6-3-309(5) was constitutionally applied in this case; defense counsel was able to waive the right to confront the technician who prepared the report by not complying with the procedural requirements of § 16-3-309(5). The right to confrontation fell within the class of rights that could have been waived by defense counsel through strategic decisions.


OUTCOME: The portion of the decision holding that the laboratory report was nontestimonial was reversed. The rest of the decision from the court of appeals was affirmed, but on other grounds.

 

96 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Coleman v. People


Case No. 06SC155


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


169 P.3d 659; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 821



September 10, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Coleman v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 1037 (Colo., Nov. 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA1648.
People v. Coleman, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 82 (Colo. Ct. App., Jan. 26, 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals (Colorado) affirmed defendant's simple possession conviction, concluding that the admission of the report of a State criminalistics laboratory, identifying the substance found near defendant at the time of his arrest as cocaine, did not violate defendant's right to confront and cross-examine the witnesses against him. The court granted certiorari to review the decision.


OVERVIEW: Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5) (2006) required that, prior to the introduction of a laboratory report at trial, a party had to give timely notice in order to require the presence at trial of the lab technician who prepared the report. By failing to comply with Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16- 3-309(5) (2006), defense counsel could waive the right to the in-court testimony of the technician who prepared the report on defendant's behalf. The right to confrontation was a right that could be waived by a defendant's attorney and did not need to be waived personally by the defendant. The failure of defendant's attorney to timely notify the prosecution that defendant desired the in-court testimony of the technician waived his right to confront the technician.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' holding that the trial court did not err in admitting the lab report identifying cocaine without the in-court testimony of the technician who prepared the report.

 

97 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Bostelman v. People


Case No. 06SC70


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


162 P.3d 686; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 535



June 25, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Modified and rehearing denied by Bostelman v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 666 (Colo., July 30, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 04CAl223.
People v. Bostelman, 141 P.3d 891, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 2016 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile appealed a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals affirming the district court's decision to deny the juvenile's motion under Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) and holding that the prosecution had the discretionary authority to charge the juvenile with second-degree burglary and theft as an adult under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-2-517(1)(a)(IV) (2005) because he was 14 years old when the charges were filed.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was 13 years old when he committed the burglary, and he was 14 years old when he was charged with the crime. On appeal, the court held that the plain language of § 19-2-517(1)(a)(IV), as it existed when the juvenile was charged, was ambiguous as to whether a juvenile had to be 14 when the charged delinquent act occurred or when the prosecution filed the charge. Given the stated purpose of the juvenile justice code and the legislature's intent in enacting the Children's Code, the court held that a juvenile had to have been 14 years old at the time the delinquent act occurred in order for the prosecution to file directly under § 19-2-517(1)(a)(IV). Because the juvenile was 13 when the alleged burglary and thefts took place, the prosecution did not have the authority under § 19-2-517(1)(a)(IV) to charge him as an adult in district court, and the district court did not have the authority to sentence him as an adult for that offense.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the appellate court with directions to return it to the district court for further proceedings.

 

98 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Arias


Case No. 07SA17


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


159 P.3d 134; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 455



June 4, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court, Summit County, Case No. 06CR139. Honorable W. Terry Ruckriegle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: ORDER AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People sought review of an order from the District Court, Summit County (Colorado), which suppressed evidence seized from defendant's truck following a traffic stop. The People argued that an air freshener hanging from the rearview mirror of the truck justified the stop under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-4-201(4) (2006). Alternatively, the People argued that the stop was justified under the fellow officer rule.


OVERVIEW: Believing defendant was involved in drug activity, a Drug Task Force agent contacted police to request that a patrol officer conduct a traffic stop and seek permission to search defendant's truck. The agent did not inform dispatch about earlier observations that he believed established a basis to stop the truck. Instead, he requested that the officer develop an independent basis for a stop. On appeal, the court held that observing an item hanging from a rearview mirror was not automatically a basis for a traffic stop under § 42-4-201(4); instead, an officer had to reasonably believe the item actually obstructed the driver's vision through the windshield. The court deferred to the trial court's findings in the instant case that the evidence failed to show that the officer reasonably believed that defendant's vision through the windshield was obstructed by the air freshener at the time of the stop. The court rejected the People's alternative argument, holding that the fellow officer rule did not apply. The court reasoned that the agent specifically requested that a patrol officer not rely on existing information and instead develop an independent basis to stop defendant's truck.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

 

99 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Flippo


Case No. 05SC794


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


159 P.3d 100; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 430



May 21, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by People v. Flippo, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 517 (Colo., June 11, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals, Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA1831.
People v. Flippo, 134 P.3d 436, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1349 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant attempted to introduce expert testimony at trial that, due to his intellectual disability, he was highly suggestible under interrogation. The trial court excluded proposed expert and lay testimony because it did not comply with Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-107(3)(b). The Colorado Court of Appeals disagreed, held that the statute did not apply, and ordered a new trial. A petition for certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted at trial of felony sexual assault. The evidence presented at trial included statements made by him directly to the police during a videotaped interrogation. Before trial, he unsuccessfully challenged the admissibility of those statements as the product of an involuntary confession. The substance of the proposed expert testimony, supported by the literature, was that people with intellectual disabilities are more suggestible in a police interview than a person without those disabilities and that they will agree with statements made by the police, even if those statements are not true. During the trial, defendant also unsuccessfully attempted to introduce lay opinion testimony regarding his suggestibility. The court held that based on the language of the statute, "mental condition," as used in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-107(3)(b), included intellectual disabilities.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' conclusion, that exclusion of defendant's expert testimony at trial was error, was reversed. On remand, the court of appeals was instructed to address defendant's remaining issues including whether defendant's lay opinion testimony was wrongfully excluded and, if so, whether such exclusion was error.

Dunlap v. People


Case No. 04SA218


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


173 P.3d 1054; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 389



May 14, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified July 2, 2007.
Modified and rehearing denied by Dunlap v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 593 (Colo., July 2, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Dunlap v. Colorado, 552 U.S. 1105, 128 S. Ct. 882, 169 L. Ed. 2d 740, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 241 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Arapahoe County District Court, Case No. 93CR2071. Honorable John P. Leopold, Judge.
People v. Dunlap, 975 P.2d 723, 1999 Colo. LEXIS 203 (Colo., 1999)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Arapahoe County District Court (Colorado) of, inter alia, four counts of first-degree murder and received the death penalty. After his motion for postconviction relief was denied, defendant appealed. The State cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the postconviction court erred by determining that defense counsel performed deficiently by deciding not to run a mental health mitigation case without first obtaining an independent, private mental health evaluation. The supreme court agreed. Presenting a mental health mitigation case would have been risky at best given the substantial amount of damaging evidence from the mental health institute that the State had to rebut defendant's claims. The evidence that would have been put forth by the State to rebut a mental health defense not only involved defendant's statements about the crime and victims, but also included evidence of defendant's misbehavior at the institute and the near unanimous view of the staff and doctors that defendant was malingering his symptoms. The postconviction court found the institute witnesses to be credible, and found that their testimony would have undermined the mitigating value of a mental health defense. Such evidence would not have been cumulative of anything else the jury had heard. Even if trial counsel had acted deficiently, the evidence before the jury demonstrated the lack of actual prejudice in the case.


OUTCOME: The holding that trial counsel performed deficiently was reversed. The judgment was otherwise affirmed and the case was remanded to set a date for imposition of the death sentence.

 

101 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Montour


Case No. 02SA365


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


157 P.3d 489; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 348



April 23, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Rehearing denied by People v. Montour, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 400 (Colo., May 14, 2007)
Related proceeding at Autobee v. Colo. Dep't of Corr., 2008 Colo. App. LEXIS 818 (Colo. Ct. App., May 15, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Stay denied by, As moot Montour v. Clements, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 126369 (D. Colo., Oct. 31, 2011)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied, Motion granted by Montour v. Clements, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 9442 (10th Cir. Colo., May 9, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Courts. Lincoln County District Court, Case No. 02CR95. Douglas County District Court, Case No. 02CR782. Honorable Paul A. King, Judge.



DISPOSITION: SENTENCE REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED FOR NEW SENTENCING HEARING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to the first-degree murder of a correctional officer at a correctional facility, where he was serving a life sentence without the possibility of parole for the first-degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to death by the Douglas County District Court (Colorado) under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-1201(1)(a) (2006) which stated that a capital defendant waived his right to a jury trial on sentencing facts when he pled guilty.


OVERVIEW: Prior to entering his guilty plea, defendant repeatedly stated that he understood that by pleading guilty he was waiving his right to have a jury determine his sentence under § 18-1.3-1201(1)(a). The court held that because under Colorado law defendant was not eligible for the death penalty until all aggravating factors were weighed against all mitigating factors, the presence of one Blakely-exempt aggravating factor standing alone did not make defendant eligible for the death penalty. The court also held that § 18-1.3-1201(1)(a) violated the Sixth Amendment because it mandated that the court sentence defendant who had pled guilty to a class 1 felony. Under the terms of the statute, the court did not need to procure an additional knowing, voluntary, and intelligent waiver of this Sixth Amendment right as was constitutionally required, but instead had to inform the defendant that by pleading guilty he automatically waived his right to a jury trial on sentencing facts. The court found that defendant's waiver could not have been knowing, voluntary, and intelligent because it was infected by the same constitutional infirmity as the statute--it was inextricably linked to his plea.


OUTCOME: The death sentence was reversed and the case was remanded to the district court. On remand, the district court was ordered to set a new sentencing hearing before a newly impaneled jury unless defendant waived his right to jury sentencing. To be valid, defendant's waiver of his Sixth Amendment right would have to be knowing, voluntary, and intelligent, and not linked to his guilty plea.

 

102 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Silva v. People


Case No. 05SC519


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


156 P.3d 1164; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 350



April 23, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by SILVA, CHRISTOPHER v. COLORADO, 127 S. Ct. 3023, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 8460 (U.S., June 25, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA0661.
People v. Silva, 131 P.3d 1082, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 843 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pro se appellant state inmate sought judicial review of a holding by the Colorado Court of Appeals that only one of his claims brought in a second Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) motion may have had justifiable excuse for late filing of the motion outside the statutory time limit set in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-5-402(1) (2006). Certiorari was granted to address the right to postconviction counsel and the standard for evaluating such counsel.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that the inmate's fifth claim concerning postconviction counsel could have been justifiably late due to the appeal of the first postconviction motion. Therefore, the trial court should have held further proceedings on justifiable excuse and made separate findings of fact and law. The court of appeals separated the inmate's fifth claim into two claims, a claim for constitutionally ineffective assistance of counsel for failing to call certain witnesses and a claim for conflict of interest concerning the public defender's office. It incorrectly held that further proceedings were not needed on the ineffective assistance of counsel claim because there was no constitutional or statutory violation requiring relief under Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c)(2)(I) as there was no constitutional or statutory right to postconviction counsel. The court of appeals held that the inmate's conflict of interest claim required further proceedings and remanded the case back to the trial court as to that claim alone. However, in Colorado, there was a limited statutory right to postconviction counsel, and the proper standard to be applied was the Strickland test.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals was reversed as to its holding that the inmate could not state an ineffective assistance of postconviction counsel claim, and the court of appeals was affirmed as to its ruling on the inmate's conflict of interest claim concerning postconviction counsel requiring further proceedings.

 

103 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wartena


Case No. 06SA232


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


156 P.3d 469; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 298



April 16, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21, Jefferson County District Court, Case No. 05CR2989. Honorable Christopher Munch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: RULE MADE ABSOLUTE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was accused of various felonies stemming from a high-speed pursuit, including extreme indifference murder and attempted murder after deliberation. In response to defendant's request to videotape the consumptive DNA testing procedures on a shotgun handle, the Jefferson County District Court ruled that it would suppress the test results unless the prosecution paid part of the costs of a defense expert. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court adopted Standard 3.4(e) of the Criminal Justice Section Standards on DNA Evidence, which noted that courts should consider procedures such as videotaping that allowed for independent evaluation of consumptive testing. However, the standard was not applied as the trial court did not assert its Color. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309 authority over the evidence, address whether it destruction of the evidence would be permitted, or outline the appropriate procedures to permit independent analysis as a condition of consumptive testing. It also did not make findings about the appropriateness of videotaping as a condition of permitting consumptive testing or make findings and conclusions about the objections to videotaping and whether videotaping should have been a part of the testing procedures in this case. Instead, the trial court focused prematurely on sanctions for unreasonable conduct in failing to preserve evidence. Thus, it abused its discretion when it committed to ordering suppression of DNA results for tests that had not yet been conducted if the testing agency refused to permit videotaping and the prosecution refused to pay the expense of a defense expert.


OUTCOME: The ruling was vacated. The rule was made absolute.

 

104 of 734 DOCUMENTS


City of Colo. Springs v. Powell


Case No. 05SC743, Case No. 05SC746


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


156 P.3d 461; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 296



April 9, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at City of Longmont v. Henry-Hobbs, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 1029 (Colo., Apr. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA2030. Certiorari to the Colorado. Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA2187.
Henry-Hobbs v. City of Longmont, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1476 (Colo. Ct. App., Sept. 8, 2005)
Powell v. City of Colo. Springs, 131 P.3d 1129, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1448 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent parents sued petitioner municipalities after their children drowned in ditches. The municipalities moved to dismiss on the ground that a bill amending the exemption from liability in the Colorado Governmental Immunity Act, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-10-101 et seq. (2001), for sanitation facilities applied retrospectively. Certiorari was granted to review the Colorado Court of Appeals' decision that the legislation operated retrospectively.


OVERVIEW: In response to decisions concluding that the municipalities were subject to suit because the ditches constituted sanitation facilities, the General Assembly adopted Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-10-103(5.5) (2003) to exclude drainage ditches from the definition of public sanitation facility. The municipalities argued that the newly-adopted legislation applied retroactively to bestow immunity on them. The lower court correctly concluded that the municipalities had not overcome the presumption of prospectivity that had to be employed in determining whether the General Assembly intended the legislative amendment to apply retroactively to these cases. Neither the language of the statute, its legislative history, nor the definition of the term in use prior to the adoption of the legislation supported the municipalities' contention that the General Assembly clearly intended the amendments to take retroactive effect. Accordingly, the lower court properly held that § 24-10-103(5.5) operated only prospectively and that the definitions supplied in the case law had to be used to resolve the two pending cases.


OUTCOME: The lower court's conclusion that the amended legislation operated only retrospectively and that the definitions supplied in case law had to be used to resolve the pending cases was affirmed.

 

105 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Flakes v. People


Case No. 05SC593


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


153 P.3d 427; 2007 Colo. LEXIS 141



February 26, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified March 19, 2007.
Modified and rehearing denied by Flakes v. People, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 213 (Colo., Mar. 19, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals, Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA1156.
People v. Flakes, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1065 (Colo. Ct. App., June 30, 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile was charged as an adult with two counts of first degree murder. On writ of certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals, the supreme court granted certiorari to consider the constitutionality of the direct file statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-2-517 (2006). Petitioner asserted that the sentencing provisions of the direct file statute required an adult sentence and violated certain constitutional doctrines.


OVERVIEW: The direct file statute authorized the prosecutor to charge petitioner as an adult by filing certain enumerated charges directly in the district court. The parties agreed that even though the direct file statute did not expressly authorize direct filing of charges not enumerated by the direct file statute, unenumerated charges could nonetheless be brought together with enumerated charges in a single prosecution based on ancillary jurisdiction. They disagreed, however, as to whether the statute mandated an adult sentence when petitioner was acquitted of the enumerated offenses but convicted of the unenumerated offenses. The supreme court held that the district court had the discretion to sentence petitioner as an adult or as a juvenile when found guilty of unenumerated charges. The supreme court also held that the direct file statute was constitutional. However, because in petitioner's case it was unclear whether the district court exercised the discretion to decide whether to sentence petitioner as a juvenile before sentencing him as an adult, and because the district court did not make findings before imposing an adult sentence, the case had to be remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals upholding petitioner's sentences and remanded with directions for the district court to conduct a new sentencing hearing and to make adequate findings before imposing an adult sentence on petitioner.

 

106 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Candelaria v. People


Case No. 04SC657


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


148 P.3d 178; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 981



December 18, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA2467.
People v. Candelaria, 107 P.3d 1080, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 1021 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both the People and defendant sought certiorari review of a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals, which vacated the judgments of conviction for conspiring to commit, attempting to commit, and committing extreme indifference murder after finding that the elements of extreme indifference murder and murder after deliberation under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-3-102 were logically inconsistent.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed to consider whether the verdicts were indeed inconsistent. On review, the court reversed the part of the judgment finding the verdicts inconsistent. Knowingly causing the death of another, as a matter of law, was not inconsistent with doing so intentionally and after deliberation under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-503(3). Whether acting intentionally or knowingly under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-501, defendant might do so in a manner demonstrating extreme indifference. Whether or not defendant intended the death of a particular individual, it was the nature and circumstances of the killing act by which he sought to accomplish his purpose that elevated his culpability, rather than his intent or the result of his conduct. Because Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-3-102(1)(d) restricted the required killing act to one evidencing an attitude of universal malice, rather than altering the mental state required for commission of the crime, the court of appeals erred in concluding that findings of extreme indifference and specific intent with regard to a particular individual were logically inconsistent. Further, the evidence was sufficient to permit a finding for both elements.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals in part as to the inconsistent verdicts and remanded for the court of appeals to direct the correction of the mittimus.

 

107 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shell


Case No. 04SA93


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


148 P.3d 162; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 980



December 18, 2006, Decided


NOTICE:

 [**1]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shell v. Colo., 2007 U.S. LEXIS 6150 (U.S., May 29, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner the People of the State of Colorado sought to hold respondent individual in contempt for violating a court order and Colorado law prohibiting the unauthorized practice of law. The presiding disciplinary judge, acting as a hearing master, recommended that the court hold the individual in contempt, fine her $6000, and assess additional legal costs and attorney's fees. The individual appealed the recommendations.


OVERVIEW: The hearing master did not clearly err in finding that the individual had engaged in the unauthorized practice of law where she had written letters to an attorney of two mothers in dependent and neglected child cases instructing him to take specific legal measures and once those instructions were ignored, had prepared legal documents for the mothers to file pro se. The individual had also filed a federal action on behalf of herself and one of the children. Colorado's ban on the unauthorized practice of law was not void for vagueness as Colo. R. Civ. P. 201.3(2)(b) unambiguously delineated the activities that were included in the practice of law and the individual understood what the ban prohibited. The ban did not violate free speech as the individual's speech was not impacted. The ban did not violate her right to petition the government as the freedom did not extend to filing a lawsuit on behalf of another. The court had the power to sanction the individual for her actions in federal court. The individual was not entitled to a jury trial as Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-10-101 did not apply to contempt charges and the fine was petty for purposes of the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The individual was held in contempt and fined $6000. The court did not assess any additional amount for costs and attorneys' fees. The court order enjoining the individual against practicing law without a license in Colorado remained in effect.

City of Florence v. Pepper


Case No. 05SC812


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


145 P.3d 654; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 796



October 2, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA0457.
Pepper v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office, 131 P.3d 1137, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 1514 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED ON OTHER GROUNDS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a city, the Industrial Claim Appeals Office (ICAO), and the city's insurer, sought certiorari review of a decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals which reversed decisions of an administrative law judge (ALJ) and the ICAO holding that respondent volunteer reserve police officer's claim for damages was barred by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-40-202(1)(a)(I)(A) (2001).


OVERVIEW: The officer sued petitioners, seeking damages for injuries he allegedly sustained from a shooting incident that occurred while he was acting in his capacity as an officer. The ALJ and the ICAO held that his claim was barred by § 8-40-202(1)(a)(I)(A) because the city did not opt to include volunteer reserve police officers within the meaning of "employee." On appeal of the appellate court's decision, the court held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-901(3)(1)(IV.5) (1996) effectively repealed that portion of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-40-202(1)(a)(I)(A) that left workers' compensation coverage of volunteer reserve police officers to the discretion of the county, town, city, or municipality. The officer was entitled to workers' compensation coverage under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-901(3)(1)(IV.5) because the parties stipulated that the officer was sworn in as a volunteer reserve police officer by the city on July 26, 2004 and that he was performing his duties as an officer at the time of the incident on September 28, 2001.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing the decisions of the ALJ and the ICAO was affirmed.

Danielson v. Dennis


Case No. 06SA174


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


139 P.3d 688; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 621



July 31, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal Pursuant to § 1-1-113(3), C.R.S. (2005) District Court, City and County of Denver Case No. 06CV954. Honorable Michael A. Martinez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parolees sought to register to vote. Defendant Colorado Secretary of State refused to allow the parolees to register to vote or vote. The parolees appealed pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. 1-1-113(3) (2005).


OVERVIEW: The parolees challenged the constitutionality of Colo. Rev. Stat. 1-2-103(4) (2005), alleging that a conflict existed with Colo. Const. art. VII, § 10. According to this constitutional provision, rights of citizenship were required to be restored to persons who were qualified electors prior to their imprisonment and who served their full term of imprisonment. The Colorado General Assembly enacted the statute to prevent a person who had been convicted of a felony and was serving a sentence of parole from voting or registering to vote. The supreme court explained that a person who was serving a sentence of parole had not served a full term of imprisonment within the meaning of Colo. Const. art. VII, § 10. Prison confinement, and the type of conditional release from confinement outside of prison walls that mandatory parole entailed, were separate components of the penalty the General Assembly had prescribed for certain crimes. The parolees failed to show that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the district court's judgment.

 

110 of 734 DOCUMENTS



City of Golden v. Parker


Case No. 05SC282


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


138 P.3d 285; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 558



June 26, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals, Case No. 03CA1946.
Parker v. City of Golden, 119 P.3d 557, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 344 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following an amendment to petitioner City's home rule city charter, respondent citizen filed a complaint challenging petitioner City's continuing obligations under economic incentive agreements it entered into with petitioner real estate developers. The trial court determined that the developers had vested rights under the agreements. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed. The developers and the City appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the developers and the City contended, inter alia, that the home rule city charter amendment did not apply to the agreements and that any such application would have been retroactive and unconstitutionally retrospective. The supreme court agreed, finding that because Colo. Const. art. II, § 11 prohibited retrospective legislation, vested contractual rights created in the developers could not be annulled by the amendment requiring voter approval of new grants of development subsidies or incentives. Under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 4-1-203 (2005), the implied duty of good faith and fair dealing in contracts provided the basis for vested contract rights, and the appellate court erred in ruling that vested contract rights had to have an independent existence. Rather, the developers had a contractual right to expect the City Council to exercise its budgetary discretion on an annual basis to determine whether to appropriate economic incentive funds to be paid to the developers under the agreements. Additionally, the agreements did not create a multiple-fiscal year direct or indirect district debt or other financial obligation, as defined by the Colo. Const. art. X, § 20.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's decision was reversed, and the case was remanded with directions to return it to the trial court for further proceedings.


 

111 of 734 DOCUMENTS


N.F. v. R.A., Jr. ( In re Adoption of C.A.)


Case No. 05SC526


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


137 P.3d 318; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 559



June 26, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA0503.
In re Petition of R.A., 121 P.3d 295, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 525 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner paternal grandparents sought review of a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals vacating an order granting the grandparents visitation with the child under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 19-1-117, who had been adopted by respondent adoptive parents.


OVERVIEW: After the child's parents died, he was adopted by the adoptive parents, his maternal aunt and uncle. His paternal grandparents sought, and were granted, visitation rights under § 19-1-117. The appellate court reversed, holding that the trial court erred by failing to give "special weight" to the parents' wishes. On review, the court held that the appropriate standard for issuance of an order of grandparent visitation under § 19-1-117 required (1) a presumption in favor of the parental visitation determination; (2) to rebut this presumption, a showing by the grandparents through clear and convincing evidence that the parental visitation determination was not in the child's best interests; and (3) placement of the ultimate burden on grandparents to establish by clear and convincing evidence that the visitation schedule they sought was in the best interests of the child. Because the trial court did not make sufficient findings of fact or identify the "special factors" to which it gave weight in entering the visitation order, the court reversed and remanded the case for additional factual findings and conclusions of law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded to the appellate court with directions that the case be remanded to the trial court to reopen the evidentiary proceedings to include evidence addressing the current best interests of the child. After the evidentiary proceedings, the trial court was directed to make findings of fact and conclusions of law consistent with the parental presumption and grandparent burden of proof requirements.

 

112 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. N.R.


Case No. 05SA273, Case No. 05SA294


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


139 P.3d 671; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 569



June 26, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion modified, and as modified, Petition for Rehearing DENIED July 31, 2006.
Modified and rehearing denied by People ex rel. N.R., 2006 Colo. LEXIS 624 (Colo., July 31, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court Yuma County District Court Case No. 05JD1. Honorable Michael K. Singer, Judge.

Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 Yuma County District Court Case No. 05JD1. Honorable Michael K. Singer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile, in a juvenile-delinquency proceeding, was charged with several offenses, which included attempted second-degree murder. Appellant sought disqualification of the prosecutor. The Yuma County District Court, Colorado, entered an order of disqualification. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's passenger eventually died after she was pinned under defendant's vehicle. Appellant left the scene of the accident, and allegedly he failed to seek help. The supreme court was faced with the People's appeal of the trial court's decision, and on the other hand, the argument that the district attorney's office should be disqualified based on a conflict of interest. The supreme court concluded that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 20-1-107 (amended 2002) eliminated appearance of impropriety as a basis for disqualification of district attorneys. Applying § 20-1-107, the supreme court further concluded that it did not authorize disqualification. Disqualification was only proper when: (1) a district attorney requested his own disqualification; (2) the district attorney had a personal or financial interest in the prosecution; or (3) special circumstances existed. In this case, the supreme court concluded the prosecutor was simply performing his prosecutorial duties, and no conflict of interest existed, although the prosecutor received financial backing from the victim's family. Finally, appellant failed to show that he would receive an unfair trial without disqualification.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgments.

 

113 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Colorado General Assembly v. Owens


Case No. 04SC816


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


136 P.3d 262; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 520



June 12, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Pursuant to C.A.R. 50, Case No. 04CA2166. District Court, City & County of Denver, Case No. 03CV3700. Honorable H. Jeffrey Bayless, Judge.
Colo. Gen. Assembly v. Owens, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 345 (Colo., Apr. 11, 2005).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner general assembly (assembly) challenged a decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals in the an action seeking a determination that respondent governor exceeded his constitutional authority to veto distinct "items" within appropriations bills. The trial court upheld the governor's line item veto of definitional headnotes in two appropriations bills (long bills) and rejected the veto of an appropriation in a separate substantive bill.


OVERVIEW: The headnotes defined terms such as "capital outlay," "lease space," and others. The court held that the headnotes were not "items" subject to the governor's item veto power under Colo. Const. art. IV, § 12. The headnotes for health, life, and dental; multiuse network payments; vehicle leases; short term disability; leased space; lease purchase; and legal services deprived the governor of the ability to allocate resources to pay for outstanding expenditures without first obtaining approval from the legislature to use funds from lines already appropriated. The headnotes thus, violated the separation of powers. The headnote that contained a prohibition from covering outstanding vehicle lease payments, lease space, and lease purchases was unconstitutional because it was intertwined with the individual headnotes that limited the governor to a degree that amounted to legislative supervision. The utilities, and computer services headnotes were both affected by the definition of operating expenses and thus were unconstitutional. However, H.B. 02-1246 partially funded a new program and was not a bill funding program. As such, the item veto of the appropriation made therein was invalid.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Ausfahl v. Caldara (In re Title for 2005-2006 # 74)


Case No. 06SA41


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


136 P.3d 237; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 483

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff objectors brought an original proceeding under Colo. Rev. The Title Board considered the initiative pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 1-40-106 and 1-40-106.5 (2005). The objectors argued that the initiative addressed multiple subjects in violation of Colo. Therefore, the Title Board should have refused to fix the titles.

Dillard v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office


Case No. 05SC494


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


134 P.3d 407; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 440



May 15, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 04CA0680.
Dillard v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office, 121 P.3d 301, 2005 Colo. App. LEXIS 845 (Colo. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant challenged the judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals, affirming the judgment of respondent Colorado Industrial Claim Appeals Office, which found in favor of respondent employer, determining that the claimant's workers' compensation claim was subject to the lower benefit cap in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-42-107.5 (2005).


OVERVIEW: The claimant slipped on ice in front of her workplace and hit her head on the sidewalk. The division-sponsored medical examination (DIME) physician rated the claimant's whole person impairment at a total of 29 percent. The 5 percent mental impairment rating, when combined with the physical impairments ratings and converted to a whole person rating, produced a DIME rating of 29 percent, pushing the claimant's impairment rating above 25 percent. The court determined that the issue was whether the claimant could combine a mental impairment rating with a physical impairment rating for the purpose of exceeding the $60,000 cap in favor of the $120,000 cap, both of which were contained in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-42-107.5. The court ruled that the DIME physician contravened the Colorado General Assembly's intent in calculating a 29 percent whole person rating for the claimant. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-42-107(7)(b)(III) (2005), precluded combining a mental impairment rating with a physical impairment rating for the purpose of obtaining the benefit of the higher cap set forth in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-42-107.5.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

116 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cross


Case No. 05SC17


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


127 P.3d 71; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 30



January 17, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0151.
People v. Cross, 114 P.3d 1, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 336 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of harassment by stalking. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed and remanded for a new trial. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-9-111(4)(b)(III), "knowingly" was limited to the statutory language that immediately followed it: "repeatedly follows, approaches, contacts, places under surveillance, or makes any form of communication with that person." The supreme court agreed. The "knowingly" mens rea in § 18-9-111(4)(b)(III) did not apply to the statutory phrase "in a manner that would cause a reasonable person to suffer serious emotional distress and does cause that person to suffer serious emotional distress." Instead, "knowingly" applied to the acts of a defendant who "repeatedly follows, approaches, contacts, places under surveillance, or makes any form of communication with" the victim or another enumerated person. The legislature recognized that the stalker in pursuing the victim might be oblivious to objective reality; he or she might not be aware that the repeated acts engaged in would cause a reasonable person to suffer severe emotional distress. The statute was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad because a reasonable person could understand what conduct was proscribed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed on the jury instruction issue, and the case was returned to the court of appeals for remand to the trial court and retrial consistent with the opinion.

 

117 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Crowe v. Tull


Case No. 04SA385


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


126 P.3d 196; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 27



January 9, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A. R. 21, El Paso County District Court, Case No. 04CV768. Honorable Robert Lowrey, Judge.
Crowe v. Tull, 2006 Colo. LEXIS 4 (Colo., Jan. 9, 2006).



DISPOSITION: RULE MADE ABSOLUTE AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner client brought a Colorado Consumer Protection Act (CCPA) claim against respondent law firm, alleging that the firm used television advertisements to perpetrate a fraud on the public. The El Paso County Distict Court, Colorado, dismissed the CCPA and breach of fiduciary duty claims. The client requested a rule to show cause, and he requested relief.


OVERVIEW: In an original proceeding, the supreme court considered whether the Colorado Consumer Protection Act applied to attorneys' allegedly deceptive trade practices. The client was seriously hurt in a car wreck. He alleged that he retained the law firm to handle his personal injury case based on the representations made in its advertisements. The client claimed that the firm's viability and profitability as a business depended upon intentionally shortchanging its clients. The supreme court held that attorneys could be held liable for violations of the CCPA. A plaintiff bringing a private CCPA claim against an attorney was required to allege that the attorney or law firm knowingly engaged in a deceptive trade practice, which occurred in the course of the attorney or firm's business, vocation, or occupation, significantly impacting the public as actual or potential consumers of legal services, and causing injury in fact to a legally protected interest. The client's CCPA claim did not duplicate his malpractice claim. Moreover, the supreme court refused to adopt the entrepreneurial distinction test, upon which the district court had relied in dismissing the CCPA claim.


OUTCOME: The supreme court remanded the matter to the district court with directions to permit the client to replead his CCPA claim and for further proceedings.

 

118 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Winter v. People


Case No. 04SC654


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


126 P.3d 192; 2006 Colo. LEXIS 29



January 9, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1]
Winter v. People, 2006 Colo. LEXIS 11 (Colo., Jan. 9, 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant admitted taking cash from fellow employees' unlocked lockers, totaling approximately $ 163. He was found guilty of third degree burglary, a class five felony, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-4-204 (2005). The Court of Appeals (Colorado) affirmed the conviction concluding that defendant entered or broke into what was tantamount to a vault. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the court of appeals erred in concluding that defendant entered a "vault" or "apparatus or equipment" under § 18-4-204(1). The supreme court had to determine whether lockers were included within the concept of a "vault" or "other apparatus or equipment" under § 18-4-204(1). The statute contained items that were almost always used to contain money or valuables exclusively: vaults, safes, cash registers, coin vending machines, product dispensers, money depositories, safety deposit boxes, coin telephones, and coin boxes. Those items had specific uses that were plainly evident: it was that appearance that the supreme court found essential to the class. The court held that for an item to be included within the class of items enumerated by the statute, it had to share the same essential characteristics of those items, including having the design and appearance for the safekeeping of money or valuables. Accordingly, the court found that when a locker, as in the case at bar, was unsecured, unlocked, and did not have the appearance of being employed for the safekeeping of valuables, it was not within the class of items contemplated by § 18-4-204(1).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the conviction for third degree burglary was vacated. The case was remanded.

 

119 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vanrees


Case No. 03SC290


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


125 P.3d 403; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1133



December 19, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA0149.
People v. Vanrees, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 874 (Colo., Oct. 3, 2005)
People v. Vanrees, 80 P.3d 840, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 205 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS EN BANC.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent was convicted of, inter alia, attempted second degree murder and second degree assault. The Colorado Court of Appeals held that evidence of mental slowness could be introduced without complying with the pleading requirements of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-103(1.5) (2005) and that the trial court's supplemental instruction improperly excluded evidence of respondent's mental slowness. Certiorari was granted on the two issues.


OVERVIEW: Mental slowness was not a mental disease or defect as defined in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-101.5 (2005). Thus, the evidence of mental slowness did not meet the threshold requirements of the affirmative defense of impaired mental condition and respondent did not have to comply with the Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-103(1.5) (2005) pleading requirements. Based upon his constitutional right to due process, respondent's mental slowness could be considered as factual evidence to show that he lacked the required culpable mental state. Thus, the trial court acted properly by admitting evidence of respondent's mental slowness to contest his guilt for the crimes requiring the culpable mental state of knowingly. As for the issue concerning the instruction given during jury deliberations, when the trial court informed the jury that it could have considered any evidence, other than intoxication, to determine whether respondent possessed the culpable mental state of knowingly, that directive permitted the jury to consider evidence of respondent's mental slowness as to whether he acted knowingly. Thus, the supplemental instruction did not preclude the jury from considering respondent's mental slowness.


OUTCOME: The holding of the lower court was reversed with respect to the jury instruction. The case was remanded to the lower court with directions to return the case to the trial court to reinstate its original judgments of conviction for the crimes of attempted second degree murder, attempted first degree sexual assault, and first degree burglary.

121 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hopp & Flesch, LLC v. Backstreet


Case No. 04SC697


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


123 P.3d 1176; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1044; 23 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1263



November 21, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Modified and rehearing denied by Hopp & Flesch, LLC v. Backstreet, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1108 (Colo., Dec. 19, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA1522.
Backstreet v. Hopp & Flesch, LLC, 107 P.3d 1022, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 1628 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff client sued defendant attorney for damages based on professional negligence and other theories. The attorney moved for dismissal and summary judgment, arguing that his advice did not fall below the requisite standard of care. The trial court determined that, as a matter of law, the attorney's advice did not fall below the standard of care. The Colorado Court of Appeals disagreed with the trial court. Certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: The client worked as a nurse at the sheriff's department. She learned that she mistakenly gave an inmate the wrong medication, and the inmate brought suit. In the ensuing investigation, the inmate's medical record was discovered to have been altered. Approximately six months later, the state filed felony criminal charges against the client for forgery, tampering with evidence, and official misconduct. The client claimed that the attorney's advice that she not participate in an internal investigation was negligent because she had received use immunity through the sheriff's advisement. The supreme court held that the sheriff department's advisement that any statements the client made during an internal investigation could not later be used against her in a criminal proceeding did not constitute an enforceable offer of use immunity. When the attorney advised the client not to rely on the advisement; and the employee followed her attorney's advice, the apparent authority doctrine implying a grant of use immunity did not apply. The attorney's advice not to cooperate with the employer did not constitute malpractice because the employer's advisement was too ambiguous.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court. The matter was returned to the lower appellate court with directions to remand it to the trial court for entry of judgment in favor of the attorney.

 

122 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Harris v. Denver Post Corp.


Case No. 04SC133


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


123 P.3d 1166; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1017; 34 Media L. Rep. 1103



November 15, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Rehearing denied by Harris v. Denver Post Corp., 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1099 (Colo., Dec. 19, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA1327.
Denver Post Corp. v. Cook, 104 P.3d 293, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 1423 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two sets of parents, challenged a judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals, which held that recordings made by their sons and seized by petitioner sheriff pursuant to a valid search warrant from their home were public records under Colorado's Open Records Act (CORA), Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-201 et seq. (2005). Respondent newspaper sought inspection of the recordings.


OVERVIEW: The parents' sons had killed 12 students and a teacher at a high school. They then killed themselves. According to the sheriff's public report of the investigation it conducted of the murders, the recordings showed the sons plotting and preparing for the murders. In its initial opinion, the appellate court held that the recordings were subject to the Colorado Criminal Justice Records Act (CCJRA), Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-72-301 et seq. (2005). However, after a petition for rehearing, the appellate court reconsidered and held that the recordings were subject not to the CCJRA but to CORA. The instant court agreed with the newspaper that the records were subject to the CCJRA, not CORA. Because the sheriff obtained the recordings pursuant to a search warrant that had not been invalidated, and used them to investigate crimes connected with the killings, the instant court held that they were criminal justice records under the CCJRA and were subject to the sheriff's exercise of sound discretion to allow the requested inspection or not, utilizing a balancing test taking into account the relevant public and private interests.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the case with directions for the district court to order the sheriff to determine under the CCJRA whether or not to allow the newspaper's inspection request.

 

123 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vanrees


CASE NO. 03SC290


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


2005 Colo. LEXIS 874



October 3, 2005, Decided


NOTICE:

 [*1] THIS OPINION WAS WITHDRAWN BY THE COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by, Rehearing denied by People v. Vanrees, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1085 (Colo., Dec. 19, 2005)
Substituted opinion at, Rehearing denied by People v. Vanrees, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 1133 (Colo., Dec. 19, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA0149.
People v. Vanrees, 80 P.3d 840, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 205 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People petitioned for certiorari review of a decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals, which reversed defendant's convictions for attempted second degree murder, attempted first degree sexual assault, and first degree burglary and affirmed his conviction for second degree assault.


OVERVIEW: After consuming a large amount of alcohol, defendant entered the victim's house and assaulted her. The victim told the detective that defendant's behavior was bizarre. Defendant claimed that he was mentally slow. The appellate court held that an instruction giving the definition of insanity without naming it as such was reversible error. The court held that evidence offered to support a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity had a legally distinct purpose from evidence offered to negate a culpable mental state. Evidence offered to negate the culpable mental state was directed to disproving an essential element of the crime charged, as opposed to being offered to establish that defendant was mentally incapable of formulating the required mental state in the first instance. The court reversed, finding that while defense counsel argued consistently with the terms of Colorado's statutory affirmative defense of voluntary intoxication, that intoxication made it so that defendant did not have the ability to have specific intent and deliberation, defendant never argued that his mental slowness made him incapable of distinguishing right from wrong.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded with directions to reinstate defendant's convictions.

High Plains A & M, LLC v. Southeastern Co. Water Conservancy Dist.


Consolidated Cases, Case No. 04SA266, Case No. 04SA267

120 P.3d 710; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 831



September 12, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified and Petitions for Rehearing Denied October 11, 2005.
Modified and rehearing denied by High Plains A & M, L.L.C. v. Southeastern Colorado Water Conservancy Dist., 2005 Colo. LEXIS 935 (Colo., Oct. 11, 2005)
Companion case at ISG, LLC v. Arkansas Valley Ditch Ass'n, 120 P.3d 724, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 832 (Colo., 2005)
Related proceeding at Fort Lyon Canal Co. v. High Plains A&M, LLC, 2007 Colo. LEXIS 822 (Colo., Sept. 10, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court Water Division 2, Case No. 02CW183. Honorable Dennis Maes, Judge. Appeal from the District Court Water Division 2, Case No. 03CW28. Honorable Dennis Maes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Applicant companies appealed the decision of the District Court Water Division 2 (Colorado) dismissing their applications for change of water rights. Applicants sought to change water rights historically used for irrigation to any one of over 50 proposed uses in any of 28 Colorado counties.


OVERVIEW: Applicants filed a motion for a Colo. R. Civ. P. 56(h) determination of a question of law that the anti-speculation doctrine and the "can and will" requirements of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 37-92-305(9)(b) (2005) applied only to new appropriations in proceedings for absolute or conditional water rights and not to change application proceedings. The water court dismissed the applications finding the applications to be speculative in nature. The court affirmed and held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 37-92-103(3)(a)(I) and (II) applied in a change in type and place of use proceeding, and the application did not identify any particular municipal or quasi-municipal entity with which applicants had an agreement for actual beneficial use of the water sought to be transferred. Nor did they identify any parcels of private land, businesses, or a service area for specific consumers of the water to be served under the change decree. To allow applicants to pursue the application for the change decree, in the absence of identifying the location or locations for actual beneficial use to be made under the change decree, would be to treat that company differently from all other change applicants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the water court.

 

125 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weinreich


Case No. 04SC436


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


119 P.3d 1073; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 828



September 12, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0995.
People v. Weinreich, 98 P.3d 920, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 437 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of reckless child abuse resulting in death, child abuse resulting in injury, and unlawfully overtaking on the left. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed his conviction, holding that a jury instruction constructively amended the charge of reckless child abuse resulting in death. The court of appeals also disapproved of the trial court's use of an obsolete version of the child abuse statute. The People sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving with his twin daughters, who were unrestrained, on his lap. Defendant attempted to pass a vehicle that was pulling a trailer. As defendant started to pass, the other vehicle began a left turn. The incident resulted in one child's death and in serious injuries to the other child. At issue on review was the trial court's use of an obsolete jury instruction. In using an obsolete instruction, the trial court informed the jury that it could convict defendant of reckless child abuse resulting in death if defendant had "unreasonably placed his daughter in a situation that may have endangered" her life or health. The operative phrase of the obsolete instruction, "may have endangered," appeared in previous versions of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-6- 401(1)(a). The trial court committed plain and reversible error by failing to give an instruction that substantially conformed to the more recent version of the child abuse statute. The instruction's use exposed defendant to a conviction for an act that the jury could perceive as endangering the child's life or health but which did not cause his daughter's death, in other words, a nonexistent crime.


OUTCOME: The supreme court upheld the judgment of the lower appellate court, which reversed the conviction.

 

126 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Medina v. People


Consolidated Cases Case No. 04SC167, Case No. 04SC334


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


114 P.3d 845; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 637



June 27, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Medina v. People, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 703 (Colo., Aug. 8, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Case No. 04SC167. Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0202

Case No. 04SC334. Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0925.
People v. Medina, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 107 (Colo. Ct. App., Feb. 5, 2004)
People v. Moses, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 583 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 15, 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED AND CASES REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of decisions of the Colorado Court of Appeals, which affirmed defendants' convictions and held that juror questioning in defendants' cases did not violate their right to a fair trial and impartial jury and that, under the terms of the Colorado Jury Reform Pilot Project, which culminated in the adoption of Colo. R. Crim. P. 24(g), defendants were not prejudiced by the questions posed by the jurors.


OVERVIEW: During the presentation of the first defendant's case, defense counsel called an investigator from the district attorney's office to testify about inconsistent statements made by the victim and his girlfriend. After examination by the attorneys, the trial court asked a juror's question regarding how often a witness's story changed. The court held that the practice of allowing jurors to ask questions of witnesses through the trial court was not per se unconstitutional under Colo. Const. art. II, § 16. The trial court erred in allowing the question because it called for irrelevant expert testimony. The witness was not qualified by the defense as an expert, and the answer had no bearing on the facts of defendant's case. The answer to the question was harmless, however, because one interpretation was that it was common for truthful witnesses to have inconsistent statements and the other was that the witnesses were lying. In the second defendant's trial, the defense and prosecution both agreed that a juror's question called for speculation. The court declined to find an error simply because the jury could have overheard a bench conference where no prejudicial statements were made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions of the court of appeals and remanded the cases to that court to return them to the trial court for further proceedings.

Dempsey v. People


Case No. 04SC362


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


117 P.3d 800; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 622



June 27, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Rehearing denied by Dempsey v. People, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 720 (Colo., Aug. 22, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Boulder County District Court. Case No. 03CV1611.
Dempsey v. People, 2004 Colo. LEXIS 848 (Colo., Oct. 25, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Boulder County District Court (Colorado) that sustained his convictions for disrupting a lawful assembly in violation of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-9-108 (2004), Colorado's Disrupting Lawful Assembly statute, and for obstructing a peace officer in violation of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-104 (2004), the obstruction statute. Defendant was arrested and convicted based on his conduct at a political rally.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support his convictions. Defendant did not challenge the constitutionality of the disruption statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-9-108, but challenged its application to his case. The court held that § 18-9-108 was constitutionally applied in this case because it was directed at conduct rather than content and the jury was instructed that it must find that defendant intended the disruption and that a significant disruption actually occurred. However, the court found that there was insufficient evidence to sustain defendant's conviction where there was no evidence that his conduct significantly disrupted the rally. The court held there was sufficient evidence to sustain defendant's obstruction conviction because the evidence showed that the officers reasonably believed that defendant had committed a crime and were justified in detaining him to issue a summons; and defendant's conduct, when viewed as a totality, constituted a knowing obstruction of that process. The court held that the trial court properly rejected defendant's requested instruction that his right to free expression was an affirmative defense to the disruption charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for obstructing a peace officer but reversed his conviction for disrupting a lawful assembly and remanded the case for further consistent proceedings.

People v. Garcia


Case No. 03SC675


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


113 P.3d 775; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 562



June 13, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA2514.
People v. Garcia, 87 P.3d 159, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 1320 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Colorado successfully petitioned for certiorari to review the judgment from the Court of Appeals (Colorado), which reversed defendant's convictions for the attempted second degree murder and first degree assault of his wife, finding that the trial court erred by not permitting defendant to proceed under his claimed affirmative defense of voluntary intoxication under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-804 (2004).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was an insulin-dependent diabetic. Three days before the alleged crimes, his wife had told him she wanted a divorce and he moved out. In anticipation of eating cake and ice cream at his daughter's afternoon birthday party, he injected himself with a large dose of insulin. He did not eat anything following the injection. Later that morning, while he and his wife were running errands for the party, he hit her in the head with a hammer and ran her over with their van. Before his trial, the trial court denied his request to raise involuntary intoxication brought on by hypoglycemia as an affirmative defense. Because the trial court refused to permit him to proceed with this defense and instead ruled that the defense could only be presented with a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity, the court of appeals reversed his conviction. On review, the court found that insanity and involuntary intoxication were separate and distinct defenses and that the medical condition of insulin-induced hypoglycemia could constitute the affirmative defense of involuntary intoxication. Accordingly, the court remanded to give both parties a chance to supplement the record on the asserted defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the part of the judgment that found that the trial court erred by not permitting defendant to proceed under his claimed affirmative defense of voluntary intoxication but reversed the judgment of reversal and remanded to the court of appeals with instructions to permit the trial court to permit both parties the opportunity to supplement the record on the issue of defendant's claimed involuntary intoxication defense.
