Spahmer v. Gullette


Case No. 03SC751


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


113 P.3d 158; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 599



June 6, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA1774.
In re Parental Responsibility of J.N.G., 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 1306 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 14, 2003).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Worried that appellant mother was going to permanently move to Arizona with the child when they went to visit the mother's family during the Christmas holiday, appellee father filed an action for the allocation of parental rights and responsibilities and requested a restraining order requiring the mother to return to Colorado. The mother was ordered to remain in Colorado, and the Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. The mother sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the mother contended the trial court abused its discretion and exceeded its statutory authority under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 14-10-124 (2004) when it ordered her to live in Colorado. The supreme court agreed, holding that in the determination to allocate parental responsibilities, the trial court had no statutory authority to order the mother to live in a specific location. The trial court should have allocated parenting time with the understanding that the mother was intending to live in Arizona and the father was intending to live in Colorado. In failing to do this, the trial court abused its discretion and exceeded its statutory authority.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with instructions to return the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

130 of 734 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Ciesluk


Case No. 04SC555


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


113 P.3d 135; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 548



June 6, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at In re Marriage of Ciesluk, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 1470 (Colo. Ct. App., Aug. 31, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA2047.
In re Marriage of Ciesluk, 100 P.3d 527, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 868 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals that affirmed the trial court's order denying her motion to modify parenting time pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 14-10-129 (2004). The mother had filed the modification motion requesting that she be allowed to relocate to Arizona with her son in order to take a new job. The trial court found it was not in her son's best interests to relocate.


OVERVIEW: The mother claimed the 1999 amendment of § 14-10-129 did not affect the presumption in favor of the majority time parent in relocation cases, and that the trial court erred in requiring her to show that the modification of parenting time would "enhance" her son. The court held that § 14-10-129, as amended, eliminated the three-part Francis test used to determine whether a proposed move was in the best interests of the child, including the presumption in favor of the majority time parent seeking relocation. Instead, both parents equally shared the burden of demonstrating what was in the child's best interests. The court held that the trial court abused its discretion under § 14-10-129(2)(c) where it prematurely concluded that it would be in the child's best interests to remain in close proximity to both parents. The court held that requiring the mother to show that the move would enhance her son's quality of life was a remnant of the Francis test that the legislature did not adopt in amending § 14-10-129, and created a presumption in the father's favor contrary to the legislative intent of § 14-10-129(2)(c). Also the trial court failed to impose an equal burden on the father.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals judgment and remanded the case with instructions to return the case to the trial court for further consistent proceedings.

 

131 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rodriguez


Case No. 04SC219


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


112 P.3d 693; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 535



May 31, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Jefferson County District Court. Case No. 02CV3437.
People v. Rodriguez, 2004 Colo. LEXIS 643 (Colo., Aug. 30, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a county trial court of sexual exploitation of a child, a class 1 misdemeanor, and theft under $ 100, a class 3 misdemeanor. The Jefferson County District Court (Colorado) reversed his conviction on grounds that his rights under Colo. Const. art. II, § 23 were violated when the county court denied his demand for a jury of 12 and instead provided only a jury of six. The State sought certiorari review.


OVERVIEW: After photographs of nude children and stolen property were found in defendant's possession, he was charged with two misdemeanors. Prior to trial, he demanded a jury of 12 pursuant to Colo. Const. art. II, § 23. The county court denied the request, finding that both Colo. R. Crim P. 23(a)(2) and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-406(1) (2004) provided for a jury of six in misdemeanor cases. The district court reversed, finding that Const. art. II, § 23 implicitly established a right to a jury of 12 in criminal cases. The supreme court disagreed. By looking to the jurisdiction of county and district courts as provided by the Colorado Constitution and the general assembly, the supreme court found there was right to a jury of 12 in felony cases that did not extend to misdemeanor cases. Thus, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-406(1) and Colo. R. Crim. P. 23 were constitutional under Colo. Const. art. II, § 23.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed and the judgment of the county court was reinstated.

 

132 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lopez v. People


Case No. 04SC150


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


113 P.3d 713; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 504



May 23, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified June 27, 2005.
Modified and rehearing denied by Lopez v. People, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 631 (Colo., June 27, 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lopez v. Colo., 546 U.S. 1017, 126 S. Ct. 654, 163 L. Ed. 2d 529, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 8421 (U.S., Nov. 14, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Lopez, 97 P.3d 277, 2004 Colo. App. LEXIS 340 (Colo. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Opinion Modified, and as modified, Petition for Rehearing DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Certiorari was granted to the court of appeals (Colorado), which affirmed the trial court's sentence, to consider whether Apprendi and Blakely invalidated the aggravated sentence imposed by the trial court against defendant where he was returned for sentencing on his deferred judgment for possession of a controlled substance after being convicted of vehicular homicide during the deferral period.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had tested positive for drugs during his deferred sentence and drug treatment was unsuccessful. The trial court found a relationship between the original drug possession charge and the vehicular homicide incident involving alcohol, suggesting a pattern of conduct. The court held that defendant's aggravated sentence under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-401(6) (2004) was not unconstitutional. Defendant's Colo. R. Crim. P. 11 advisement notified him that he could be sentenced to 12 years if the judge found extraordinary aggravating circumstances present, but it alone did not authorize the judge's use of other facts defendant did not admit to aggravate the sentence. The court held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-401(6) (2004) could be applied constitutionally, and that the prior conviction facts based on his convictions in the vehicular homicide case adequately support the aggravated sentence. Thus, under the circumstances of this case, the vehicular homicide convictions were proper Blakely-exempt aggravating factors for the possession sentence because the facts were reflected in a jury's verdict that was entered before the deferred possession sentence was imposed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' judgment and upheld the sentence.

 

133 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Canister


Case No. 03SA170


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


110 P.3d 380; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 368



April 18, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at People v. Hagos, 110 P.3d 1290, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 359 (Colo., 2005)
Related proceeding at People v. Washington, 2007 Colo. App. LEXIS 1029 (Colo. Ct. App., May 31, 2007)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed a judgment of the Arapahoe County District Court (Colorado), which held that, because defendant could not have been constitutionally sentenced to death as of the last day of his trial and because the current sentencing scheme was unconstitutional, the prosecution could not seek the death penalty. The prosecution appealed the trial court's ruling to the instant court pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-12-102(1) (2004).


OVERVIEW: Prior to defendant's trial, the prosecution announced its intention to seek the death penalty. Three days into defendant's trial the United States Supreme Court ruled that the Arizona capital sentencing statute, which was similar to Colorado's statute, violated a defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial. In response, the Colorado legislature enacted Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.4-102(1)(e), which provided that if the prosecution announced by July 12, 2002, that it was seeking the death sentence, the sentence could still be determined by the trial judge. Defendant and one other person were the only two people to whom the provisions of that section could ever apply. The court upheld the district court's ruling that § 18-1.4-102(1)(e) was unconstitutional on the grounds that it contravened the prohibition on special legislation in Colo. Const. art. V, § 25. The court noted that § 18-1.4-102(1)(e) came within at least one enumerated prohibition in Colo. Const. art. V, § 25. The court also determined that a class that was drawn so that it would never have any members other than those targeted by the legislation was illusory and that § 18-1.4-102(1)(e) created such a class.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the ruling of the district court and remanded for imposition of a life sentence without the possibility of parole.

 

134 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hagos


No. 03SA271


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


110 P.3d 1290; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 359



April 18, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by People v. Hagos, 2009 Colo. App. LEXIS 1868 (Colo. Ct. App., Oct. 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from Denver County District Court Case. No. 99CR2738. Honorable H. Jeffrey Bayless, Judge.
People v. Canister, 110 P.3d 380, 2005 Colo. LEXIS 368 (Colo., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury before the Denver County District Court, Colorado, convicted defendant of first degree murder, attempted first degree murder, conspiracy to commit first degree murder, and retaliation against a witness. Defendant later moved for a life sentence, and the motion was granted. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Before trial, the State announced its intention to seek the death penalty. On the day the sentencing hearing was originally scheduled, the method of sentencing in death penalty cases, which consisted of a three-judge panel, was declared unconstitutional. The governor later called a special legislative session, the death penalty statute was reformed, and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.4-102(1)(e) (2002) was passed. The State sought review of the constitutionality of this section. The supreme court held that the section was unconstitutional special legislation, in violation of Colo. Const. art. V, § 25, because the statute could only apply to two people: this defendant and one other defendant.


OUTCOME: The supreme court upheld the district court's order. The matter was remanded for imposition of a life sentence without the possibility of parole.

 

135 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Argus Real Estate, Inc. v. E-470 Pub. Highway Auth.


Case No. 04SC100


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


109 P.3d 604; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 322



March 28, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA2044.
Argus Real Estate, Inc. v. E-470 Pub. Highway Auth., 97 P.3d 215, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 2038 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Decisions of Court of Appeals and trial court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to review whether the Colorado Court of Appeals erred in upholding a district court's entry of summary judgment based on its conclusion that res judicata precluded the filing of a separate action requesting reformation of a property agreement under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-11-1106(2) (2004). Petitioner real estate company made the claim for reformation against respondent public highway authority.


OVERVIEW: The company was the assignee of a parcel of land that the original owner donated to the authority. The owner asserted that the authority agreed to return the land to the owner if it was not used as a highway, as intended by the parties. The authority did not use the land as a highway but did not return the land to the owner. In the company's original action against the authority, both the owner and the authority sought quiet title to the parcel. It was incumbent upon each party to raise any claims, issues, and defenses it may have had that would affect the adjudication of rights in the parcel. Not only could the owner have raised its statutory reformation claim as affecting its rights in the parcel, but the owner should have raised the claim because the claim directly involved the adjudication of its rights in the parcel as part of the quiet title claim. The court held that in absence of any clear intent to abrogate the common law doctrine of res judicata, § 15-11-1106(2) did not provide an exception to res judicata such that the company could raise the statutory reformation claim in a subsequent judicial proceeding when the claim could have been raised in the previous action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

136 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Wash. County Bd. of Equalization v. Petron Dev. Co.


Case No. 03SC787


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


109 P.3d 146; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 334; 160 Oil & Gas Rep. 788



March 28, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA1476.
Petron Dev. Co. v. Wash. County Bd. of Equalization, 91 P.3d 408, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 1575 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent oil company sought a tax deduction of certain processing cost from a leasehold site. Appellant Washington County Board of Equalization disallowed the request. The Colorado Court of Appeals held that processing costs occurring on the leasehold site to make the oil marketable were properly deducted from the sale price of the oil in valuing the unprocessed material. Certiorari was granted to review the decision.


OVERVIEW: The oil company operated 10 oil wells on six leasehold sites. The following issues were presented: (1) whether the further extraction and separating of crude oil from emulsion at an oil well lease converted the product from unprocessed to processed for purposes of valuing oil producing leaseholds; (2) whether an oil producer's price that was received at a sale at the leasehold should be the selling price at the wellhead for purpose of ad valorem taxes; (3) whether the term "well site" included the separation and storage facilities of crude oil located at or near the well bore for purposes of defining deductions for manufacturing/processing, gathering and transportation. The applicable constitutional and statutory provisions were Colo. Const. art. X, § 3(1)(b) and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-7-101(1)(d) (2004). The legislature intended "wellhead" to mean the physical location where the extracted material emerged. The general assembly implemented § 39-7-101(1)(d) by basing tax assessment on a taxpayer's declaration of the selling price at the wellhead. The netback method for reporting selling price at the wellhead effectuated the intent of the constitutional and statutory provisions.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court upheld the court of appeals' judgment.

 

137 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Robbins v. People


Case No. 03SC563


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


107 P.3d 384; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 162



February 22, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Robbins v. Colorado, 126 S. Ct. 117, 163 L. Ed. 2d 126, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 6922 (U.S., 2005)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Complaint dismissed at Robbins v. Ortiz, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 55972 (D. Colo., June 14, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA0927.
People v. Robbins, 87 P.3d 120, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 509 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, an inmate, filed a motion under Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) to vacate a judgment of conviction entered against him in 1958 for felony first degree murder. The trial court dismissed the motion on the ground that it was barred by laches. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of murder in 1958 and brought his postconviction motion, claiming ineffective assistance of trial counsel, in 1995, after counsel died. Addressing the finding that the motion was barred by laches, the court held that while Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-5-402 (2004) did not impose an absolute statutory limitation on the period in which a defendant could bring a collateral attack against a class one felony, time bars could arise from application of the doctrine of laches. Trial courts had the flexibility to assess cases individually. In the case at bar, the record supported the laches finding. The inmate's conviction was affirmed in 1960, and since that time his life sentence had been commuted and he had been paroled three times. More than three decades after his conviction, and a mere eight months after his original defense counsel died, he filed a motion asserting ineffective assistance of counsel. The issue was available to him in the 1960's, and during his parole, he had the opportunity to contact counsel and pursue postconviction matters. The court agreed with the trial court that the timing of the motion was suspect and that the State demonstrated prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the inmate's motion and accordingly affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

 

138 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lobato v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office


No. 03SC556


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


105 P.3d 220; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 29



January 18, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals. No. 02CA1145.
Lobato v. Indus. Claim Appeals Office, 94 P.3d 1173, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 909 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prior to the effective date of a 1999 amendment to the Workers Compensation Act, respondent employer provided petitioner employee a final admission of liability (FAL) that failed to provide notice of the new, retroactive time limit. Respondent Industrial Claim Appeals Office (ICAO) upheld the striking of the employee's division independent medical examiner (DIME) report as untimely. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: Amendments to the Act retroactively altered the timeline for a claimant's selection of a DIME. The court of appeals found that the effective date of the 1999 amendment was the triggering event for the new 30-day period. Based on that trigger, the employee failed to meet the deadline, even though the FAL gave no notice of the new time limit. On further review, the court reversed. The court reasoned that Colo. Div. of Workers' Comp. Rule IV(L)(3), 7 Colo. Code Regs. § 1101-3 (2004), provided a procedure whereby employers or insurers could choose to provide amended notice to claimants with pre-amendment FALs in order to preserve their time-bar defenses to the selection of DIMEs. The court found that this procedure correctly emphasized the importance of administrative notice, while still providing a way to expedite particular open cases. The rule thus bridged the gap between the legislature's primary intent to expedite cases and its concern for adequate administrative notice. The employee did not receive any notice that would have triggered the 30-day time limit of the 1998 and 1999 amendments. Therefore, those time limits did not apply to his case and his DIME selection was timely.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded with directions to reinstate the employee's claim and DIME report and to return the case to the administrative law judge for further proceedings.

140 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Galvadon


Case No. 04SA166


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


103 P.3d 923; 2005 Colo. LEXIS 6



January 10, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Interlocutory Appeal from the District Court, El Paso County, Case No. 03CR5313. Honorable Timothy J. Simmons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Trial court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution filed an interlocutory appeal of an order of the District Court, El Paso County, Colorado, granting defendant's motion to suppress evidence discovered as a result of a search of the back room of the liquor store where he was employed as a night manager.


OVERVIEW: A police officer investigating suspicious activity followed two individuals into the back room of the liquor store. Other officers were called to the scene and, while standing in the back room, discovered bricks of marijuana. An officer seized a store surveillance video tape upon seeing defendant attempting to stuff it into his pants. The video showed defendant placing what officers believed to be bricks of marijuana in the back room. The trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress the evidence seized from the liquor store, finding that defendant had standing to assert Fourth Amendment protection on the basis of his reasonable expectation of privacy in the back room. The supreme court affirmed. Defendant, as the night manager and the sole person in control of the store, maintained a reasonable expectation of privacy from government intrusion in the back room of the store, an area without public access, such that he was entitled to assert the Fourth Amendment's protection. The use of a surveillance system reviewable only by the night manager and the owner of the store did not diminish his reasonable expectation of privacy from government intrusion.


OUTCOME: The trial court's ruling was affirmed.

142 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Argomaniz-Ramirez


Case No. 04SA105


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


102 P.3d 1015; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 981



December 6, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. District Court, City & County of Denver, Case No. 03CR2202. Honorable Michael A. Martinez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Rule made absolute.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court precluded the introduction into evidence a videotaped interview of two girls based on a Violation of the Confrontation Clause, U.S. Const. amend. VI, and the State asked the supreme court to invoke its original jurisdiction under Colo. App. R. 21.


OVERVIEW: The State sought to introduce videotaped statements made by two children to a law enforcement officer into evidence against defendant in his sexual assault trial. The trial court determined that the videotaped interviews were inadmissible even though both children were scheduled to testify at trial, but the supreme court disagreed, stating that, consistent with the Confrontation Clause, U.S. Const. amend. VI, and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-25-129 (2004), prior recorded statements made by children to law enforcement officials could be introduced into evidence when the children testify at trial. The State made repeated assurances that both children would testify at trial, and the supreme court based its decision on those assurances. Because the hearsay declarants would be testifying at trial and would thus be subject to cross-examination, admission of their out-of-court statements did not violate the Confrontation Clause.


OUTCOME: The supreme court made its rule absolute and ordered the trial court to vacate its order precluding the introduction into evidence of the videotapes.

Vigil v. Franklin


Case No. 03SC479


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


103 P.3d 322; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 941



November 30, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 01CA2172.
Vigil v. Franklin, 81 P.3d 1084, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 778 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Court of Appeals reversed; remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review by writ of certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals' decision that the trial court's grant of summary judgment was proper in petitioner's action for premises liability against respondent property owners.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, who was mildly retarded, was working as a laborer through a handicapped center for respondents. Petitioner was injured when he attempted to dive into an above-ground pool on the premises. The court of appeals held that respondents had no duty to warn of the open and obvious danger of diving into an above-ground swimming pool. The court held that the language of the premises liability statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-311-15 (2004) evidenced the general assembly's intent to establish a comprehensive and exclusive specification of the duties landowners owed to those injured on their property. The plain language preempted prior common law theories of liability, and established the statute as the sole codification of landowner duties in tort. The court concluded that the open and obvious danger doctrine was not intended to be one of the defenses to the existence of a landowner's duties. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals stating that it erroneously considered respondents' common law open and obvious danger defense and that summary judgment was improper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded.

 

144 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Bovard v. People


Case No. 04SC4


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


99 P.3d 585; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 845



October 18, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0974.
People v. Bovard, 87 P.3d 215, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 1786 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado Court of Appeals dismissed defendant's appeal from the district court's judgment convicting him of driving under the influence of alcohol for lack of subject matter jurisdiction on the grounds that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-6-310 (2004) provided that further appeal from the final judgment of a district court, in a case appealed from a county court, was only by writ of certiorari. The supreme court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court granted certiorari to determine whether the court of appeals erred in rejecting defendant's appeal on the grounds that his only remedy was to request a writ of certiorari issued in the discretion of the supreme court. The supreme court noted that, under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-6-310(2), as well as under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-12-101, the defendant did not have an appeal as of right from a judgment of the district court that only modified the county court judgment. The supreme court read Colo. R. Crim. P. 37(h) to provide that, only in cases tried de novo by the district court would the district court judgment be subject to direct appeal. While the defendant's appeal as of a right from county to district court was satisfied when the district court acted in its appellate capacity, that right was not satisfied when the district court entered the judgment from a trial de novo. Because Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-12-101 granted defendant at least one appeal from the proceeding resulting in his conviction, that appeal as of right meant a direct appeal to an appellate tribunal, which was the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and remanded for consideration of the merits of defendant's appeal.

In re Trupp


Case No. 03SA344


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


92 P.3d 923; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 525



June 28, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Decision of the Presiding Disciplinary Judge. Case No. 01PDJ068. Hon. Roger Keithley, Presiding Disciplinary Judge.
People v. Trupp, 51 P.3d 985, 2002 Colo. LEXIS 448 (Colo., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent assistant attorney regulation counsel appealed a decision from the (Colorado) Presiding Disciplinary Judge (PDJ), which, pursuant to an attorney's motion, found that the counsel violated Colo. R. Civ. P. 11(a) when she filed a complaint against the attorney alleging that he violated Colo. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(c) by stating on his annual fee registration forms that he was taking measures to cure his child support arrearages.


OVERVIEW: An attorney told the assistant counsel who was investigating his noncompliance with his child support duty that the measures he had taken to comply with his duty was to apply for Social Security retirement that would go to his son. Because the attorney did not obtain a court modification of his child support order even after the counsel told him to, the counsel filed a complaint alleging a Colo. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(c) violation in his forms. The attorney obtained a directed verdict and filed a motion for Colo. R. Civ. P. 11 sanctions against the attorney, and the PDJ found that the counsel violated Rule 11. On appeal, the court held that because the counsel sought and obtained information from the attorney, had her investigator obtain court records and interview the attorney's child's mother, and conducted legal research on the issues, the counsel complied with her Rule 11 duty to investigate the matter. Because the counsel presented the facts to the Colorado Attorney Regulation Committee, which authorized the complaint, and because the complaint did not state that the attorney knowingly violated Colo. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(c), the counsel did not violate Colo. R. Civ. P. 11.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the PDJ with orders to deny the attorney's motion for sanctions.

Garhart v. Columbia/Healthone. L.L.C.


Case No. 02SA182


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


95 P.3d 571; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 528



June 28, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion Modified, and as Modified, Petition For Rehearing Denied. En Banc. August 16, 2004.
Modified and rehearing denied by Garhart v. Columbia/Healthone, L.L.C., 2004 Colo. LEXIS 642 (Colo., Aug. 16, 2004)
Appeal after remand at Garhart v. Columbia/HealthONE, L.L.C., 2007 Colo. App. LEXIS 1395 (Colo. Ct. App., July 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Adams County District Court, Case No. 98CV2973 Honorable Chris Melonakis, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  [**2] JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, minor child and parents, sought review of the decision of the Adams County District Court (Colorado), which found in favor of defendant hospital, holding that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-205(1)(f) (2001) of the Health Care Availability Act (HCAA), Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-101 et seq., was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The mother gave birth to a son on September 2, 1996 and as a result, the mother suffered severe pelvic damage during the delivery and the son incurred severe cerebral palsy from birth trauma. The trial court found Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-205(1)(f)(I) (2001), providing for periodic payments, to be unconstitutional and the supreme court then accepted jurisdiction over the appeal. The supreme court reversed the trial court's judgment in part, stating that the trial court erred in determining that the periodic payment provision of the HCAA was unconstitutional. The trial court was instructed to order periodic payment of the child's future damages, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-203 (2003). The son did not meet the criteria set forth in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-205(1), thus, he could not receive future damages in one lump sum. In addition, the trial court was also ordered to calculate the mother and son's noneconomic damages by first applying the HCAA noneconomic damages caps and then apportioning the award pursuant to the jury's percentages, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-302 (2001). The supreme court further rejected the mother and son's constitutional challenges to the HCAA.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed in part and remanded, finding that the trial court erred in determining that the periodic payment provision of the HCAA was unconstitutional. The trial court was also ordered to calculate the noneconomic damages awards by first applying the HCAA noneconomic damages caps and then apportioning the award pursuant to the jury's percentages. In all other respects, the trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Owens v. Colo. Cong. of Parents


Case No. 03SA364


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


92 P.3d 933; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 531



June 28, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from District Court, City and County of Denver. Case No. 03CV3734. Honorable Joseph E. Meyer, III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee parents et al challenged the constitutionality of the Colorado Opportunity Contract Pilot Program (Pilot Program), Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 22-56-101 to -110 (2003). The District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, concluded that the Pilot Program was unconstitutional as it interfered with the local school districts' discretion to allocate their funding. Appellant Governor et al sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the Governor contended the local control provisions of Colo. Const. art. IX, § 15 did not place any impediment in the way of the general assembly's power to enact the Pilot Program. The supreme court disagreed, finding that the Pilot Program did violate the local control requirements of § 15 because it directed the school districts to turn over a portion of their locally-raised funds to nonpublic schools over whose instruction the districts had no control. Irrespective of the fact that the goals of the program and the policy considerations underlying it were laudable, the supreme court could see no way to reconcile the structure of the program with the requirements of the Colorado Constitution. To hold otherwise would have rendered the local control provisions of § 15 meaningless. As such, the trial court properly found that the Pilot Program was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Cacioppo v. Eagle County Sch. Dist. Re-50J


No. 03SA336


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


92 P.3d 453; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 487

DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of the decision of the district court, which found in favor of defendant school district in the taxpayer's action challenging a mill levy increase approved by voters and declined to overturn the election. The Colorado Court of Appeals transferred the case to the supreme court because of the constitutional nature of the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court reversed a constitutional challenge to a ballot issue approved by voters raising taxes to allow a cost-of-living increase for the school district. He filed his action nearly four months after the election seeking to overturn the election, arguing that the ballot title and the notice sent to voters violated Colo. Const. art. X, § 20. All of the taxpayer's justiciable claims were time-barred either by § 1-11-203.5 or by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 1-11-213 (2003), which required a claimant who contested the result of an election to filed a written statement of intent within 10 days of the filing of the official survey of returns.

E-470 Pub. Highway Auth. v. Revenig


Case No. 03SA356


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


91 P.3d 1038; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 490



June 14, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at E-470 Pub. Highway Auth. v. Revenig, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 484 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 6, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer from the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 03CA0772. Adams County District Court, Case No. 96CV1409. Honorable C. Vincent Phelps, Judge.
E-470 Pub. Highway Auth. v. Jagow, 30 P.3d 798, 2001 Colo. App. LEXIS 264 (Colo. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and case returned to trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants landowners appealed the judgment of the Adams County District Court (Colorado) that upheld the constitutionality of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 38-1-114(2)(d) (2003) allowing the compensation paid to them for a taking to be reduced by up to 50 percent.


OVERVIEW: The landowners argued, inter alia, that they were entitled to $ 197,000 in cash in addition to the surplus of $ 197,000 in special benefits received. However, under Colo. Const. art. II, § 15, the landowners were entitled to receive the value of which they were deprived and "just compensation" was not synonymous with a cash payment. The landowners received the compensation to which they were entitled. As a result of the taking, they were owed $ 1,323,691 for the property actually taken and $ 100,000 for damages to their remaining property, which came to a total of $ 1,423,691. As compensation, they received a cash payment of $ 1,126,691 and $ 297,000 in special benefits, for a total of $ 1,423,691 received. Thus, the landowners received the value of which they had been deprived. Therefore, the trial court's application of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 38-1-114(2)(d) did not the Colorado Constitution.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed and the case was remanded with directions.

 

151 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. M.B.


Consolidated Cases Nos. 04SA37, No. 04SA38, No. 04SA39, No. 04SA40, No. 04SA41


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


90 P.3d 880; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 413



May 24, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceedings Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. Case No. 03CR1669. Case No. 03CR1752. Case No. 03CR1588. Case No. 03CR1670. Case No. 03CR595. Weld County District Court. Honorable Gilbert A. Gutierrez, Judge



DISPOSITION: Rule made absolute.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, juveniles, were all adjudicated juvenile delinquents and charged with felony escape, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-208(2) (2003). The Weld County District Court, Colorado, found that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-210.1 (2003) violated Colo. Const. art. XVIII, § 4 by impermissibly modifying the definition of "felony." A rule to show cause was granted.


OVERVIEW: The trial court reasoned that adjudication as a juvenile did not qualify as a felony under the Colorado Constitution, and therefore a juvenile adjudication could not provide the basis for a subsequent felony charge under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-208(2). The reviewing court held that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-210.1 did not conflict with Colo. Const. art. XVIII, § 4, because the statute's sole effect was to criminalize escape when it was committed by an adjudicated juvenile delinquent who was confined because he committed an offense that would be a felony if committed by an adult. The power to criminalize such conduct lied firmly within the purview of the Colorado Legislature, and, absent a constitutional infirmity, there was no basis to interfere with that power, Colo. Const. art. V, § 1. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-210.1 did not re-classify adjudicated delinquents as felons. Rather, it authorized the State to bring felony escape charges against a sub-set of juvenile offenders who committed acts that, if committed by an adult, would have been a felony. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-8-210.1 did not deem this sub-set of adjudicated juvenile offenders felons.


OUTCOME: The ruled was made absolute.

 

152 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Ainscough v. Owens


No. 03SC164


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


90 P.3d 851; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 417; 175 L.R.R.M. 2025



May 24, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Case No. 03SC164. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA0436.
Ainscough v. Owens, 77 P.3d 761, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 121 (Colo. Ct. App., 2003),



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Court of Appeals reversed and case remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, state employees and their associations, filed suit against respondents, the Governor of Colorado and the Personnel Director in their official capacities, in an attempt to contest a new policy strictly limiting automatic payroll deductions. Respondents' motions to dismiss were granted, and the Colorado Court of Appeals upheld the trial court's dismissal of the case. Certiorari review was granted.


OVERVIEW: Colo. Rev. Stat. § 24-50-104(8) did not define which deductions were state-sponsored for all state employees, and thus statutorily entitled to automatic deductions. The Governor by Executive Order designated the Colorado Combined Campaign as the only entity state-sponsored for all state employees and thus, the only non-statutory program to be exempted from executive review and approval. In response to the Executive Order, the Personnel Director issued a payroll deduction policy, cancelling all automatic deductions except those authorized by statute or executive order and those repaying money owed to the state. The reviewing court held that petitioners had standing to challenge the payroll deduction policy. Petitioners alleged injuries sufficient to satisfy the injury-in-fact prong of the standing test when they alleged that they were using a state-provided service, but were now precluded from doing so by a change in government policy and practice. Also, petitioners were deprived of the right to even be considered for a payroll deduction, satisfying the legally protected interest prong of the standing test.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remand with directions to return the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

153 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Borer v. Lewis


Case No. 02SC808


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


91 P.3d 375; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 419



May 24, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Rehearing denied by Borer v. Lewis, 2004 Colo. LEXIS 500 (Colo., June 21, 2004).


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA1073.
Borer v. Lewis, 2002 Colo. App. LEXIS 1227 (Colo. Ct. App., July 25, 2002).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a negligent entrustment case arising from a collision, the trial court removed a default against defendant, finding that she proved by a preponderance of the evidence that process had never been served to her. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed, and plaintiffs sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Both the trial court and the court of appeals found that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-25-127(1), which required a preponderance standard in "civil cases," applied to a motion to set aside a default judgment and therefore replaced the common law burden of clear and convincing proof. The court disagreed. The term "civil action" was limited to substantive actions. A motion for the setting aside of a default judgment was procedural and therefore subject to the court's power to promulgate rules governing procedural matters. Thus, the common law standard, not § 13-25-127, applied. As a matter of judicial economy, the court addressed the issue of whether defendant had met the greater burden based on the trial court's factual findings and found that she had. Defendant presented clear, strong, and satisfactory proof that she was not served. The only evidence of service was a return signed by a deputy, who was inconsistent at best. Conversely, the trial court found no reason to doubt defendant's claim that she was not served. She had no incentive to deny service, given her insurance company's participation, and her insurance representative corroborated her testimony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower courts' rulings setting aside the default judgment against defendant.

 

154 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dunaway


Case No. 02SC675


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


88 P.3d 619; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 296



April 12, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 21, 2004.  [**1]
Rehearing denied by People v. Dunaway, 2004 Colo. LEXIS 350 (Colo., May 3, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 00CA1863.
People v. Dunaway, 2002 Colo. App. LEXIS 1007 (Colo. Ct. App., June 13, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for child abuse resulting in serious bodily injury under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-6-401(1)(7)(a)(III) (2003). The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed his conviction on the basis that the trial court instructed the jury on two alternative theories of culpability for the crime and the evidence did not support both of those theories beyond a reasonable doubt. The court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The jury was presented with evidence that defendant shook his girlfriend's baby, that he was aware of the seriousness of its resulting medical condition, and that he failed to seek or prevented medical treatment. He did not dispute the sufficiency of the evidence that he knowingly or recklessly caused serious bodily injury to the baby. There was also sufficient evidence, under the alternative theory on which the jury was charged, that, by failing to seek treatment, defendant permitted the baby to be unreasonably placed in a situation posing a threat of injury. However, the evidence was insufficient on that theory to find that the failure to seek treatment resulted in further injury. The court nevertheless affirmed the conviction, overruling a prior decision. The court held that, where the evidence was otherwise sufficient to support a determination of guilt, it did not violate the due process clause of Colo. Const. art. II, § 25 to provide the jury with an instruction containing a factually insufficient theory of liability for the same charged offense. The court also held that the decision to admit expert testimony on the force associated with subdural hematoma was not plain error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' opinion and remanded the case for reinstatement of the conviction.

156 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vasquez


No. 03SA218


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


84 P.3d 1019; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 66



February 9, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Rehearing denied by People v. Vasquez, 2004 Colo. LEXIS 114 (Colo., Mar. 1, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21. Adams County District Court, Case No. 02CR2231. Honorable Thomas Ensor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Rule to show cause made absolute; remanded to trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with first-degree murder. The State filed a notice of intent to seek the death penalty pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-1201(3) (2003). Defendant filed a notice of mental retardation, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-1102(1). The Adams County District Court, Colorado, declared the statute unconstitutional. The State filed a petition for a rule to show cause. The rule was issued, and the parties briefed the matter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's first-degree murder charge involved the death of his wife. The trial court declared Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-1102 unconstitutional, because it required defendant to prove mental retardation by clear and convincing evidence to the satisfaction of the trial court at a pre-trial proceeding. The trial court found that the statute impermissibly placed on defendant the burden of proving his mental retardation. To cure that problem, the trial court ordered the State to prove that defendant was not mentally retarded by a preponderance of the evidence. Defendant argued that the prohibition announced in the U.S. Supreme Court case of Atkins v. Virginia, could not be sustained by requiring him to bear the burden of proof concerning the fact of mental retardation. Defendant insisted that the State must prove his lack of mental retardation beyond a reasonable doubt. The reviewing court ruled that the standard of proof required under the statute was constitutionally permissible. Imposing upon defendant the burden of proving his mental retardation by clear and convincing evidence for the purpose of capital punishment offended no constitutional mandate.


OUTCOME: The rule to show cause was made absolute, and the case was remanded.

 

157 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Davidson v. Sandstrom


Case No. 03SC287


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


83 P.3d 648; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 49



January 26, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended March 22, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Pursuant to C.A.R. 50, Case No.: 03CA0485. Pueblo County District Court, Case No.: 02CV944. Honorable David A. Cole, Judge.
Davidson v. Sandstrom, 2003 Colo. LEXIS 476 (Colo., May 27, 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a district attorney, a board of county commissioners, and two voters, and petitioner Colorado Secretary of State filed a joint petition for a writ of certiorari to challenge a decision from the Pueblo County District Court (Colorado), which determined that district attorneys were subject to the term limits set forth in Colo. Const. art. XVIII, § 11(1), and that the board had authority to refer a term limit measure to the voters.


OVERVIEW: Colo. Const. art. XVIII, § 11(1) limited the term of certain officials in Colorado. The Secretary informed the district attorney that he was unable to run for office again because he had already been in office for 20 years. Thereafter, the district attorney, the board, and the voters filed a declaratory action against the Secretary. When the district court determined that the district attorney was subject to the term limits under § 11(1) and that the board was permitted to submit the matter to the voters, both parties sought review. In affirming, the court determined that district attorneys were "nonjudicial elected officials" within the meaning of § 11(1), and were subject to the term limits of § 11(1). The provision of § 11 which allowed the voters to modify or eliminate term limits for a particular office was self-executing, requiring no further action by the legislature for implementation. Thus, because the boundaries of the judicial district and the county were coextensive, with identical electors, the board validly referred a measure to the voters of the judicial district regarding the elimination of term limits for their district attorney.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

C.S. v. People (In the Interest of I.S.)


Consolidated Cases No. 03SC335, No. 03SC336


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


83 P.3d 627; 2004 Colo. LEXIS 48



January 26, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA2315. Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 02CA2316.
C.S. v. People ex rel. S.S., 2003 Colo. LEXIS 521 (Colo., June 16, 2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother's parental rights were terminated. The Colorado Court of Appeals dismissed the appeal. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by terminating her parental rights. The supreme court disagreed. Testimony showed that the mother had lived at 13 different places in a year's time, either in a motel or with friends or family. At the time of the hearing, the mother was not working and had not had stable employment since the case began - approximately two and a half years. The mother discontinued her drug and alcohol monitoring and her failure to obtain stable housing and employment represented a failure to comply with the recommendations of the psychological evaluation. Expert testimony established that the mother's permanent psychological state was such that she would likely never be emotionally intimate with her children and that she failed to demonstrate any ability to keep a safe, secure home for her children or herself. The mother's inability to trust others severely inhibited her capacity to seek the help she needed to become a fit parent. While the evidence showed that the mother tried to improve her situation, she had had little success during the crucial treatment period, which was the best indicator of the mother's future ability to parent.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

People v. Welsh


Case No. 02SC340


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


80 P.3d 296; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 961



December 8, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at People v. Welsh, 2007 Colo. App. LEXIS 608 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 98CA2167.
People v. Welsh, 58 P.3d 1065, 2002 Colo. App. LEXIS 566 (Colo. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' reversal of the defendant's conviction affirmed, and case remanded to the court of appeals and directed it to remand to the trial court with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People challenged a decision from the Colorado Court of Appeals, which reversed a lower court's decision to convict defendant of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed her boyfriend. At the crime scene and at the hospital, defendant refused to answer any questions regarding the shooting. At trial, defendant raised the defense of not guilty by reason of insanity. To prove that she was sane at the time of the crime under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-8-105.5(2) (2003), the People presented evidence of defendant's pre-arrest silence. Defendant sought review after she was convicted of first degree murder. An appellate court reversed the conviction, and the People sought review. In affirming the appellate court's decision, the court determined that evidence of defendant's pre-arrest silence was not admissible. The fact that defendant was unable to respond to questioning was so lacking in probative value that it was inadmissible under Colo. R. Evid. 401. She may have been unable to respond due to a lack of memory, confusion, disorientation, or the pain of her own gunshot wounds. Further, the unfair prejudicial impact far outweighed any possible probative value, rendering the evidence also inadmissible under Colo. R. Evid. 403. Finally, the admission of the evidence did not amount to a harmless error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision to reverse defendant's conviction. The case was remanded with directions to exclude all evidence of defendant's pre-arrest silence. The case was also remanded for a new trial.

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by COLORADO GENERAL ASSEMBLY v. KEN SALAZAR, ATTORNEY GENERAL OF COLORADO, ET AL., 2004 U.S. LEXIS 4029 (U.S., June 7, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to Colo. Constitution Art. VI, § 3.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state attorney general filed an original action pursuant to the Colo. Const. art. VI, § 3, asking for an injunction preventing respondent secretary of state from implementing the general assembly's 2003 redistricting plan and requesting a writ of mandamus requiring the secretary to return to the 2002 redistricting plan. The secretary filed her own original action, requesting dismissal of the attorney general's petition.


OVERVIEW: When the general assembly failed to redraw congressional districts after the latest census and before the upcoming election, the court settled on a new seven-district plan. After the election, the general assembly enacted 2003 Colo. Sess. Laws 1645, 1645-58, a new redistricting plan. The attorney general filed the instant action requesting an injunction to prevent implementation of the new plan. V, § 44 further limited that power to redistricting only after a decennial census, before the ensuing election, and at no other time.

161 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Goodale


Case No. 02SA345


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


78 P.3d 1103; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 885



November 3, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the District Court, Chaffee County, Case No. 02CR12. Honorable Kenneth M. Plotz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with unlawful use of marihuana in a detention facility, in violation of section Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-406.5 (2003). The District Court, Chaffee County (Colorado), found that the statute violated defendant's equal protection rights and granted defendant's motion to dismiss the prosecution. The People appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court agreed with defendant that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-406.5 (2003) was unconstitutional, as it imposed a higher punishment on marihuana users than it did on users of more serious drugs. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the lack of an addiction treatment option under § 18-18-406.5 did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Colo. Const. art. II, § 25, even though the treatment option was available for users of drugs considered to be more dangerous to society. Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-404 (2002), which provided the treatment option, specifically excluded marihuana offenses. Therefore, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-406.5 (2003) was directly related to the legislature's purpose of punishing those who used marihuana in a detention facility. Defendant failed to show that the statute was based on an arbitrary distinction not based on real differences. The court noted that symmetry of punishment was not required. The court also noted that the classification of treating marihuana crimes differently was rationally based on real differences between the substances. Therefore, the lack of a treatment option for marihuana offenses was not completely arbitrary or irrational.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling and remanded the case for further proceedings.

In re Matter of Atty. E.


Case No. 01SA404


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


78 P.3d 300; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 872



October 14, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Atty. E v. Third Party Movants, 157 L. Ed. 2d 888, 124 S. Ct. 1042, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 48 (U.S., Jan. 12, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding in Discipline Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 and C.R.C.P. 251(d). Case No. 01PDJ098. Honorable Roger L. Keithley, Presiding Disciplinary Judge.



DISPOSITION: Rule made absolute in part and discharged in part; remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: While investigating misconduct allegations against respondent attorney, petitioner Attorney Regulation Counsel (ARC) subpoenaed Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) documents and provided copies to the attorney. The attorney publicly disclosed the documents, which implicated third parties named in the documents. The presiding disciplinary judge (PDJ) issued a protective order, and both the ARC and the attorney sought to have the order vacated.


OVERVIEW: On review, the ARC argued that the order misconstrued the ARC's obligations when the investigative subpoena was issued, that it overextended the concept of confidentiality in Colo. R. Civ. P. 251.31, and that it hindered the ARC's investigation of the attorney. The attorney also filed a motion for leave to intervene in support of the ARC's petition, arguing that the protective order violated his First Amendment right to free speech. The supreme court held that the attorney, who was the target of the investigation, was a "party" to the investigative proceedings. As such, the ARC had to provide the attorney with notice of the subpoenas and copies of the subpoenaed documents. The PDJ's protective order directing that the documents subpoenaed from the FBI be treated as confidential was proper. The supreme court further held that, under both the two-pronged balancing test of Seattle Times and the "good cause" showing required under Colo. R. Civ. P. 251.31(e), the protective order did not violate the attorney's First Amendment rights. However, the protective order had to be modified, as it unduly hindered both the ARC and attorney's ability to further their respective cases.


OUTCOME: The issuance of the protective order was affirmed, but the case was remanded to the presiding disciplinary judge with directions to modify the protective order.

Campbell v. Hobbs (In re Title, Ballot Title and Submission Clause for 2003-2004)


Consolidated Cases: Case No. 03SA115, Case No. 03SA116


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


76 P.3d 460; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 760



September 8, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to § 1-40-107(2), 1 C.R.S. (2002) Appeal from the Title Board.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challengers filed an action for review of a decision from respondent Colorado Title Board, which set the title, ballot title, submission clause, and summary for two initiatives. The challengers also sought review of the Board's refusal to set titles for two other initiatives.


OVERVIEW: Four initiatives were proposed to the Board. The purpose of the initiatives was to liberalize the procedure for initiative and referendum petitions. They contained a provision that excluded lawyers from membership on the Board. The Board refused to set title for two of the initiatives after finding that they violated Colo. Const. art. V, § 1(5.5) because they contained more than one subject. Titles were set for the other two initiatives after the wording was changed. The challengers then filed an action to challenge the Board's decision. On review, the court determined that all four initiatives violated § 1(5.5) because they contained more than one subject. However, the court based its decision on broader grounds. The court found that the initiatives limited the substantive political rights of all attorneys. This right was impacted in situations in which one specifically identifiable group of individuals was prohibited from seeking to enact protective legislation. The prohibition did not have a necessary or proper connection to the purpose of the proposed measures. As such, the Board should not have set titles on any of the proposed initiatives.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's refusal to set titles for two initiatives. However, the court reversed the Board's decision to set titles for the other initiatives. The case was remanded with directions to strike the titles set and to return the four initiatives.

 

164 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mojica-Simental


No. 02SC44


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


73 P.3d 15; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 529



June 23, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended July 21, 2003.
Modified and rehearing denied by People v. Mojica-Simental, 2003 Colo. LEXIS 588 (Colo., July 21, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Pueblo County District Court. Case No. 01CV630. Honorable Joyce S. Steinhardt, Judge.
People v. Mojica-Simental, 2002 Colo. LEXIS 583 (Colo., July 22, 2002)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a DUI case, the Pueblo County District Court (Colorado) ruled that blood test results would not be admitted without the testimony of a lab technician. The county court also held Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5)(2002) was not applicable in a trial for a DUI charge. On appeal, the district court ruled that the statute applied to all criminal cases where laboratory tests were required. The State petitioned for certiorari review.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of Colorado granted certiorari in this DUI case to consider two issues: first, whether Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-3-309(5)(2002), which allowed criminalistics laboratory reports to be admitted without the testimony of the lab technician, applied in a driving under the influence (DUI) case; and second, whether § 16-3-309(5) was constitutional. The court concluded that § 16-3-309(5) did apply in a DUI case and was constitutional on its face, as it did not infringe upon a defendant's confrontation rights. Under § 16-3-309(5), a defendant wishing to confront the technician who prepared the report could do so by notifying the prosecution, at least 10 days before trial, that he wanted the technician present at trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed, as to application of the statute allowing criminalistics laboratory reports to be admitted at trial without the testimony of the lab technician in a DUI proceeding. The judgment was reversed, as to finding that the statute was unconstitutional. The case was remanded for proceedings consistent with the opinion.

 

165 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Campbell v. People


Case No. 02SC454


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


73 P.3d 11; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 531



June 23, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Rehearing denied by Campbell v. People, 2003 Colo. LEXIS 592 (Colo., July 21, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 01CA0446.
People v. Campbell, 58 P.3d 1080, 2002 Colo. App. LEXIS 736 (Colo. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to review the decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals, which upheld the trial court's rejection of defendant's argument that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-401(1)(a) (2002) violated equal protection. Defendant had pled guilty to possession of cocaine.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court granted certiorari to review the appellate court's decision. The trial court rejected defendant's argument that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-405(1)(a) (2002), prohibiting possession of a controlled substance, violated equal protection because it prescribed a harsher sentence than Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-404(1) (2002), prohibiting use of a controlled substance. Defendant had pled guilty to possession of cocaine. The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's decision, stating that defendant's sentence under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-405(1)(a), the possession statute, did not offend equal protection. Section 18-18-405(1)(a), prohibiting possession of a controlled substance, and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-18-404, prohibiting use of a controlled substance, did not describe or punish identical conduct. Furthermore, possession and use of a controlled substance were not identical conduct for equal protection purposes, even when the drugs possessed and used were the same.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
