In re Atty. E.


No. 01SA404


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


73 P.3d 648; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 492



June 16, 2003, Decided


NOTICE:

 [**1]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended July 2, 2003.
Modified and rehearing denied by In re Matter of Investigation of Atty. E., 2003 Colo. LEXIS 578 (Colo., July 2, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding in Discipline Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 and C.R.C.P. 251(d). Case No. 01PDJ098. Honorable Roger L. Keithley, Presiding Disciplinary Judge.



DISPOSITION:  [**2] Opinion modified and, as modified, Petition for Rehearing DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a discipline proceeding, the Colorado Attorney Regulation Counsel (ARC) investigated allegations regarding respondent attorney. Both matters concerned, in part, statements the attorney made, asserting that two Colorado judges had purchased and used cocaine. The presiding disciplinary judge (PDJ) issued a protective order to prevent public disclosure of discovery materials. The ARC and the attorney petitioned to have the order vacated.


OVERVIEW: A witness stated that the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) had investigated both judges, and had evidence of the judges' cocaine use. Because the attorney was entitled to the documents themselves as a "party" to the proceedings, ARC forwarded him copies that had been redacted by the FBI. The attorney then publicly distributed the FBI documents, by filing them in various courts of record. The appellate court held that the attorney, who was the target of the investigation, was a "party" to the investigative proceedings. Because Colorado rules governing discovery by the ARC incorporated certain provisions of the rules of civil procedure, the ARC had to provide the target attorney with notice of the subpoenas and copies of the subpoenaed documents. However, the PDJ's protective order limiting further dissemination of the documents involving the two judges was appropriate. Under Seattle Times and the "good cause" showing required under Colo. R. Civ. P. 251.31(e), the protective order did not violate the attorney's First Amendment rights. The PDJ was required to modify the protective order to allow the limited use of the FBI documents by both parties, the attorney and the ARC.


OUTCOME: The rule was made absolute in part and discharged in part, and the case was remanded to the disciplinary judge with directions to modify the protective order consistent with the appellate court's opinion.

 

167 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Lake Durango Water Co. v. PUC


Case No. 02SA100


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


67 P.3d 12; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 303



April 7, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified April 28, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the La Plata County District Court Case No. 01CV32. Honorable Gregory G. Lyman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water company appealed the judgment of the La Plata County District Court (Colorado), which affirmed an award by appellee Colorado Public Utilities Commission (PUC) of attorney fees and costs to appellee metropolitan water district for expenses incurred during related proceedings between the district and company.


OVERVIEW: The company argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by improperly determining that it breached the settlement agreement. The supreme court disagreed. The plain language of the contract identified the behavior of each party in relation to when the company filed its tariffs. Therefore it was not necessary that the agreement specifically stated that the tariffs must be filed. The language of the agreement did not say "if" the company filed tariffs, the district could not object to an application for an extension of time in which to do so. Rather, the district agreed not to object to an extension of time up to December 31, 1996, within which the company would file tariffs. The company breached the settlement agreement, as it failed to timely file the tariffs. Further, the company could not rely on the agreement, as it repudiated the agreement by informing the district that it would not be filing tariffs at all. The company materially breached the contract not only by seeking another extension of time beyond the December 31, 1996, deadline, but also by filing the tariffs beyond the December 31, 1996, deadline. Therefore, the trial court did not err.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed as to the issue of when interest began to accrue on the attorney fees and costs. The case was remanded with directions to correct the judgment on the interest due on the award of attorney fees and costs. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

168 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Whiteside v. Smith


Case No. 01SA399


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


67 P.3d 1240; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 305



April 7, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the District Court, City & County of Denver, Case No. 00CV9120. Honorable John N. McMullen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court, City and County of Denver, Colorado, granted judgment in favor of appellee, an injured indigent employee, in his suit against appellants, Director of the Division of Workers' Compensation and the Examiners of the Industrial Claim Appeals Office, claiming that the Workers' Compensation Act was unconstitutional under the due process and equal protection clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court certified a class of injured indigent employees and struck down Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 8-42-107(8)(b)(II), (8)(c), 8-42-107.2(5) (2002), the fee provisions of the Colorado Workers' Compensation Act (Act), Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 8-40-101 et seq., and 7 Colo. Code Regs. 1101-3 as unconstitutional on due process and equal protection grounds. The appellate court determined that, once the employer admitted liability, the Act entitled the employee to property interests that included temporary benefits and medical treatment. The fee requirement violated the Fourteenth Amendment, as the employees were denied any opportunity to be heard before they were deprived of the benefits and treatment. There was a high degree of hardship imposed by the termination on indigent employees. The group got no opportunity for an independent evaluation, no benefit of the statutory presumptions accorded to the evaluation, no administrative hearing, no administrative appeals, and no right to appeal to the court system. It was unclear how providing evidentiary hearings to indigent employees to protect their statutory property rights imposed additional costs or burdens on the State.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

169 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Borghesi


No. 01SC479


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


66 P.3d 93; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 257



March 24, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]
Modified and rehearing denied by People v. Borghesi, 2003 Colo. LEXIS 329 (Colo., Apr. 14, 2003)
Judgment entered by, On remand at People v. Borghesi, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 969 (Colo. Ct. App., June 19, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 99CA1358.
People v. Borghesi, 40 P.3d 15, 2001 Colo. App. LEXIS 350 (Colo. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of three counts of aggravated robbery. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed one of the convictions on the grounds that two of the charges were multiplicitous. The State appealed. Defendant cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court properly dismissed one of the counts as multiplicitous, as he committed only one aggravated robbery during the first incident, as there was only one violent taking of property from a common owner. The supreme court disagreed. Both clerks, who were counting the money in the cash register, had sufficient control over the store's property when defendant brandished a hatchet and demanded money from the register. Defendant directed threats to both employees when he sought and obtained control over the store's money. While it was the second clerk who handed over the money, the property was taken from the presence of the first clerk, who would have been able to retain control over it but for the threats by defendant to the second clerk. Because the common property taken from the second clerk was also taken from the presence of another, the first clerk, the robber's acts constituted different offenses as to each victim. Therefore, the judgments of conviction for both clerks were not multiplicitous, as defendant was being punished for two separate offenses, or the robbery of each clerk, and not for the same offense.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed on the issue of defendant's multiple counts and convictions arising from a single incident. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

170 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Colo. Dep't of Revenue v. Garner


No. 02SC235


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


66 P.3d 106; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 258



March 24, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 01CA0259.
Colo. Dep't of Revenue v. Garner, 53 P.3d 763, 2002 Colo. App. LEXIS 259 (Colo. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Colorado Department of Revenue, challenged the judgment of the Colorado Court of Appeals, holding that respondent, driver, had to be convicted of a driving offense committed while the driver's operating license was revoked before the Department could extend the driver's license revocation under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-2-138(3) (2002).


OVERVIEW: The driver failed to appear at the scheduled revocation hearing, and the Department revoked his license for three months. During this time, the driver was driving when another vehicle rear-ended him. The driver was ticketed for driving under restraint, and at the end of the initial revocation period, the Department issued the driver a new driver's license. However, after receiving the accident report, the Department revoked the driver's operating license for an additional year. The appellate court construed Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-2-138(3) to prohibit the department from revoking the license of a driver who drove while his license was under restraint. The appellate court required, as a condition for extension or renewal of revocation, that the driver be actually convicted of another driving offense or violation. The reviewing court ruled that the Department, upon receiving the accident report for a person who was driving under revocation, could impose an additional period of revocation under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 42-2-138(3), after finding that the person was driving under restraint at the time of the accident.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

171 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hawes v. Colo. Div. of Ins.


Case No. 01SC743


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


65 P.3d 1008; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 150



March 3, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Hawes v. Colo. Div. of Ins., 2003 Colo. LEXIS 306 (Colo., Apr. 7, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 00CA1144.
Hawes v. Colo. Div. of Ins., 45 P.3d 763, 2001 Colo. App. LEXIS 1519 (Colo. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals, the appellate court affirmed the Colorado Commissioner of Insurance's decision denying petitioner attorneys' requests for attorney fees, as the Commissioner determined that he had neither express nor implied statutory authority to award fees. The attorneys appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 10-16-324(4)(e) accomplished the equivalent of the cy pres doctrine, and, as such, during the conversion of a nonprofit health care insurer to a for-profit corporation, the Commissioner presided over proceedings that were equitable in nature and served as a fiduciary for the funds transferred to a private charitable corporation. Because the legislature empowered the Commissioner to do whatever was necessary to accomplish the conversion, the supreme court held that the Commissioner could award attorney fees under the common fund doctrine, where there was no legislative abrogation of the common law right; the legislature did not otherwise prohibit an award of fees; and the participation of the attorneys, representing the general public interest, was necessary to effectuate the conversion. On remand, the Commissioner had to determine: whether the attorneys' efforts were necessary to effectuate the conversion and substantially responsible for enhancing the consideration paid; whether a percentage-based award of fees was appropriate; and whether the attorneys should receive compensation for nonpecuniary benefits to the conversion process.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the case to the appellate court for return to the Commissioner for further proceedings.

 

172 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Woldt v. People


Case No. 97SA193, Case No. 97SA392, Consolidated Cases


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


64 P.3d 256; 2003 Colo. LEXIS 118



February 24, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Colorado v. Woldt, 157 L. Ed. 2d 251, 124 S. Ct. 85, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 7312 (U.S., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the District Court, El Paso County, Case No. 97CR1563. Sentencing Order and Judgment by Honorable Richard V. Hall, Honorable Douglas E. Anderson, and Honorable Larry E. Schwartz, Judges. Appeal from the District Court, Jefferson County, Case No. 97CR1699. Sentencing Order and Judgment by Honorable Michael C. Villano, Honorable Robert S. Hyatt, and Honorable Deanna E. Hickman, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Death sentence judgments reversed; cases remanded for resentencing to life imprisonment without parole.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted in separate proceedings in the District Courts of El Paso County and Jefferson County, Colorado, of first-degree murder and other offenses. Each defendant was sentenced to death by a three-judge panel. Defendants' appeals were consolidated for review.


OVERVIEW: Each defendant was convicted of the charged offenses by a jury. A three-judge panel then followed the four-step procedure set forth in former Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-11-103 (2002) (repealed 2002) and, after weighing aggravating and mitigating factors, unanimously sentenced each defendant to death. The appellate court held that the use of a three-judge panel to impose a death sentence was unconstitutional under the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Ring v. Arizona, which held that only a jury could find an aggravating circumstance necessary to raise the maximum punishment for an offense from life to death. The death sentences had to be reversed. The court could not use the discretion granted to it by Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.4-102 (2002) to remand the cases to the trial courts for resentencing before a jury because Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1-105(5) (2002) (now Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-1.3-405 (2002)), which was in effect at the time of the original sentencing, mandated imposition of a life sentence without parole. To do otherwise would violate the Ex Post Facto Clauses of the United States and Colorado Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The death sentences were reversed and the cases were remanded to the trial courts for imposition of life sentences without parole.

 

173 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Hill v. DeWitt (In re Estate of DeWitt)


Case No. 01SC136, Case No. 01SC248


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


54 P.3d 849; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 778



September 9, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 99CA1349. Court of Appeals Case No. 99CA2251.
 Becker v. Becker (In re Estate of Becker), 32 P.3d 557, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 2155 (Ct. App. 2000) Hill v. DeWitt (In re Estate of DeWitt), 32 P.3d 550, 20600 Colo. App. LEXIS 2156 (Ct. App. 2000)



DISPOSITION: Case No. 01SC136 JUDGMENT REVERSED. Case No. 01SC248 JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, petitioners, former spouses of the decedents, sought a writ of certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals challenging its finding in favor of respondent estates in the application of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-11-804(2).


OVERVIEW: The former spouses argued that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-11-804(2) did not apply to revoke revocable rights granted during a marriage that dissolved before the statute became effective and one ex-spouse died after that effective date. The appellate court found that the plain language of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-17-102 (2001) indicated the legislature's intent that the death of a decedent on or after the date the statute became effective triggered its application, notwithstanding that the insurance contract may have been entered into, and the divorce may have occurred, before the statute's effective date; the statute would be retroactive under such circumstances. The retroactive application of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 15-11-804(2) was not retrospective with regard to the beneficiaries' interests. The decedents could expect that their life insurance policies would be regulated by statute. The spouses did not have a contract clause claim because they were not parties to the contract, but third-party beneficiaries. Because the statute only addressed the donative aspect of the insurance contract, the contract between the decedents and the insurance companies was not impaired.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in one case and reversed in the other.

 

174 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stewart


No. 00SC672


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


55 P.3d 107; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 774



September 9, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion modified, and as modified, Petition for Rehearing Denied September 23, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Case No. 98CA838.
 People v. Stewart, 26 P.3d 17, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 1084 (Ct. App. 2000).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of reckless second degree assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-3-203(1)(d) (2001). The Colorado Court of Appeals held that the conviction violated defendant's equal protection rights under Colo. Const. art. II, § 25, because it imposed a more severe penalty for the same behavior proscribed by the vehicular assault statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-3-205(1)(a) (2001). The court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of reckless second degree assault after he ran over the victim with his vehicle, which put the victim in a coma. The court found no equal protection violation because the court concluded that the statutes for reckless second degree assault and vehicular assault proscribed different conduct. The vehicular assault statute was limited to acts concerning a vehicle, and both statutes differed in terms of causation. The trial court did not commit reversible error in failing to give the jury an intervening cause instruction because the facts did not warrant the instruction. The act of defendant in driving forward and running over the victim's head was the actus reus for which defendant was convicted, and the victim's prior act of jumping on the car did not cause defendant's subsequent act of running over him. While the trial court erred in allowing an investigating officer to testify as an expert without complying with Colo. R. Evid. 702, the error was harmless because the officer's testimony was corroborated and did not result in any unfairness in the trial. The trial court was entitled to consider defendant's appeal bond without waiting for a limited remand.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment that defendant's conviction violated his equal protection rights, and reinstated the conviction. The court affirmed the appellate court's decision that the appeal bond was properly heard by the trial court without a limited remand.

Karr v. Williams


Case No. 02SA18


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


50 P.3d 910; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 570



July 1, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied: Williams v. Karr, 154 L. Ed. 2d 812, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 158 (U.S. 2003).


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Original Proceeding Pursuant to C.A.R. 21 El Paso County District Court Case No. 01CV2030 Honorable Richard V. Hall, Judge Case No. 02SA18.



DISPOSITION: Party enjoined from proceeding pro se as a plaintiff in pending or future litigation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent inmate sued petitioner individuals in district court. In a separate original proceeding, the individuals sought an injunction to prevent the inmate from continuing his district court action against the individuals in pro per.


OVERVIEW: The inmate claimed that he was indigent and used the judicial process to engage in a campaign of harassment and intimidation spanning more than 20 years. He repeatedly filed frivolous, vexatious lawsuits against his adversaries in an attempt to either gain leverage over them or exact vengeance on them. The Colorado Supreme Court held that: (1) the inmate was enjoined from proceeding as a proponent of any claim in the courts of the State of Colorado without the representation of an attorney licensed to practice law in Colorado, unless he had first obtained permission to proceed pro se; and (2) as to the cases presently pending, respondent was ordered obtain permission to proceed in pro per within 45 days or the court in which the action was pending could dismiss the action.


OUTCOME: An injunction was issued.

 

176 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Trujillo


Case No. 01SC434


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


49 P.3d 316; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 569



July 1, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Court of Appeals Case No. 98CA2575.
 People v. Trujillo, 30 P.3d 760, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 2213 (Ct. App. 2000).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Jefferson County District Court, Colorado, for the crime of violating bail bond conditions. He was adjudicated a habitual criminal and sentenced to imprisonment for six years. The Colorado Court of Appeals reversed the conviction. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant failed to appear at his trial, and a warrant was issued for his arrest. He was thereafter arrested in another county on the warrant. While in custody, and without being given Miranda warnings, defendant made incriminating statements that he knew there was a warrant for his arrest. At trial, defendant did not testify, but his wife testified he possessed poor cognitive ability and therefore did not know about the hearing. The State impeached defendant's wife's statement with defendant's incriminating statements. Defendant was convicted, and the court of appeals reversed, holding the admission of his custodial statements was constitutional error. The supreme court initially noted when impeaching with a prior inconsistent statement, it was axiomatic that the prior inconsistent statements needed to belong to the testifying witness. The supreme court held a defendant's voluntary, unwarned, custodial statements could only be used to impeach the defendant, if he testified at trial, and, if the defendant did not testify at trial, such unwarned custodial statements could not be used either to rebut a defense theory or to impeach a witness other than the defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

HealthONE v. Rodriguez


Case No. 00SC772


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


50 P.3d 879; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 529



June 24, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 98CA2173.
 Rodriguez v. HealthONE, 24 P.3d 9, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 935 (Ct. App. 2000).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals in a medical malpractice action, the Colorado State Supreme Court granted certiorari review to resolve several legal questions.


OVERVIEW: A patient sustained injuries while he was receiving treatment at a hospital. He initiated a medical malpractice action against the hospital and two physicians as he was left incapacitated. Before trial, the patient settled with his treating physician. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the non-treating physician. Following trial against the hospital, the jury rendered judgment in favor of the patient. The state supreme court granted certiorari to review several legal issues in the case. The state supreme court concluded as follows: (1) The patient did not waive his right to appeal by accepting attorney's fees as a result of the judgment against the hospital; (2) the non-treating physician still owed the patient a common law duty of reasonable care; and (3) Colo. Rev. Stat. § 13-64-205, which provided that the patient, as an incapacitated person, could not elect to receive future medical and non-economic damages in a lump-sum payment, did not violate equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed in part, and reversed in part.

 

178 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Close v. People


Case No. 00SC690


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


48 P.3d 528; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 428



May 28, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion Modified, and as Modified, Petition For Rehearing Denied. En Banc July 1, 2002.
Modified and rehearing denied by Close v. People, 2002 Colo. LEXIS 547 (Colo., July 1, 2002)
Subsequent appeal at People v. Close, 2006 Colo. App. LEXIS 545 (Colo. Ct. App., Apr. 20, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 97CA1863.
People v. Deroulet, 48 P.3d 520, 2002 Colo. LEXIS 426 (Colo., 2002)
People v. Close, 22 P.3d 933, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 851 (Colo. Ct. App., 2000)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was sentenced pursuant to the crime of violence statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-11-309(1)(a) (2001). He requested a proportionality review of his sentence. The trial court declined to conduct a proportionality review of defendant's 60-year sentence. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed because the sentence imposed was the minimum allowable by statute. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: After reviewing federal and state case law discussing the issue of proportionality review of a defendant's sentence, the supreme court concluded that a criminal defendant who was sentenced under the crime of violence statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-11-309(1)(a) (2001), was entitled, upon request, to an abbreviated proportionality review of his sentences. If, and only if, that abbreviated proportionality review gave rise to an inference of gross disproportionality would an extended proportionality review be required. Given the gravity and seriousness of the crimes included in the crime of violence statute, the necessity of conducting an extended proportionality review and thus a successful challenge to a sentence imposed under that statute would be exceedingly rare. The supreme court found that the trial court and the court of appeals were authorized to conduct a proportionality review of defendant's sentences; however, the supreme court held that the sentences imposed were not grossly disproportionate to the offenses and affirmed the judgments and sentences imposed.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

 

179 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Deroulet


Case No. 00SC626


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


48 P.3d 520; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 426



May 28, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion Modified, and as Modified, Petition For Rehearing Denied. En Banc July 1, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 98CA715.
 People v. Deroulet, 22 P.3d 939, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 999, 2000 Colo. J. C. A. R. 3180 (Ct. App. 2000).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree burglary, third-degree assault, theft, harassment, and two counts of being a habitual criminal. He was to be sentenced under the habitual criminal statute. The trial court conducted a proportionality review and imposed a reduced sentence. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed. Certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court granted the State certiorari to determine whether defendant was entitled, upon request, to a proportionality review of his sentence under Colorado's habitual criminal statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 16-13-101, as amended in 1993, and, if so, whether the sentencing court erred in reducing defendant's sentence. The supreme court held that a defendant was entitled, upon request, to an abbreviated proportionality review of a sentence under the habitual criminal statute as amended in 1993, even when the sentence imposed was less than a life sentence. Thus, in the instant case, defendant was entitled to an abbreviated proportionality review. However, the statutorily mandated sentence was not unconstitutionally disproportionate and, therefore, the trial court erred in reducing defendant's sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

180 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Middleton v. Hartman


Case No. 00SC809


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


45 P.3d 721; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 294; 146 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P34,539



April 15, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion Modified, and as Modified, Petition For Rehearing Denied. En Banc May 13, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 98 CA1633. Hartman v. Regents of the Univ. of Colorado, 22 P.3d 524, 2000 Colo. App. LEXIS 1177 (Ct. App. 2000) 



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sued defendant university and defendant university officials, alleging that defendants retaliated against her for exercising her rights under the Fair Labor and Standards Act (FLSA), 29 U.S.C.S. § 215. The Colorado Court of Appeals held that the university officials did not enjoy sovereign immunity when sued in their individual capacities for violating the FLSA retaliatory provisions. The officials appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether the notice-of-claim provisions of the Colorado Governmental Immunity Act (CGIA), applied to a claim against the university officials sued in their individual capacities for retaliation under FLSA. The officials argued that the employee's FLSA retaliation claim was barred by sovereign immunity because it was really a claim against the state and the officials were only nominal defendants. The employee argued that she sued the officials in their individual capacities for their willful and wanton retaliatory conduct, and not as nominal defendants. The employee claimed that the officials did not enjoy sovereign immunity. The instant court held that the officials did not enjoy sovereign immunity because they were sued in their individual capacities and relief was sought from them personally. The CGIA's notice-of-claim provisions were intended to apply to such claims despite the lack of sovereign immunity. However, because the FLSA preempted the notice-of-claim provisions, the notice-of-claim provisions did not apply to the employee's claim against the officials in their individual capacities for retaliation under the FLSA.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's decision was affirmed.

 

181 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Tattered Cover v. City of Thornton


Case No. 01SA205


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


44 P.3d 1044; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 269; 30 Media L. Rep. 1656



April 8, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Modified and as Modified, Petition For Rehearing Denied. En Banc. Justice Coats does Not Participate. April 29, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Transfer from the Colorado Court of Appeals, Case No. 00CA2150. District Court, City and County of Denver, Case No. 00CV1761. Honorable J. Stephen Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bookstore brought suit, seeking to restrain defendant city's police from executing a search warrant of the bookstore. The District Court of the City and County of Denver, Colorado, granted a restraining order with respect to a request for a criminal suspect's 30-day purchasing history, but it allowed the police to discover information related to a mailing envelope found in the suspects' garbage. The bookstore appealed.


OVERVIEW: This case required the instant court to decide what test should be applied to balance the constitutional rights of individuals and bookstores against the duty of law enforcement officials to investigate crime. The bookstore argued that it should not be required to disclose any information regarding customer purchase records. The city argued that the information sought was necessary: (1) to prove that the operator of an illicit drug lab acted with the level of intent necessary to secure a conviction; (2) to prove the identity of the perpetrator; and (3) to "connect" a suspect to the drug lab. The instant court held that the Colorado Constitution required that the bookstore, as an innocent bookseller, be afforded an opportunity for an adversarial hearing prior to execution of a search warrant seeking customer purchase records. Applying a balancing test, the instant court concluded that the search warrant in this case was not enforceable and should not have issued. The city failed to demonstrate that its need for the customer purchase record was sufficiently compelling to outweigh the harm that would have been caused to constitutional interests, if the search warrant were executed.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the judgment of the district court, with respect to the police request for information related to the books mailed in the envelope found in the suspects' garbage. The case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the instant court's opinion.

Beauprez v. Avalos


Case No. 02SC87


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


42 P.3d 642; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 224



March 13, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified April 26, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Pursuant to C.A.R. 50, Case No. 02CA253. District Court, City & County of Denver, Case No. 01CV2897.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, members of the State Democratic Party, petitioned for certiorari review of the decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals, affirming adoption of the state redistricting plan.


OVERVIEW: With the results of the 2000 census, Colorado became entitled to a new seat in the United States House of Representatives. The State Democratic Party filed an action in the district court to begin the court process of redistricting. The district court allowed seventeen parties to intervene. The district court issued its ruling adopting a map created by Republican leaders. Members of the Democratic Party appealed, alleging the Republican map violated equal protection rights. On review, the state supreme court considered whether the redistricting map in effect diluted minority voting strength contrary to the Voting Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971 (1988). Upon determining the case was ripe for adjudication, the state supreme court determined that the process utilized by the district court in adopting a redistricting plan was thorough, inclusive, and non-partisan. The approximately 3 percent drop in the Hispanic population of District 1 did not give rise to a finding of unconstitutional voter dilution.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

184 of 734 DOCUMENTS


SL Group, LLC v. Go West Industries, Inc.


Case No. 00SA398


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


42 P.3d 637; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 222



March 11, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal From The District Court, Water Division 4, 98CW23. Honorable J. Steven Patrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff and defendant owned adjoining properties that once comprised an undivided parcel. Defendant was granted an absolute surface water right, based upon an historical appropriation for irrigation. Thereafter, plaintiff filed a verified petition for reconsideration to correct substantive errors in the judgment. The District Court, Water Division 4 (Colorado), dismissed the petition without reconsidering the merits. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: A resume of defendant's application was published in a local newspaper and mailed to the U.S. Forest Service, which defendant identified as the owner of the land at the point where creek water was diverted to fill a stock pond. No statements of opposition were filed. A year and a half later, plaintiff, claiming to be a party entitled to receive mailed notice of the action, filed its petition alleging: (1) its failure to protest the application was due to mistake, inadvertence, or excusable neglect; (2) the legal description of the historically irrigated property submitted by defendant included plaintiff's property; and (3) the stock pond was actually located on plaintiff's property. The supreme court held that it was unnecessary to determine whether the due process requirement of reasonable notice was satisfied to give the water court personal jurisdiction over plaintiff. Under the circumstances, plaintiff's failure to protest in a timely manner was due to mistake, inadvertence, or excusable neglect under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 37-92-304 (2001). Plaintiff's petition sufficiently alleged that its rights were adversely affected by substantive errors.


OUTCOME: The supreme court held that the water court abused its discretion in dismissing the plaintiff's petition for reconsideration, reversed the order of the water court, and remanded.

 

185 of 734 DOCUMENTS


City of Commerce City v. State


Case No. 01SC281


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


40 P.3d 1273; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 140



February 11, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals Pursuant to C.A.R. 50, 01CA144. District Court, City and County of Denver. Case No. 98CV1098. Honorable Paul A. Markson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners home-rule municipalities challenged whether certain provisions of Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 42-4-110.5 and 42-3-112(14) (2001) regulating the use of automated vehicle identification systems (AVIS), popularly known as photo radar and photo red light, unconstitutionally infringed on their powers under Colo. Const. art. XX, §6. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of the provisions. The municipalities appealed by writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Affirming the trial court, the supreme court held that the regulation of AVIS to enforce traffic laws was a matter of mixed local and state concern, and it held that the challenged provisions of §§ 42-4-110.5 and 42-3-112(14) were not superseded by the cities' local ordinances or charters. The constitutionality of the challenged provisions were upheld. Without uniform state legislation, drivers might have been subject to a significant variety of conflicting local legislation, further increasing the potential for confusion and substantially affecting their expectations. Important to the supreme court's uniformity analysis was the fact that cities wishing to ticket traffic violators using automated systems must access those drivers' names and addresses through the state motor vehicle registration office. Thus, a certain level of cooperation between a city and the state was necessary to make the system work. The need for cooperation between cities and the state was an important factor in the supreme court's analysis. Thus, the regulation of automated vehicle identification systems affected the residents of the state as a whole, as opposed to simply affecting local residents.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

In re Reapportionment of the Colo. Gen. Assembly


Case No. 01SA386


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


45 P.3d 1237; 2002 Colo. LEXIS 115



January 28, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Colorado Reapportionment Commission's action set aside, apportionment plan disapproved and remanded for reconsideration and resubmission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Colorado State Supreme Court reviewed the decennial Apportionment Plan approved by the Colorado Reapportionment Commission for the reapportionment of Colorado General Assembly house and senate districts, based on the year 2000 federal census.


OVERVIEW: In this original proceeding under Colo. Const. art. V, § 48 (1)(e), the state supreme court reviewed the decennial Apportionment Plan (Adopted Plan) the Colorado Reapportionment Commission (Commission) approved for the reapportionment of Colorado General Assembly house and senate districts, based on the year 2000 federal census. Upon its review, the appellate court held that the Adopted Plan did not comply with the criteria of Colo. Const. art. V, §§ 46, 47 because: (1) it was not sufficiently attentive to county boundaries to meet the requirement of § 47(2); and (2) was not accompanied by an adequate factual showing that less drastic alternatives could not have satisfied the equal population requirement of the Colorado Constitution. The Commission failed to advance an adequate explanation for division of Adams, Arapahoe, and Mesa counties and the cities of Boulder and Pueblo between Senate Districts.


OUTCOME: The Colorado Reapportionment Commission's action was set aside, the Adopted Plan was remanded, and the matter was returned to the Commission for reconsideration and resubmission of a reapportionment plan that complied with the substantive and procedural requirements of the Colorado Constitution.

Empire Lodge Homeowners' Ass'n v. Moyer


Case No. 00SA211


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


39 P.3d 1139; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 1061



December 17, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion modified, and as modified, Petition for Rehearing DENIED. EN BANC. February 11, 2002.
Modified and rehearing denied by Empire Lodge Homeowners' Ass'n v. Moyer, 2002 Colo. LEXIS 136 (Colo., Feb. 11, 2002)
Costs and fees proceeding at Moyer v. Empire Lodge Homeowners' Ass'n, 2003 Colo. LEXIS 873 (Colo., Oct. 20, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court, Water Division 2, Case No. 96CW123. Honorable John E. Anderson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners' association diverted water to fill recreational ponds. Defendant operators of a senior water right placed calls to curtail the diversions. The association sued alleging enlargement of use and invoking the futile call doctrine, and the operators counterclaimed. The District Court for Water Division No. 2 dismissed the complaint and issued an injunction ordering cessation of the diversions. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: For over 12 years, the association diverted water to fill ponds for recreational use by residents of a subdivision. Although it made diversions under periodic approvals by the state engineer, it failed to initiate a water court augmentation plan proceeding. The operators of the senior water right placed frequent calls upon the association's pond-filling activities. On appeal of the injunction curtailing its out-of-priority diversions, the association contended that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 37-80-120, when combined with the state engineer's approval of its out-of-priority diversions, conferred upon it a legally protected water right. The appellate court held that the association lacked standing to invoke the enlargement or futile call doctrines against the operators of the senior water right, as their out-of-priority diversions required a judicially decreed augmentation plan that authorized them. The state engineer's enforcement discretion under § 37-80-120 did not alleviate the duty of the association to obtain a decree for an augmentation plan. The water court did not abuse its discretion in enjoining the association's diversions pending adjudication authorizing them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the water court's judgment.

Patton v. People


Case No. 00SC82


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


35 P.3d 124; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 923



November 13, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals Case No. 98CA0892.

 People v. Patton, 7 P.3d 1057, 1999 Colo. App. LEXIS 316, 1999 Colo. J. C. A. R. 6589 (Ct. App. 1999) 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a petition for writ of certiorari seeking review of the decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals convicting him upon his plea of guilty to manufacturing and possessing methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pleaded guilty to manufacturing and possessing methamphetamine, a schedule II controlled substance, both counts in violation of Colo. Rev. Stat. tit. 6, § 18-18-405(1)(a) (2000). The appellate court granted certiorari to determine whether petitioner could lawfully be convicted and punished on both counts pursuant to his guilty plea in light of the double jeopardy provisions of the Colorado and United States Constitutions. The appellate court held that petitioner was not entitled to dismissal of either conviction. The state supreme court disagreed. Petitioner sufficiently demonstrated a double jeopardy violation. Here the double jeopardy violation appeared on the face of the information and guilty plea record, because the legislature had not provided for separate punishment under these circumstances. The double jeopardy clauses required the state supreme court to vacate the lesser-included offense.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

People v. Coleby


Case No. 00SC416


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


34 P.3d 422; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 924



November 13, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the El Paso County District Court. Case No. 99CV1893. Honorable Douglas E. Anderson, Judge.

 People v. Coleby, 2000 Colo. LEXIS 1186 (2000) 



DISPOSITION: Approve..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People filed for certiorari review of the order of the El Paso County District Court, Colorado, dismissing charges against respondent ex-husband for violating a restraining order that prohibited him from contacting his ex-wife or directing another person to contact her.


OVERVIEW: Ex-husband was charged with violating a restraining order that prohibited him from contacting his ex-wife. He asserted that he did not knowingly or intentionally violate the restraining order. The jury acquitted him after it was instructed, over the prosecution's objection, that the crime of violating a restraining order required that the defendant had committed the crime both knowingly and intentionally. On appeal, the district court ruled that the county court erred in finding that Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-6-803.5 (1997) required the culpable mental states of both "intentionally" and "knowingly." The state supreme court granted certiorari to determine the mental state required under § 18-6-803.5. Agreeing with the trial court's conclusion, the state supreme court concluded that § 18-6-803.5 required a knowing violation, which was satisfied either implicitly by personal service of the restraining order or explicitly by actual knowledge of the contents of the order.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Acad. of Charter Schs v. Adams County Sch. Dist. No. 12


Case No. 99SC548


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


32 P.3d 456; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 774; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 4583



September 17, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 97CA2177.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The plaintiff charter school filed suit against the defendant school district in district court alleging numerous claims that the district had not fulfilled its contractual obligations. The district filed a motion to dismiss, or alternatively a motion for summary judgment. The district court granted the district's motion. The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed, holding that the school did not have standing. The school appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court addressed several issues, inter alia: (1) whether the school could sue the school district to enforce its service agreement contract; and (2) whether the school could sue the school district to enforce governing policy provisions in its charter contract. The supreme court held that: (1) Colo. Rev. Stat. § 22-30.5-104(7)(b) expressly authorized the school to sue the district for enforcement of its services contract created pursuant to § 22-30.5-104(7)(b), therefore, because the services at issue were clearly controlled by § 22-30.5-104(7)(b), the school had standing to sue for enforcement of those contractual claims and the trial court erred in dismissing the suit; and (2) the school, if dissatisfied with the result of its dispute resolution process, could seek board review of that resolution, however, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 22-30.5-108(3)(d) provided that, once the board had reached its decision, that decision was final and unappealable; the legislature made it clear that charter schools did not have standing to sue their school districts for disputes arising from implementation of the governing policy provisions in their charter contracts.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed insofar as it denied the school standing to pursue its service contract claims and remanded with directions to allow the parties to litigate the service claims. The judgment was affirmed as to the dismissal of the school's claims as related to the governing policy provisions. The judgment was affirmed in all other respects.

People v. Dunlap


Case No. 00SA191


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


36 P.3d 778; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 757; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 4498



September 10, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Dunlap, 2001 Colo. LEXIS 882 (Colo., Oct. 22, 2001)
Writ of certiorari denied Dunlap v. Colorado, 534 U.S. 1095, 122 S. Ct. 844, 151 L. Ed. 2d 722, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 67 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the District Court. Arapahoe County, Case No. 93CR2071. Honorable John Leopold, Judge.
People v. Dunlap, 975 P.2d 723, 1999 Colo. LEXIS 203 (Colo., 1999)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Arapahoe County District Court (Colorado), denying his motion for reduction of both his death sentence and other sentences pursuant to Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(b).


OVERVIEW: The state supreme court concluded that the weight of the evidence supported the death sentence, and such a finding circumscribed the limits of the trial court's authority to overturn that sentence or to reduce it, in the absence of a showing that the sentence was clearly erroneous as contrary to the weight of the evidence. Defendant claimed it was error to deny him a hearing to present mitigating evidence in the penalty phase of his trial. Furthermore, the trial court adequately demonstrated that it considered the mitigating factors in defendant's motion, and explained its reasoning in support of its denial.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed.

 

193 of 734 DOCUMENTS


Pub. Serv. Co. v. Van Wyk


Case No. 99SC783


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


27 P.3d 377; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 557; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 3394



July 2, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 98CA2102.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent property owners brought claims against petitioner public utility for inverse condemnation, trespass, and nuisance. The Colorado trial court concluded that the state public utilities commission's (PUC) decision allowing an upgrade of electrical lines precluded the property owners from pursuing their claims, and also granted the utility's Colo. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(5) motion. The Court of Appeals reversed, and the utility appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that previous quasi-judicial PUC determinations did not preclude the inverse condemnation, trespass, and nuisance claims by the property owners as a result of the effects of the upgrade, rejecting the utility's argument that this would allow the owners to indirectly seek reversal of the PUC's approval of the upgrade itself. The court of appeals further concluded that the owners adequately alleged each of the torts claimed in their complaint. The supreme court affirmed in part, holding that PUC decisions were not adjudications of property rights, and thus did not preclude private rights of action regarding property rights. The court further held that while a plaintiff could not assert a claim for intentional nuisance unless a defendant's conduct was both intentional and unreasonable, here the owners successfully stated such a claim. The court reversed in part, holding that the owners could not assert a claim for inverse condemnation where the alleged taking concerned intangible impacts, namely noise and electromagnetic fields, and where the alleged damages were a depreciation in property value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that part of the court of appeals's decision that approved the claims seeking to adjudicate property rights, and affirmed the ruling sustaining the owners' nuisance claim. The court reversed in part, denying the claims for inverse condemnation and trespass, and remanded to allow litigation of the nuisance claim.

 

194 of 734 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cooper


Consolidated Cases, Case No. 00SC474, Case No. 00SC499, Case No. 00SC587, Case No. 00SC588


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


27 P.3d 348; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 504



June 25, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 98CA1614. Court of Appeals No. 99CA1522. Court of Appeals No. 99CA712. Court of Appeals No. 99CA764.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of Colorado granted certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals to review four decisions that reversed the trial court's denial of the defendants' Colo. R. Crim. P. 35(c) motions and determined that parole following sexual offenses committed between July 1, 1996 and November 1, 1998 was discretionary under the provisions in Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 17-2-201(5)(a.5), 18-1-105(1)(a)(V)(C) (2000).


OVERVIEW: The supreme court addressed the question of the extension of its opinion in Martin, issued the same day, which held that under Colo. Rev. Stat. § 17-2-201(5)(a) (2000), a person convicted of a sexual offense committed before July 1, 1996 was subject to discretionary parole no longer than the remainder of the maximum sentence or five years, whichever was shorter. In the instant case, the four defendants committed their sexual offenses after July 1, 1996 but before November 1, 1998, thereby bringing into question the effect of § 17-2-201(5)(a.5). The court concluded that the exact same language in § 17-2-201(5)(a), (5)(a.5) had the identical meaning, and that as in Martin, they prevailed over the general sentencing provisions in Colo. Rev. Stat. §§ 17-22.5-403(7)(a), 18-1-105(1)(a)(V) (2000). Therefore, a person convicted of a sexual offense committed after July 1, 1996 but before November 1, 1998, was subject to a period of discretionary rather than mandatory parole, no longer than the remainder of the maximum sentence of incarceration imposed by the court.


OUTCOME: The decisions of the court of appeals were affirmed in all four cases.

State Dep't of Labor & Empl. v. Esser


Case No. 00SC292


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


30 P.3d 189; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 461; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 2630



June 4, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified June 25, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 99CA106.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted a writ of certiorari to consider the decision of the Colorado Court of Appeals, reversing the administrative determination that a workers' compensation claim was subject to denial.


OVERVIEW: In a workers' compensation action, the Administrative Law Judge and Industrial Claim Appeals Office ruled that, because the claimant did not present oral testimony, only reports and letters of a licensed physician or psychologist, her request for workers' compensation benefits was subject to denial. The court of appeals reversed. The state supreme court granted certiorari to determine the meaning of the requirement set forth in Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-41-301(2)(a) (2000) whereby any request for workers' compensation arising from a claim of mental impairment had to be proven by evidence supported by the testimony of a licensed physician or psychologist. On review, the state supreme court affirmed. The court determined that § 8-41-301(2)(a) encompassed any species of proof legally presented by the parties at trial through the medium of witnesses, records, documents, concrete objects, and the like. This claimant submitted reports, handwritten notations, and other documents authored by the licensed professionals. The agency wrongly denied her benefits.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Davidson v. Comm. for Gail Schoettler, Inc.


Case No. 00SA154


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


24 P.3d 621; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 456; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 2626



June 4, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the District Court, City & County of Denver, No. 98CV8139.



DISPOSITION: APPEAL DISMISSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Secretary of State challenged the judgment of the District Court, City & County of Denver (Colorado) holding that portions of the prior version of the Fair Campaign Practices Act (FCPA), specifically Colo. Rev. Stat. § 1-45-104(2) and (7) (1999) (repealed 2000), unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Secretary argued that the case was not moot because the Attorney General may bring an action pursuant to the FCPA as it existed at the time of the alleged violations, and because the case involved matters of great public importance since the constitutionality of the old contribution limits was still at issue in other litigation. The supreme court determined that the instant case was moot because: (1) the court declined to afford the Secretary an exception to the mootness doctrine on grounds that she may decide to prosecute these parties or others based upon the previous version of the FCPA; (2) the Secretary's concerns regarding other pending litigation in which the constitutionality of the prior statutes may be raised was collateral to the present case; and (3) the question of attorney fees in a related case could not alone resurrect the case from operation of the mootness doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal, and vacated the judgment of the district court.

198 of 734 DOCUMENTS


CF&I Steel, L.P. v. Usw, Locals 2102 & 3287


Case No. 99SC587


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


23 P.3d 1197; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 440; 113 A.L.R.5th 631; 167 L.R.R.M. 2370; 143 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P59,295; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 2538



May 29, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Cf&I Steel, L.P. v. USWA, 2003 Colo. App. LEXIS 908 (Colo. Ct. App., June 5, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 97CA2045.
CF&I Steel, L.P. v. USW, 990 P.2d 1124, 1999 Colo. App. LEXIS 169 (Colo. Ct. App., 1999)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the trial court enjoined respondent union from residential picketing, based on Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-3-108(2)(a), the Colorado Court of Appeals reversed the injunction, finding that § 8-3-108(2)(a) violated U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and remanded to the trial court. A petition for writ of certiorari was filed, and the writ was granted.


OVERVIEW: Certiorari granted to consider the constitutionality of Colo. Rev. Stat. § 8-3-108(2)(a) of the Colorado Labor Peace Act, which made all residential picketing arising from labor disputes an unfair labor practice. The provision was facially unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Section 8-3-108 made an impermissible distinction between lawful and unlawful conduct based on the subject of the picket. The statute did not establish a minor place restriction on an extremely broad category of communications, it impermissibly curtailed speech on a narrowly drawn category of expression. The content-based restriction affecting property in a traditional public forum was not narrowly drawn to serve a compelling state interest. Any valid interest the state may have had in preserving residential privacy and tranquility was not furthered by selective prohibition of labor picketing. The trial court's order enjoining peaceful picketing outside the residence of a non-striking employee was invalid to the extent that it relied on § 8-3-108. On remand the trial court must have clarified whether the union-related misconduct was enjoinable under the state police power.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded to determine whether respondent authorized or ratified the wrongful conduct of its members and to clarify whether the union-related misconduct was enjoinable under the state police power, and if so, to what extent the injunction was based on that police power.

City & County of Denver v. Bd. of Assessment Appeals


Case No.99SC926


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


30 P.3d 177; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 356; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 2125



April 30, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified En Banc on Denial of Rehearing of May 21, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 99CA1948.



DISPOSITION: ACTION REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Certiorari was granted to the Colorado Court of Appeals in a case wherein petitioner city challenged the validity of respondent Board of Assessment Appeals' decision holding that the Fire and Police Pension Association was exempt from ad valorem taxation.


OVERVIEW: The issue in the case was whether petitioner could tax the ownership interests of the Fire and Police Pension Association (FPPA) in property located within petitioner's city limits. The FPPA claimed its ownership interest was exempt from ad valorem taxation under Colo. Const. art. X, § 4 and Colo. Rev. Stat. § 39-3-105 (2000). The FPPA was a political subdivision of the state, established for a public purpose and benefiting the state as a whole, as well as local government. However, the general assembly had not made provision for FPPA to be exempt from ad valorem taxation under the property of the state category of exemption.


OUTCOME: Respondent's decision was reversed. The general assembly had not provided for FPPA's ownership interest in real or personal property to be exempt from ad valorem taxation. FPPA's petitions for refund or abatement of the taxes it paid to petitioner were to be dismissed on remand.

 

200 of 734 DOCUMENTS


A.P.E. v. People of Colorado


Case No. 99SC392


SUPREME COURT OF COLORADO


20 P.3d 1179; 2001 Colo. LEXIS 264; 2001 Colo. J. C.A.R. 1514



March 26, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari to the Colorado Court of Appeals. Court of Appeals No. 98CA588.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a juvenile, was convicted of possession of an illegal weapon, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-12-102 (2000), and unlawful carrying a concealed weapon, pursuant to Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-12-105 (2000). The Colorado Court of Appeals affirmed petitioner's convictions. The instant court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The knife petitioner was arrested for carrying was a T-handled push knife. The handle had finger grooves similar to metallic knuckles and was attached to a knife-blade that was less than three and one-half inches in length. Petitioner argued that the knife he carried did not fit the definition of "knife" for purposes of the concealed weapons statute, Colo. Rev. Stat. § 18-12-105 (2000). There was no evidence, other than the outward appearance of the knife, that petitioner intended to use it as a weapon. In overturning petitioner's conviction on the concealed weapons charge, the instant court concluded that to sustain a conviction under the concealed weapons statute in a case involving a knife with a blade less than three and one-half inches in length, the prosecution had to prove that petitioner used or intended to use the short-bladed knife as a weapon. Because neither the trial court nor the appellate court made a determination of petitioner's intent based on all the relevant circumstances, petitioner was entitled to a new trial on the concealed weapon charge.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded with directions to return it to the trial court for proceedings consistent with the instant court's opinion.
